# Ash Under Gold Leaf

# Chapter 1

Marisol stood across from the museum steps with her tote strap digging into her shoulder and the October wind pushing damp air up Flatbush Avenue like a warning. The building looked expensive in the way old certainty looked expensive, all stone certainty and carved faces that had never wondered whether twenty dollars would clear before midnight. A school group spilled out of a bus, bright jackets, lunch coolers, one kid already crying because he had left a dinosaur water bottle at home. She let them pass, then checked her phone again even though she had checked it on the train and once on the sidewalk outside the bagel place.

The sobriety app was plain black text on a white screen, stripped of medals and fake confetti. It read: 30 months.

She held her thumb on the screen until it dimmed, trying to pin the number there by force. Thirty months. Not a holy number, but long enough that people had started using words like stable around her, long enough that her mother had stopped smelling her breath before handing her coffee, long enough that she had begun to believe the body's old hunger had finally lost interest in her. She knew better than that. Hunger did not lose interest. Hunger took notes.

She crossed at the light and climbed the steps. Her left wrist prickled where the old burn scar tightened in the cold. Inside, the museum air turned dry and filtered, solvent clean under the perfume of polished stone. Security ran her bag through the scanner, eyed the spiral notebooks and recorder she had packed, and waved her toward a temporary desk where a volunteer handed over a plastic badge sleeve and a one-page map with too many arrows. The volunteer had glitter nail polish flaking off in crescent moons and whispered the cafeteria warning like contraband: the coffee was drinkable only before noon.

Chief Curator Nora Feldman met her outside Administration in a charcoal suit and museum-issued tote stuffed with folders and what looked like a pair of black flats. Nora was taller than Marisol had expected, pale enough that the skin under her eyes showed lavender from fatigue, and she blinked in the quick, overused way of someone who had spent too many years under track lighting reading donor emails. She shook Marisol's hand once, firm and brief.

"Thanks for being early," Nora said. "Intake always takes longer than anyone admits. Come on."

Her office sat behind two keycard doors and a wall of framed exhibition posters that made every decade look cleaner than it had been. Nora motioned Marisol into a chair, opened a file, and slid a contract addendum across the desk.

"Before we talk aesthetics," Nora said, "we need to clarify institutional posture. The residency is attached to the Rivas retrospective. Donors are underwriting both. That means deliverables have a reputational horizon, not just an artistic one."

Marisol read the first paragraph, then the next. She had seen versions of this language in grant packets and adjunct contracts, but this version had sharper teeth. If commissioned material caused donor withdrawal, legal exposure, or public claims of defamation, the museum could suspend publication support, revoke access, and claw back stipend funds. There it was in polished legal English: if you embarrassed the money, the money got to erase you.

"This looks less like a poetry residency and more like a nondisclosure agreement in costume," she said.

Nora did not flinch. "It is both. We are paying you four thousand dollars to produce public-facing text in connection with a living artist whose estate and collectors are extremely litigious. I need you to understand where the lines are before you start stepping near them."

"The project brief said I could write against sanctification."

"Against simplistic sanctification," Nora said. "Not against the institution's ability to function next season."

She said institution the way some people said weather, too large and too indifferent to take personally. Then she leaned back, kicked one heel off under the desk, and flexed her toes before slipping on one of the flats from her tote. Human, suddenly, with a blister she had not budgeted for.

"Let me put this in plain English," Nora said. "We need language that gives donors a legacy story they can stand beside at dinner without choking on it. Complexity is fine. Public bloodletting is not."

Marisol felt the old flare in her chest, that instant heat when somebody asked for complexity with guardrails. She thought about rent arrears waiting in a drawer back in Sunset Park. She thought about the copy shop manager who kept scheduling her for closing shifts because she never complained. She thought about the way people in funding rooms said voice when they meant labor.

"You commissioned me because I don't write brochures," she said.

"Correct," Nora said. "We commissioned you because your work carries edge without collapsing into spectacle. I'm asking you to keep that balance inside a legal framework."

Nora tapped the final page. "Sign here, here, and here. Then we'll activate your badge."

Marisol signed. The pen dragged once where her hand trembled, a tiny shake she hoped Nora would read as cold. Nora stacked the pages, initialed two corners, and passed over a lanyard with MARISOL VEGA printed in black block letters.

On the margin of her own notebook, Marisol wrote donor-safe grief, stared at it, then crossed the phrase out so hard the paper thinned.

"Your primary contacts are me for curatorial matters and Dr. Ada Park for conservation coordination," Nora said. "You're meeting both now. Rivas requested an introductory walk-through before public hours."

"Requested me specifically?"

Nora's mouth made a brief, tired line that might have been amusement. "Rivas requests weather patterns and blood chemistry. We negotiate what we can."

They moved through a corridor that narrowed near the conservation wing, where the air got even drier and smelled faintly of ethanol and old canvas. Ada Park waited by a steel door with a ring of keys and a tablet tucked under one arm. She was lean, close-cropped hair tucked behind one ear, and the scar across her right knuckle pulled white when she tightened her grip on the tablet. Her hands looked overwashed, skin cracked at the cuticles from solvents and gloves.

"Ms. Vega," Ada said, offering a nod instead of a handshake. "I finished humidity checks in Gallery Three. You're clear to enter, but don't touch anything that isn't designated touch-safe. If a label looks loose, tell me. Don't fix it yourself."

"I grew up Catholic," Marisol said. "I can follow shrine rules."

Ada's mouth twitched. "Good. Same posture, less incense."

Nora swiped her card. The lock released with a hard mechanical click that sounded too loud in the quiet hall.

Gallery Three was still half asleep. Lights were up at maintenance level, paintings floating in dim rectangles, ladders pushed aside, one crate still open near the far wall. The room held that charged pre-opening silence museums liked to call calm, when every object waited to be looked at and every employee waited for something to go wrong first. A wheelchair sat near the central bench, but Salvador Rivas stood beside it with one hand on the backrest, tall even bent, shoulders pitched forward in a way that made every breath look negotiated.

He wore a dark suit jacket over a black T-shirt, collar loose at the throat. His skin carried a yellow cast under the gallery lights. A tremor moved through his right hand in small, persistent pulses as he folded and unfolded a linen handkerchief into sharp squares. When he looked up, his eyes were clear, almost bright.

"There she is," he said. "The museum's poet with a deadline."

Nora stepped forward. "Salvador, this is Marisol Vega, our resident writer. Marisol, Mr. Rivas."

"Salvador," he corrected. "If I have to die in public, we can skip honorifics."

His voice had theatrical weight, but there was gravel under it, a scrape that sounded painful when he pushed volume. He held out his left hand. Marisol took it. His skin was cool and dry, painter's callus still thick at the base of his fingers.

"Vega," he said, still holding her hand a second too long. "From where?"

"Flatbush," Marisol said. "Originally Sunset Park."

"No. The name. Vega from where?"

Nora shifted. "Salvador, we can do biographical context after we review process."

He ignored her. His gaze stayed on Marisol like he had found a word he recognized in a language he had pretended to forget.

"Your mother's name?" he said.

The question landed in her body before it landed in thought. "Gloria Vega," she said. "Why?"

The tremor in his hand sharpened once. He let go of her and sat in the wheelchair with careful annoyance, turning the motion into an insult someone else had scheduled.

"Because I knew a Gloria Vega with hair pinned so tight it looked like discipline," he said. "Worked nights. Knew exactly how to stretch two chickens across five people."

Nora cut in, voice polished. "For today's purposes, we're orienting Marisol to approved materials. Interviews will be scheduled through my office with either me or a designated staff member present, and recordings route through Legal review before transcription."

Salvador laughed once, no humor, then coughed into the handkerchief. The cough took too long. Ada took one step forward, then stopped when he lifted two fingers without looking at her. When he caught his breath, he spoke to Nora without taking his eyes off Marisol.

"No," he said. "If you put three people in a room, I perform. If you put Legal in the room, I lie. If you put this woman and me in a room, maybe I tell the truth before my pancreas finishes eating me and my right hand forgets line pressure."

Nora did not look away. "No witness means contested notes. Contested notes turn into defamation exposure, and then donor counsel shuts the whole project down."

"Then build medical guardrails, not a chaperone," he said. "Ada outside the door. Panic button on the wall. Let the room smell like solvent and old canvas. No curator script in my ear."

He turned the chair slightly, now addressing Marisol directly. "What are you writing?"

"A poem cycle tied to your retrospective," she said. "And notes for a larger manuscript about my father's death."

"Your father the martyr," he said softly.

The room thinned. Even the vents seemed to hush.

"You know about that?" Marisol asked.

He picked at the edge of his handkerchief, folding it again. "Brooklyn is small. Grief is loud."

Nora stepped between them by half a pace, not enough to block, enough to remind them she could.

"Salvador," she said, "we're not discussing unvetted personal history in an unscheduled intake."

He looked up at her, almost kind. "Nora, if I wanted your season donor-safe, I'd die quietly and leave you three paragraphs about brushwork, underpainting, and varnish bloom. You invited a poet because you wanted voltage. Voltage burns insulation."

Ada shifted her weight, key ring clicking softly against her thigh. "We need to decide access parameters now," she said. "I've got a treatment window in forty minutes, and this room has to clear for lighting tests."

Practical, clipped, no drama. Marisol was grateful for the blunt timing. It kept the moment from floating off into metaphor.

Nora exhaled through her nose and spoke in that legal cadence again, each noun set down like a paperweight.

"Fine. Concrete terms," she said. "Ninety minutes max because your vitals crash after that. Door closed but unlocked. Ada's in the corridor with a med line. Any notebook handling gets logged as potential estate evidence."

Salvador's mouth curled. "I can do ninety. I can do unlocked. But if someone steps in to steer language, the notebooks stay in storage with the cracked canvases and bad gesso."

He leaned forward in the chair, tremor running through his hand harder now, and for the first time Marisol saw fear under the performance, not fear of death exactly, fear of speech failing before he could spend it.

"I've got notebooks," he said to Marisol. "Forty years, maybe more. I call them the Saint Ledger Notebooks when I'm being dramatic. Dates, names, thumbnail sketches, pigment mixes, where the ground failed, lies I told myself and then crossed out. You don't get them with donor handlers sipping oat milk outside the glass."

Nora's voice cooled another degree. "If you withhold material after documented terms, we'll record noncooperation and proceed without your private archive."

"Do what you need," he said. "I don't have time left for your script."

A pulse beat in Marisol's throat hard enough to feel at her gums. This was the pivot she had not expected before noon on day one. She could hear two futures opening at once. In one, she stayed procedural, kept Nora in the room, delivered tasteful language about light and mortality and civic memory, and maybe left with enough stipend money to pay part of December. In the other, she stepped into a room alone with a man famous for staging truth like a set piece and hoped she could still tell document from trap when he started talking about her family.

She asked the only question that mattered. "What are you asking for exactly?"

"Three closed-door sessions to start," Salvador said. "Ninety minutes each, timed to when pain meds are low enough that I can think and high enough that I can sit up without vomiting. Before the tremor eats my hand-memory and every line turns to mud. Keep the curator out of the room, and keep donor observers out too. You can bring one notebook. No recorder until I say yes."

"That isn't acceptable," Nora said.

"Then you can publish your wall labels without me."

Nora turned to Marisol, and now the institutional voice dropped, just for a second, into human fatigue.

"If you agree to this," she said quietly, "you're taking on risk personally and professionally. There won't be a witness if he later disputes your notes. There won't be institutional protection if this becomes a claims fight. Defamation exposure, indemnity clawback - no, direct personal exposure if this goes bad. It lands on you. I need you to hear that clearly."

Marisol heard it. She also heard the scrape of late rent against her name, heard the manuscript in her backpack like a body she had to carry uphill, heard her father's saint story rattling in its frame.

"I hear you," she said.

Salvador watched her with unnerving stillness. His right hand shook against the armrest, but his face was calm, waiting.

The word yes pressed against her teeth before caution could assemble. She tasted metal, felt the burn scar on her wrist pull tight in the cold air, and let the risk land where it landed.

"I'll do it," she said. "Closed door. Me alone."

Nora's eyes closed briefly, one controlled blink held too long. "I need written acknowledgment that this deviates from standard protocol," she said. "And a scheduling structure through my office, no side texting, no private transport."

"Fine," Marisol said.

"You'll submit contemporaneous notes after each session."

"Fine."

"If at any point he is medically unstable, staff can enter regardless of interview status."

Salvador made a dismissive sound. "If I pass out, by all means, preserve me."

Ada stepped in before Nora could answer. "I'll set the room with a panic button and med line in reach," she said. "And I'm putting a chair by the wall for when either of you gets dizzy. That's not negotiable."

Salvador gave her a tired, almost affectionate look. "You're the only adult in this building, Ada."

"I know," she said.

Nora straightened the folder in her hands, trying to square the moment by squaring paper. "We'll draft terms by end of day," she said. "Marisol, stay after and we'll process the access addendum."

The meeting broke without ceremony. Ada moved toward the far wall to check a label seam, already back inside practical tasks. Nora took a call the second her phone lit up, voice instantly donor-smooth: "Yes, of course, we are fully aligned on messaging." Salvador sat in his chair, eyes on the triptych across from him, fingers worrying the handkerchief into smaller and smaller squares.

As Marisol turned to leave, he spoke without looking at her.

"Bring a pen that doesn't skip," he said. "The cheap ones always fail when the sentence gets ugly."

Outside the gallery, the corridor felt colder than before. By the time she reached the public atrium, her jaw hurt from clenching. She went down the service stairs instead of taking the elevator, hand sliding along chipped paint, and came out near the loading dock where staff smoked under a NO SMOKING sign that everyone ignored. Rain had started, light and mean, enough to slick the curb and darken the concrete.

The craving arrived so fast it felt like being shoved from behind. Not a thought first. Mouth first. Saliva flooding, pulse snapping awake, the body remembering vodka's clean burn before her conscience had finished forming a sentence. She could see the liquor store two blocks down, bright refrigeration light, discount sign in the window, a shelf of half-pints behind plexiglass. Her legs had already angled that direction.

She stopped at the corner and dug for her phone with hands that did not feel attached to her. Her contact list had three people pinned at the top. She stared at Luis's name long enough for the screen to threaten sleep, then hit call before she could decide she was being dramatic.

He picked up on the third ring. Background noise: echoing announcements, a bus kneeling, somebody laughing too loud, and the wet snap of the orange peels he was always tearing into strips at dispatch to keep his hands busy.

"You okay?" he said.

No hello. Just a gate.

"No," she said. "I'm not drinking. Yet. But my body thinks that's the plan."

"Where are you?"

"Museum loading dock."

"Can you see a store?"

"Yeah."

"Turn your back to it."

She did, facing a chain-link fence and stacked milk crates wet with rain.

"Now what happened," Luis said. "Facts, not theater."

His voice was steady in that specific way that annoyed her when she wanted sympathy and saved her when sympathy would have killed her. She told him about intake, the contract language, donor clauses, Salvador saying her mother's name like a match strike, closed-door demand, Nora warning her she could get buried if it went bad.

Luis listened without interruption except for one "mm-hm" when a truck backed up and beeped through her sentence. When she finished, he asked, "Did you say yes?"

"Yeah."

"Why?"

She leaned against the fence, cold metal through her coat. "Because if he's telling any truth at all, it's in private. Because I need the material. Because I can't keep writing around my father like he's a stained-glass window. Pick one."

"Those are reasons," Luis said. "I'm asking which one was driving the bus."

A laugh broke out of her, sharp and mean. "You're doing transit metaphors now?"

"I'm at work. Adaptability. Answer me."

She closed her eyes. Behind her lids she saw Salvador's hand shaking against the armrest, the way he had said martyr like he knew the architecture of that word from the inside.

"The father thing," she said. "That was driving."

"Okay. That's honest." Pause. Paper shuffling on his end. "You got a notebook on you?"

"Always."

"Write three facts before you write one feeling. Date, time, who said what exact words. Then write what you wanted to hear and didn't. Don't call your mother yet. Don't call anybody you want to perform for."

"You think I'm performing right now?"

"I think you're scared and smart, and that's a dangerous combo when liquor is two blocks away. Write it down so your future self can't lie about today."

Rain thickened, ticking against the fence and her phone screen. She crouched under the shallow overhang by the dock door, pulled her notebook from the tote, and clicked her pen.

"I'm writing," she said.

"Good. Read me the first line when you're done."

She wrote: October 27, 2026. 10:42 a.m. Gallery Three intake with Nora Feldman, Ada Park, Salvador Rivas.

The letters steadied as she continued. Nora said donor clauses allowed suspension and clawback for reputational harm. Salvador recognized Gloria Vega's name before any family prompt. Salvador requested three closed-door sessions, no curator present. Nora warned no witness means no protection in a claims fight. Marisol accepted.

"Read it," Luis said.

She read. He let silence sit for one breath, then two.

"That's usable," he said. "Now feelings, one sentence only."

She looked at the wet page, ink feathering at the edge where rain had touched it.

"I wanted him to be just an artist," she said slowly as she wrote, "and now I think he's a door I can't close."

"There you go," Luis said. "You don't have to walk through today. You just have to stay sober today."

"I know."

"Do you?"

"Yeah," she said, angry because he was right to ask. "I do."

"Then next step. Send the curator your acceptance in writing with terms clear. Keep it factual. No poetry in email. Then get coffee with sugar and protein, because your blood sugar sounds like trash and low blood sugar lies louder than donors."

"You flirting with me, Lou?"

"I'm old enough to be your worst decision. Go eat. Text me a photo of food, not coffee, by noon."

She smiled despite herself. "Bossy."

"Alive," he said, and hung up.

Marisol stayed crouched until her legs went numb, then stood and wiped rain off the notebook cover. The liquor store sign down the block hummed in her peripheral vision, still there, still offering the exact fast mercy she had trained herself not to call mercy. She turned away from it and walked back inside, shoes squeaking on the service corridor linoleum.

At a staff desk outside Administration, she opened her laptop. Her right hand skidded on the trackpad and she missed her password twice before forcing both palms flat against the warm plastic. She had bitten the inside of her cheek somewhere between the dock and the elevator; copper sat on her tongue.

Before she finished the subject line, a security supervisor stopped beside her desk.

"Ms. Vega? Curatorial flagged your badge," he said. "Until Legal countersigns your addendum, you're restricted to Admin and public floors. No Gallery Three access without escort."

He set a paper temporary pass beside her wrist and moved on.

Subject: Interview Protocol Confirmation - Rivas

Nora,

Confirming that I accept the three-session closed-door structure requested by Salvador Rivas, with scheduling routed through your office and contemporaneous notes submitted after each session. I understand this deviates from standard witness protocol and carries dispute risk. I acknowledge temporary badge restrictions pending signed addendum language.

Thanks,
Marisol Vega

She read it once, deleted temporary, sent it.

Her inbox chimed before the sent icon stopped spinning.

From: Nora Feldman
Cc: Legal, Security Operations
Report to Conference B at 11:30 for risk acknowledgment and access terms. Until completion, no unsupervised contact with Mr. Rivas.

Then she opened a new document titled MARTYR MANUSCRIPT - FACT LOG and pasted the notes from her notebook, line by line, before memory could sand them smooth. She added a final entry:

Accepted terms: three private sessions, no in-room witness, dispute exposure acknowledged.

When she leaned back, the museum hummed around her, carts rolling, printers spitting labels, a child somewhere in the lobby asking too loudly whether dead artists still got paid. Through the window she could see rain stippling the plaza and, beyond it, the city moving with complete indifference to whatever she had just agreed to. Her hands still shook, but the shake had changed. Not appetite now. Aftershock.

Thirty months sober, she reminded herself, pressing her thumb against the ridge of her burn scar until the skin hurt.

Thirty months, and now this.
At five thirty she'd be in the museum's education room, legal pad squared, trying to get one concrete, verifiable claim before either of them started performing.


# Chapter 2
At five thirty, the museum gave school groups a bright education room with wipe-clean tables and a sink for paint cups, and the fluorescent lights flattened everybody into the same bad color. Marisol sat alone at the table closest to the door, legal pad squared with the table edge, recorder centered on top of her residency folder like it might behave better under official paper. Through the cinderblock wall she heard a cart wheel squeal every eight seconds as a guard pushed stanchions back into storage. The room smelled less like art than cafeteria bleach and the sour sugar of old apple juice.

She had written three lines at the top of the page before he came in.

Objective: test credibility.

No myth language in the questions.

Get one concrete, verifiable claim.

When the orderly opened the door, Salvador did not look at her first. He stared at the sink like he was trying to remember what water was called, then held up his own right hand and flexed each finger, checking it like a tool he no longer trusted. He was taller than any chair let him be, even folded forward in the transport chair, long knees like bent coat hangers under a museum blanket. The skin under his eyes was the yellow of nicotine stains. He carried his pain in stages: first the jaw set, then the breath, and then, if it got bad enough, a tiny dry click in his throat before he swallowed.

"You picked the student room," he said while the orderly parked him. "I like your optimism. Children paint better lies than adults."

"It was available," Marisol said.

"So was heaven."

The orderly gave Marisol a quick apology with his eyebrows, checked the morphine log clipped to the back of the chair, and left. The door shut with a soft seal. Salvador tugged the blanket off his legs and winced when the wool dragged over his shins.

Marisol pressed record. "For the record, today's date is October twenty-ninth, two thousand twenty-six. Interview two, private session, five thirty-seven p.m. Present are Salvador Rivas and Marisol Vega." She looked up. "Are you lucid enough to continue?"

"You people love that word." He leaned back and shut his eyes. "Lucid. As if I'm a window."

"Are you lucid enough to continue?"

He opened one eye, then the other. "Yes. For now. If I start praising governors and dead critics, shut it off."

She let that pass. "Yesterday you said my father didn't die the way my family says he died. I want specifics, not riddles."

He smiled without warmth. "You're thirty-four and still announcing your age with that jaw. Gloria kept the world from chewing you."

"I didn't ask about my mother."

"Everything here is your mother." He tapped the table with one finger, slow, like counting in a song nobody else heard. "And your father. And the lies people tell children because they can't survive their own choices."

Marisol held his gaze. She had trained herself to sit through silence in workshop rooms where the person with power waited for somebody else to volunteer bleeding. This silence had different teeth. Salvador studied her the way a forger studies paper grain, deciding what would hold and what would tear.

"Say it clean," she said.

"Fine. Your family has been painting halos over burns." He said it flat, no theater, and then he looked down at his hand again. "There. That's your sentence for the catalog."

A radiator clanged once in the wall and then went still. Marisol heard her own breath scrape. She wrote the line down exactly, pressing hard enough to score the page below. "What burns?"

"Foil. Lungs. The skin when somebody nods off with hot foil in his hand. Tomas died in an overdose room, not under a protest banner."

"Then give me what I can verify," she said. "Date, address, witness."

"Not tonight."

"Convenient."

He rubbed his thumb against the pad of his index finger, a dry friction like sanding wood. "Necessary."

"Where?"

"Not on a bridge. Not with cameras."

"Where?"

"You don't get to drag a confession out of a body on schedule." He leaned his head back. "I didn't paint on deadline. I'm not dying on deadline either."

Marisol let five seconds pass, then ten, then fifteen. The guard's cart wheel screamed in the hallway and moved farther away. She wrote in the margin: evasive when pinned to location. Then she hated herself for writing it like a field note from a zoo and crossed out zoo before she had even written the word.

"You invited private sessions," she said. "You asked for me. You brought up Gloria's name before I brought up anything. If this is about leverage, say that. If this is about truth, stop performing."

He laughed once and then coughed hard enough to bend forward, forearm across his ribs. The cough sounded wet and expensive; each burst spent something he could not replace. Marisol half stood, then stopped when he raised his palm.

"Don't hover," he said into the cough. "I hate being watched like an appliance about to break."

He took two breaths through his nose and swallowed. "You want one thing you can touch? Tomas had a split callus in the center of his right palm from moving crates before they gave him gloves. He would pick at it when he lied. Always that hand. In meetings. In church. In my studio while he pretended he didn't care about art." He looked at her, and his voice thinned. "He picked at it the last week."

Marisol wrote fast, then forced herself to stop. "You just gave me settings, not proof. Meetings, church, studio. That's atmosphere."

"Good. You're not stupid."

"Then give me evidence. Date. Address. Witness."

Salvador's head drifted left like a flower stem cut too high. His eyes closed halfway. "Morphine doesn't make me a liar," he said. "It makes it hard to care whether you believe me."

"How much did they give you today?"

"Enough to keep my pancreas from setting itself on fire." He opened his eyes again, irritated now. "Don't turn into a nurse to avoid turning into a daughter."

"I'm not your daughter." The line came out quick and hard. She felt heat climb from her neck into her face. "You're not going to bait me into some fever-dream confession about blood and genius."

He watched that land. He did not smile this time. "Good," he said softly. "Keep that. You will need it."

She shifted tactics. "Tell me what you gain by destabilizing me."

"You think like a contract now. Nora would be proud."

"Answer."

"I gain very little. I'm dying in a borrowed office with bad lights. You think I need your pity or your poems?" He spoke slowly, each phrase dragged through pain but still precise. "I gain the chance to stop one story before I go. The saint story flattened him. It flattened Gloria. It flattened you into a girl praying to a dead man who never existed." He lifted two fingers, shaky but deliberate. "I'm not asking for absolution. I'm saying the frame is wrong."

Marisol could feel the old script in her body anyway, a muscle memory from novenas and photo candles and adults lowering their voices when they said Tomas's name. In family language Tomas was the brave one, the organizer, the man who stood in front when police charged and never came back. She had built half a book around that architecture, fourteen poems and three prose sections, all of it tuned to a man in a rain-soaked jacket raising a hand toward riot shields.

"If the frame is wrong," she said, "you can still be using the crack to crawl out from your own mess."

"Of course I can." He looked almost amused. "Finally, we're speaking as adults."

"So why should I trust anything?"

"You shouldn't trust me." He took another shallow breath, then another. "You should verify me."

She stared. "With what."

"With paper." He blinked slowly. "With logs. With signatures. With the boring machinery that outlives us. I'm not your priest, Marisol. I'm evidence in a failing container."

Her name in his mouth felt like a hand where she did not want one. "Don't say my name like that."

"Like what?"

"Like you earned it."

He made a rough sound that might have been a laugh and might have been pain. "I didn't earn much."

He went quiet long enough that she thought he might be gone for the moment, floating somewhere two inches above the room. She listened to the fluorescent hum, the hiss of the vent, the small involuntary crack of his knuckle when he flexed his right hand. Then he said, without opening his eyes, "Room had green carpet. Burn marks by the radiator from cooked heroin on foil. Tomas stopped breathing before anybody wanted to call it in. You can start there if you need an image to tattoo on your notebook."

Marisol did not move. "Then give me the address and the 911 timestamp."

"You don't get all of it today." He opened his eyes and looked directly at her. "You get enough to stop writing propaganda."

"You think I write propaganda."

"I think you write beautiful certainty." His voice had gone thin, almost gentle, and that made her want to throw something. "Certainty is the favorite paint of cowards."

"That's rich coming from you."

"Yes." He nodded once. "It's."

The nurse who covered his evening meds knocked once and opened the door before either of them answered. Young, Dominican accent, glitter pen clipped to her scrub collar. She apologized for interrupting and checked his wristband while Salvador rolled his eyes and called her General. She ignored him and asked if he had eaten. He told her the museum food tasted like drywall and she told him that was not a food group. When she peeled the foil off the medication cup, her nails flashed bright orange under the fluorescent lights.

Marisol stepped back to give space and watched the room change shape around ordinary care. Salvador took his pills dry and coughed, then accepted water with obvious irritation. The nurse wiped a drip from his chin with a square of gauze and told him he needed to stop trying to impress interviewers with dehydration.

"This one doesn't impress easy," he said, jerking his chin toward Marisol.

"Good," the nurse said. "You don't need more fans."

When she left, the door seal whispered shut again and the air felt meaner for the contrast.

Marisol clicked her pen. "One more pass. If I try to verify, where do I start."

"Ask where Tomas worked nights that winter." Salvador's eyes were already drifting. "Ask who signed him in. Ask who signed after."

"At the museum?"

He did not answer.

"At the museum?"

He lifted one shoulder. "Maybe don't walk into your mother's kitchen with a tape recorder and righteousness. You're not built for that kind of fire yet."

"You don't get to tell me what I'm built for."

"Everyone tells you what you're built for." He shut his eyes. "I'm only less polite about it."

She waited, and nothing happened.

"Salvador."

Nothing happened again.

"Mr. Rivas, interview is ending at six twenty-three p.m." She reached across the table and turned off the recorder. Even the click sounded loud.

He spoke without opening his eyes. "Bring me something true next time. Not your posture."

The hallway outside felt too wide after the room, polished stone and donor names and a security guard rubbing hand sanitizer until it squeaked. Marisol signed the exit log with a pen chained to the desk and wrote her full name too hard. The guard asked if she needed a car service and she said no before he finished the sentence.

Outside, Eastern Parkway held a damp October dark that turned every headlight into smeared mercury. Bus exhaust folded into the cold. Her phone said 6:41. She had not eaten since noon. The recorder in her tote felt heavier than any object that size had a right to feel.

She started walking east without deciding where she was going.

The first two blocks felt like logistics. She zipped her coat. She pulled the tote strap higher and avoided a puddle with an oil rainbow. She told herself not to step on slick leaves near the curb. By the third block her body had switched tracks. Her mouth went wet. Her jaw clenched and unclenched on its own. A memory arrived whole and unwelcome: the clean plastic crack of a vodka seal giving way. Her shoulders tightened with old debtor panic, the feeling that someone behind her had called her name and she already owed more than she could pay.

She passed a bodega where a man in a Mets cap stacked avocados into a pyramid that would collapse in under an hour. She passed the check-cashing place with the blue sign that buzzed louder than traffic. She passed a daycare with finger-paint turkeys taped to the glass. On the corner of Albany and Lincoln she stopped because across the street a liquor store sign blinked V & L in red neon, half the bulbs dead so it read V _ L, like a missing tooth.

She knew that store too well for someone who had not walked in for thirty months. Cheap quart bottles sat behind plexiglass. The floor near the beer cooler always ran warm. The clerk with the Knicks lanyard never asked for ID after the second week and always slid the bottle over like he was handing over medicine.

Her feet angled toward the crosswalk before her brain filed the motion. She cursed out loud and an older woman under a transparent umbrella glanced at her, then away.

"No," Marisol said, to nobody anyone else could see.

The walk signal changed. People moved around her shoulder first, then hips, city choreography with no sentiment. She could step forward and be at the store in twenty seconds. She pictured it with cruel clarity: palm on the metal pull handle, bell dinging, cold bottle neck in her hand before the cap came off with that bright stupid pop. Her body answered the picture like a dog hearing a can opener.

She pulled her phone out and almost dropped it because her fingers were slick. She opened Luis's contact and stared at his name while the countdown on the crosswalk ticked from eleven to zero.

She did not want a pep talk or a speech. She only wanted accountability before the door.

She hit call.

He picked up on the second ring. The dispatch radio on his end crackled with route numbers, and she heard the small click of the mint tin he kept refilling with fennel seeds on late shifts. "You good?"

"No."

"Where are you?"

"Lincoln and Albany. Across from V and L."

He breathed once through his nose, a sound she had learned meant he was moving into structure. "How close are you to the door?"

"Half a crosswalk."

"Turn your back to it."

She turned. Now she faced a laundromat window full of rotating silver drums, pink socks slapping glass with each spin.

"Done," she said.

"Tell me three things you see."

"Laundromat. Kid licking the window. Guy smoking under a no-smoking sign."

"Good. Three things you hear."

"Bus brakes. Somebody yelling about lotto numbers. My own teeth."

"Where's the nearest meeting right now?"

She knew because she kept the schedule screenshot starred and pinned, because sobriety was routine and ritual and logistics when it worked. "St. Agnes basement, seven o'clock step meeting. Ten minutes if I walk fast."

"Walk there. Stay on with me till you see the church doors."

She started moving north, fast enough that the tote banged her hip. "I talked to him," she said.

"Salvador?"

"Yeah. He said my father wasn't a martyr. He said we painted halos over burns."

Luis was quiet for two steps, then four. "What exactly did he claim and what exactly did he verify?"

"He didn't verify anything. He gave details and pulled back and then gave more." Her breath came short from the pace, not fear yet but heading there. "He said no bridge, no protest. He said there was green carpet and a radiator with burn marks from foil. He kept drifting. He kept baiting."

"Did he give you a date?"

"No."

"Address."

"No."

"Witness name besides himself?"

"No."

"Then it's an allegation, not a case."

"It still blew a hole in everything." She took the curb too fast and nearly rolled an ankle. "Jesus, sorry."

"You don't owe me polite language while you're trying not to drink. Keep walking." His voice stayed level, no sympathy sugar. "Listen to me. You're not confronting family off one dying man's theater. You hear me."

"I hear you."

"Say it like you mean it."

She hated that he was right. She hated him for being right in the exact tone that kept her alive. "I am not confronting anyone without documents."

"Again, and contract it."

"I'm not confronting anyone without documents."

"Good. Step one: get through that church door. Step two: sign in and sit where people can see you. Step three: after the meeting, write a clean list with claims, missing data, and what you can verify. Then text it to me before you call anybody in your family."

She almost laughed despite herself. "Only you could make grief sound like dispatch."

"Because dispatch keeps people from dying in dumb ways. What's your next right action in ten minutes?"

"Get to St. Agnes. Sign in. Sit where someone can see me."

"After the meeting?"

"Text you. Eat something with protein. Write down exactly what he said while it's fresh. No interpretation words."

"Good. Add a column for what you can verify." He clicked his tongue once. "And Mari, one more thing."

"What?"

"If this story's real, it will still be real tomorrow morning. Tonight you stay sober and build a record."

She crossed Washington, headlights throwing rain sheen across her shoes. "It doesn't feel like yesterday."

"Let it feel bad. Still no family calls tonight. Notes first, sleep second, confrontation later."

St. Agnes came into view as a block of brown stone and tired stained glass. The church basement entrance sat off to the side under a buzzing floodlight and a handwritten sign taped crooked to the rail: AA DOWNSTAIRS, COFFEE HOT, PLEASE CLOSE DOOR.

"I see the doors," she said.

"Stay on till you're inside."

She took the steps down. Warm air hit her face with burnt coffee and damp wool and old radiator heat. Folding chairs scraped concrete somewhere down the hall. Somebody laughed too loud at something not very funny, the way people laughed before meetings when they were trying to prove they were not one bad hour from disaster.

Luis said, "You got your chip on you?"

"Thirty-month chip."

"Touch it."

She dug in her coat pocket and found the coin by feel, ridged edge and smooth center worn from habit. Her thumb sat on the stamped triangle. "Got it."

"You're choosing this minute. Then the next one." He exhaled. "Go check in. Call me after."

"Wait." She closed her eyes for a second. "Thank you."

"Don't thank me. Do the work."

The line clicked dead.

In the basement hallway a woman at the folding table looked up from a spiral notebook and smiled like she recognized Marisol even if she did not. She wore a denim jacket patched at one elbow with red thread in a loose star shape and kept tapping a green pen against her ring finger while she spoke.

"First name and last initial," the woman said. "Sign-in's for the room count only."

"Marisol V." She wrote it down and noticed her handwriting had jumped tracks, slanted hard right like it was trying to outrun itself.

"Welcome back," the woman said, and then lowered her voice. "Coffee's bad tonight, but the cookies are weirdly decent."

It was such a specific, unnecessary kindness that Marisol felt tears sting before she could stop them. She nodded and took a paper cup anyway.

The meeting room held thirty metal chairs in a rough oval, one leg on half of them shorter than the others so they rocked if you shifted your weight wrong. Fluorescent lights buzzed above a ceiling stain shaped like Florida. A guy in paint-splattered work pants arranged sugar packets by color on the literature table for no reason except maybe control. Another woman with silver braids peeled an orange in one long ribbon and laid the peel along the edge of her notebook like a border.

Marisol took a chair near the back but not in the corner. Luis had taught her that hiding was just drinking with better posture.

When introductions came around she gave her name and said, "I'm sober today." The words scraped on the way out. It was true and not enough and everything.

People shared in turns. A man talked about losing his MetroCard and bursting into tears on the Q platform because he thought the tears were about the card when they were actually about his brother not answering texts for six months. A home-health aide in pink sneakers said she had screamed at a microwave at four in the morning because oatmeal did not count as dinner and she was tired of pretending she was fine. A young guy with face tattoos said he had walked three blocks behind his old dealer yesterday and did not call out, and he wanted credit for that and also did not trust himself with the credit.

Nobody performed salvation. Nobody asked for proof of worthiness. They reported the weather inside their own bodies and passed the turn.

When it got to Marisol again she kept her cup steady with both hands and said, "I had an interview tonight for work. Somebody said something that cracked a family story I built my life around. I walked toward a liquor store and called my sponsor before I crossed. I'm here because I want to stay where my feet are." The cup trembled hard enough to tap her teeth, but she did not look away. "I also want to set everything on fire, but I can postpone that till I've got facts."

A few people laughed, short and knowing. The woman with silver braids said, "Postponement is an underrated spiritual tool."

After the Serenity Prayer, chairs scraped and coats came on. Marisol stood in line for stale cookies because the sugar steadied her hands faster than pride did. She stepped outside to the basement landing and pulled her phone out.

Before she called Luis she opened the notes app and typed while the details were still hot enough to hurt.

Interview 2, 10/29/26, 5:37-6:23 p.m.

Claims made by S.R.:

- He said, "Family has been painting halos over burns."
- He said Tomas died in an overdose room, not at a protest or on a bridge.
- He mentioned a split callus in the center of Tomas's right palm from crate work.
- He mentioned a room with green carpet and burn marks by a radiator from cooked heroin on foil.
- He said I should verify with paper, logs, and signatures.
- He said I should ask where Tomas worked nights that winter and who signed him in and out.

Data missing:

- He gave no date.
- He gave no address.
- He gave no corroborating witness.
- He named no documentary source.

She added one last line because she knew better than to pretend she was above body facts.

Craving note: It peaked on Albany and Lincoln facing V & L; I had a saliva surge, jaw tension, and tunnel focus on the bottle-cap sound.

Then she called Luis.

"You stayed," he said by way of hello.

"I stayed."

"Good. Read me your claims list and your missing-data list."

She read each line exactly. He interrupted twice to make her slow down, once to ask if Salvador's quote about halos had been verbatim, once to ask what time she left the museum because sequence mattered when memory started rewriting itself.

When she finished, he said, "That's usable. Keep this version. Email it to yourself and to me before you get on the train."

"I don't think I can sleep."

"You probably won't sleep much. Still, get home, eat, set two alarms, and lie down sober. If your head won't shut up, count backward in Spanish and English." His voice softened half a notch. "You did the right thing before the first drink. That matters more than your feelings about it."

She leaned against the damp brick of the church wall. Rain had started in a fine mist, enough to bead on her eyelashes. "I'm going to investigate," she said. "Not accuse. Not yet."

A new email banner slid across her screen.

NORA FELDMAN - LEGAL HOLD: Upload all Rivas interview audio to curatorial server by 9:00 a.m. Route all Tomas-related staff questions through my office pending counsel review.

"Hold on," Marisol said. "Nora just put legal hold on this. Nine a.m. upload and no direct staff questions."

Luis gave a low whistle. "There's your new weather."

"If I hand them the only file, they can bury this in process."

"Then duplicate it tonight," he said. "Cloud backup, local copy, and a transcript header with start and stop times. Chain of custody starts now, Mari."

She stared at the wet sidewalk, anger waking where the craving had burned out. "So now I'm racing legal and grief."

"Welcome to your life."

"No family confrontation. Not without records."

"Good. Saturday morning meeting, eight thirty. You coming?" he asked.

"Yeah."

"Bring coffee if you can afford it. If not, bring yourself and an honest mouth."

"I can afford bodega coffee. Not good coffee."

"Nobody in that room trusts good coffee anyway." She heard the smile in his voice. "Go home, Mari. Eat eggs if you've got them."

"I've got two and a bruised tomato."

"That's dinner."

When she hung up, she stayed on the landing another minute, not because she wanted to drink now, but because the urge had left a ghost in her muscles and she did not trust sudden emptiness. Cars hissed by on wet pavement. Somewhere up the block a delivery guy cursed at a locked gate. The red neon from the pharmacy sign across the avenue pulsed on and off over her hands.

She put the thirty-month chip back in her coat pocket and held it there through the fabric, thumb rubbing the edge until the metal warmed.

It was not solved, she thought, and it was not even close. But she was not drunk, and she was not blind.

She stepped into the rain and headed for the Flatbush-bound train, planning one bodega stop for marigolds if they had them, black candles if they did not. Her notebook knocked against her hip with each block, each step a small, unromantic vote for tomorrow.


# Chapter 3

By the time Marisol reached Flatbush, the hems of her jeans were dark with rain and bus salt, and the notebook in her tote knocked against her hip with each block. The corner bodega had sold out of marigolds, so she brought two black candles, a loaf of Portuguese bread wrapped in paper gone translucent with butter, and a plastic bag of limes she had not planned to buy. She stood in the hallway outside her mother’s apartment and listened before knocking: the TV low, Leo arguing with somebody in Minecraft, Gloria’s voice from the kitchen saying somebody better not touch that pot with a wet spoon.

When Carina opened the door she had a phone pinned between shoulder and jaw, one sneaker half unlaced, and a stack of school forms under her arm with fluorescent tabs sprouting from the top like feathers. She looked Marisol up and down and stepped aside without hanging up.

“Yeah, Ms. Perez, I hear you. No, I didn’t lose it, they changed the portal again. Hold on, my sister’s here looking like a drenched pigeon.”

Marisol kissed her cheek anyway. “I brought candles.”

“Good, because Mami bought the saint kind and they smell like old man cologne.” Carina pointed the forms at the kitchen. “Put your wet coat on the chair, not the sofa. Leo, move your backpack before your aunt trips and sues us.”

The apartment held the same blended weather it always did in late fall: frying garlic, wet wool, laundry soap, and the honey-wax perfume of prayer candles burning by Tomas’s photo in the corner. Gloria stood at the stove in a faded Mercy Hospital sweatshirt and an apron that said KISS THE COOK in glittering letters worn down to KISS THE C. Her hair was pinned so tight it made her brow look permanently lifted, and every few minutes she nudged her left knee against a cabinet as if reminding it to behave.

“You’re late,” Gloria said, not looking up from the pot. “Rice is done. Chicken’s almost done. Bread can wait. Bread never saves anybody.”

“It was the B41. And rain.”

“It’s always rain, always train, always excuses.” Gloria turned and kissed her forehead fast, then reached for the candles in Marisol’s hand. “Black? Who died in a nightclub?”

“Bodega was out of white.”

Carina finally ended her call and dropped the school forms on top of the microwave. “Those are dramatic. We’re gonna summon a reggaeton demon with those.”

Leo appeared from the bedroom in socks and a Spider-Man T-shirt too small in the sleeves, carrying a plastic container full of pennies. He had drawn a mustache on his upper lip with marker and forgotten about it. “Tia, look. I counted one hundred and twelve cents. Mami says if I get five dollars, I can buy pigeon feed and become pigeon king.”

“Pigeon king of where?” Marisol asked.

“Church steps. Duh.” He glanced at the black candles and wrinkled his nose. “Those look evil. I like them.”

Carina snapped her fingers at him. “Wash your hands and your face. Your face looks like a failed villain.”

“It’s a mustache.”

“It’s Sharpie.”

He marched toward the bathroom muttering, “Every artist gets hated first.”

Marisol laughed before she could stop herself. The laugh came out rusty; she had spent most of the week in controlled voices, donor-safe voices, museum voices with no elbows. Here, the apartment pressed in from all sides and nobody had room for posture.

She set the bread and limes on the table and moved a pile of unopened mail to make space. There were Con Edison envelopes, a school fundraiser packet, a folded circular from Western Beef, and one letter from Mount Sinai addressed to Gloria with FINAL NOTICE stamped in red. Gloria slid that one under the others without comment.

“Where’s the altar cloth?” Carina asked, already opening drawers.

“In the good pillowcase, top closet shelf,” Gloria said.

“Who put altar stuff in a pillowcase?”

“People with no linen closet.”

The first twenty minutes stayed blessedly useless. Gloria told a story about Ms. Batista downstairs who had started charging the building kids fifty cents to pet her Pomeranian because “he has anxiety and labor has value.” Carina countered with gossip from school about a father who tried to enroll his daughter in second grade using a Dunkin receipt as proof of address. Leo reported that Deacon Ortiz had fallen asleep during youth choir rehearsal and snored through “Pan de Vida” while still holding the tambourine. Gloria laughed hard enough to lean one hand on the counter, and Carina did a dead-on impression of the deacon’s little whistle snore that made Leo choke on his own giggling.

Marisol ate and listened and let the pressure come down in increments. This was what she had promised herself on the bus: no interrogation, no notebook on the table, no prosecutor’s tone disguised as curiosity. A family supper on All Souls could carry memory without turning into court, if she kept her nerve and didn’t lunge. Salvador’s voice still sat in her head from two nights ago, the rough low murmur saying family myth like he was saying mildew, something that fed on dark corners and damp. She was here to test corners, not kick the walls in.

Gloria crossed herself before they sat. They stood around the table while she led the quick version of the prayer for the dead, half Spanish, half English, the one she used when the chicken was getting cold.

“Por las almas de la familia, por Tomas, por abuela Luz, por los que no tienen quien rece por ellos.”

Leo whispered, “And for Mr. Jenkins’s cat, because the cat got hit by a bike.”

“A car,” Carina corrected.

“Bike sounds sadder.”

“Sit,” Gloria said. “God understands cats and your dramatics both.”

They sat. The rice came out fluffy and steaming, studded with peas and little cubes of carrot because Gloria still cooked as if vitamins could cancel everything else. The chicken had crisp skin and a puddle of orange fat collecting at the edge of the platter. Marisol tore bread, squeezed lime over her beans, and watched her mother serve everyone else first, last serving for herself as usual.

She started with weather and work and let the conversation drift where it wanted. Carina complained about a copier that ate report cards. Gloria said her patient in Midwood kept hiding blood pressure pills in a fake fern. Leo asked whether saints had birthdays. Marisol said yes, probably, but the church celebrated death days because Catholics liked homework.

“Catholics like guilt,” Carina said.

“Same thing,” Marisol said.

Gloria gave them both a look. “Catholics like structure. People with structure don’t end up talking to themselves in subway stations.”

“Plenty of structured people talk to themselves,” Carina said. “Have you met any principals?”

The room kept moving. Forks clinked against plates. Rain tapped the fire escape. Somebody upstairs dropped something heavy, then yelled sorry through the floor. Marisol let three beats pass, then set her fork down and asked, “Mami, what year was it when we started doing this dinner at home instead of church basement?”

Gloria did not pause long enough to count as a pause, but Marisol felt it anyway.

“After your grandmother died,” Gloria said. “Basement got too crowded and noisy. It’s cleaner here.”

Carina snorted. “Cleaner? In this apartment? Be serious.”

“Cleaner spiritually.”

“No such thing as spiritually clean with Leo around.”

“Mami,” Marisol said, keeping her tone easy. “I mean like the first year after Papi. Did we go to St. Brigid’s that first All Souls or did we stay here?”

Carina answered before Gloria could. “St. Brigid’s. You wore that awful black cardigan with the sequins falling off. You cried through the whole homily.”

Gloria shook her head. “No, that was the year after. First year we stayed here because of your cough, remember? You had that barking cough all week.”

Carina frowned. “That was me with the cough.”

“That was Mari.”

“No, Mami. I missed the science fair because of it.”

“You didn’t miss science fair. You won honorable mention with the volcano.”

“That was sixth grade.”

“Exactly.”

Carina stared. “Mami, what does that even prove?”

Gloria took a sip of water and turned to Marisol. “Why are you asking like this?”

Marisol picked up her fork again and shrugged. “No reason. I’m trying to map the chronology in my head. Salvador said something weird about dates and I wanted to check what I remembered.”

At Salvador’s name, Carina leaned back and gave Marisol the smile she used when a kid tried to hand in copied homework. “Ah. There we go. We’re in museum mode.”

Marisol felt her shoulder blades cinch. Museum mode was exactly what she had promised herself she would not bring to this table, and hearing it named made her want to push just to prove she was not pushing.

“I’m not in museum mode.”

“You’re doing the face.”

“What face?”

“The one where you ask normal questions with your little recorder-brain behind your eyes. Like you’re billing us by the hour.”

Heat climbed Marisol’s neck. Carina was not wrong about the face; she could feel it, that narrow professional calm she wore when she was scared of looking stupid.

Gloria set down her fork a little too hard. “Don’t bring that man to this table.”

“I brought chicken to this table,” Marisol said. “And bread. I asked one question.”

“You asked three questions,” Carina said. “In a row. About a funeral from a hundred years ago.”

“It wasn’t a hundred years ago.”

“It feels like it. I was a kid.”

“So was I.”

Marisol tasted metal at the back of her mouth and kept her shoulders loose, pretending this was still a normal dinner question.

“Exactly,” Carina said. “Kids remember weird pieces. Sequins, coughs, who stole whose socks. Not legal timeline.”

Gloria wiped her fingers on a napkin and looked from one daughter to the other, referee and defendant in the same body. “The dead are not a calendar problem. We cook, we pray, we remember, that’s enough.”

That should have settled it. Instead, Marisol felt the old panic that came when facts went soft in her hands, like paper left in rain.

Marisol kept her voice flat. “It’s not enough if dates don’t match.”

“Match what?” Gloria asked. “A story from a sick man who likes to hurt people with details?”

Sick man. The words landed crooked in her chest, because they were true and also a dodge, and she hated that both could fit at once.

Marisol felt the old spike of temper and tamped it down with bread, chewing until her jaw hurt. “He said Papi’s march happened after the museum strike, and I thought it was before. I’m asking because maybe I’ve got it wrong.”

Carina gave a short laugh with no humor in it. “Or maybe you’re shopping for cracks.”

“Can you not?”

“Can you?”

Gloria cut in. “Both of you, stop. Carina, pass Leo his water. Mari, keep your voice down. Tomas died, we buried him, and I still took the 5:20 bus to Mercy the next morning because rent was due. That is what happened here.”

“That’s church language—”

“No,” Gloria said. “That is bill language. That is rent language.”

The sentence landed heavy enough to quiet everyone for a moment. Even Leo looked up from sculpting a rice mound with his spoon.

Carina recovered first, voice bright and mean in the way sisters perfected by age ten. “You want exhibits? Fine. I’ll print labels after I pack Leo’s lunch and call Ms. Perez back. Exhibit A, Mami’s blood pressure cuff. Exhibit B, cold chicken because we’re doing this again. Exhibit C—”

“I didn’t ask for—”

“Then eat.”

Marisol put rice in her mouth and tasted almost nothing.

Leo reached for the beans, elbowed his water, and knocked half his plate onto the table. Rice shot across the plastic tablecloth like hail. A fork clattered onto the floor. There was one second of shocked silence, then Carina cursed under her breath, Gloria said “Ay bendito” like she was exhausted by gravity itself, and Leo froze with his eyes already wet.

“It’s okay,” Marisol said, standing.

“It’s not okay, it’s sticky,” Carina said, grabbing paper towels. “Leo, feet up. Don’t grind it in.”

Leo lifted his feet and looked miserable. “I’m sorry. I was trying to make a mountain for the beans to flow.”

“A rice dam project at dinner,” Carina said. “Very engineer. Very doomed.”

Gloria couldn’t help it; she laughed once, sharp and involuntary. “Your father would’ve said same thing. Tomas always made floods with his plate.”

There it was: Tomas as punch line, Tomas as weather pattern, Tomas the affectionate ghost who never had to stay still long enough for questions.

Marisol crouched to gather grains from the floor and felt her knee pop. At eye level with the chair legs and shoe scuffs she could smell bleach from the morning mop, old grease under the stove, and candle wax softening in the heat. Carina passed her more towels without meeting her eyes.

“Thank you,” Marisol said.

“Don’t thank me, just don’t step on the rice,” Carina muttered.

They reset plates and kept eating. Gloria shifted the conversation to neighborhood survival: who got their package stolen, which landlord finally fixed a boiler, whose son got picked up for tagging the B44 stop with giant silver letters. Marisol contributed where she could, laughed in the right places, and let the current pull away from dates. That was the punishment here, she realized. They did not shout and they did not exile her; they deflected until she either played along or looked cruel for refusing.

After dinner Gloria lit the black candles with obvious skepticism and arranged them anyway in front of Tomas’s framed photo, beside a chipped ceramic Virgen and a supermarket bouquet gone papery at the edges. They said another short prayer. Leo mumbled names of two dead pigeons and one dead YouTuber. Carina squeezed his shoulder, trying not to laugh. Then she sent him to brush his teeth while Gloria packed leftovers into mismatched deli containers.

The apartment moved into cleanup choreography everyone knew by muscle memory. Carina scraped plates and stacked them by size. Gloria wrapped chicken bones for soup stock because no part of anything got wasted in this house, not even cartilage. Marisol rinsed glasses and listened to the faucet’s stutter and the murmur of TV commentators through the wall. She was careful with her breathing. She had not been this physically close to her mother and sister in months without somebody storming out.

“Mari, grab the foil from my room,” Carina said, shoulder-deep in the fridge. “Top dresser, by the fan. If Leo touched it, it might be in his backpack. If it’s not there, check under the bed because this house hates me.”

“Got it.”

The hallway to Carina and Leo’s room was narrow enough that Marisol brushed both walls if she carried anything wider than her own hips. A night-light shaped like a basketball cast weak orange light over the floor. She stepped around a pile of sneakers and a basket of unfolded laundry and entered the bedroom, where a standing fan clicked in tired circles and a poster of planets peeled at one corner.

Foil sat exactly where Carina said, half-used roll on top of a dresser crowded with school IDs, hair ties, hand sanitizer, and a jar of pennies Leo had not finished counting. Marisol reached for it, then stopped when she saw what sat beside it.

On the dresser, tucked partly behind a devotional card of Saint Michael, lay a narrow envelope the color of nicotine. It had no address and no stamp, but faded blue ballpoint on the flap read, B.B. Nov. 89 - G/S. The initials caught in her ribs because Salvador had blown up dinner with dates and Gloria had answered by shutting the door. The flap had been opened and reclosed so many times it held a permanent bow in the paper. She should have turned around right then, and she felt that warning in her throat like a cough. The errand was done, the family was still talking, and nobody had stormed out. She could walk back to the kitchen, hand over the foil, and keep her dignity intact.

Instead, she set the foil down and touched the envelope with two fingers as if it might burn.

She chose to verify one fact before anybody could fold it away again. She told herself she was only checking whether the label meant anything real, and then she opened it anyway.

Inside were six photographs, all old prints with scalloped white borders gone soft and gray from handling. The top one showed Gloria in front of a brick wall wearing a waitress uniform Marisol had never seen, hair loose to her shoulders, mouth open mid-laugh. The second showed Carina as a toddler in mismatched socks on a tricycle. The third was a group shot at what looked like a church picnic, faces sun-blown and hard to read.

The fourth photograph stopped her cold.

Gloria stood on a boardwalk with the ocean washed out behind her and gulls streaking the sky. She looked younger than Marisol had ever known her, maybe late twenties, maybe thirty, hard to tell with the grain and light. Her arm was around a man in a paint-splattered denim jacket, his head tipped toward hers like they shared one private joke. He had Salvador’s long nose, Salvador’s crooked right index finger, Salvador’s specific amused contempt in the eyes, the look that said he saw through everyone and still wanted their company.

On the back, in blue ink faded to smoke, someone had written: Brighton Beach, Noviembre 1989. After shift. G + S.

Marisol flipped it over, then back again, as if speed could turn it into somebody else. She did the math automatically, the way she handled subway transfers, relapse days, and invoice due dates. It was pre-1990, years before any version of the family story put Salvador anywhere near her mother’s life. This was not a donor-cocktail sighting or a once-at-a-gallery acquaintance; his arm circled Gloria’s waist, her shoulder pressed into his, and the touch read as practiced. He was embedded in the story, not peripheral to it.

From the kitchen Carina called, “Mari? Did you die in there? I need that foil before this chicken turns into science.”

Marisol slid the photo back into the envelope so quickly she bent one corner. Her hands shook, just enough to make the paper rasp loud in the small room. She forced herself to flatten the corner with her thumbnail, stacked the photos exactly as she had found them, and replaced the envelope behind Saint Michael.

“Coming,” she called.

She stepped into the hallway, then stopped and went back two steps. She told herself she would not steal the photo, because a missing print would be obvious by breakfast and Gloria would burn every other trace. She pulled her phone from her pocket, opened the camera with the sound off, and snapped two shots, front and back, frame tight, timestamp visible. If Carina caught her doing it, she would call it spying and throw her out for good. If Gloria found out, she would lock every drawer and Marisol would never see this envelope again.

Back in the kitchen, Carina held out her hand without looking. “Foil?”

Marisol gave it to her.

Carina tore off a sheet with her teeth because the box edge was dull, then glanced up and squinted. “You okay? You look pale.”

“Hot in here,” Marisol said.

“It’s November. We have one window cracked.”

“Still hot.”

Gloria was portioning rice into containers with priestly attention, pressing each lid down until it clicked. “Take some home. You’re too thin.”

“I’m always too thin to you.”

“Because you skip real meals and eat almonds like a bird.”

Marisol almost laughed, and the almost hurt. She watched Gloria’s hands work: short nails, a knuckle swollen from old cleaning jobs, the vein on the back of her right hand like blue thread. Those hands had scrubbed hospital floors, lifted patients, peeled onions by the fifty-pound bag when she took cafeteria shifts, counted cash at midnight so tuition checks did not bounce. Those hands had also tucked a photograph into an envelope and hidden it behind a saint.

She tried one more time, softer.

“Mami,” she said, “that year Papi died, did he work at the museum right up to the end or had he already stopped?”

Carina made a sound like a brake squeal. “Jesus, Mari.”

Gloria did not look up from the container. “He worked when there was work. Your father was never afraid of work.”

“That’s not what I asked.”

“He did what he had to do.”

“Mami.”

Now Gloria looked at her, tired and furious and frightened all in one expression. “Tonight we fed the dead. We did what we were supposed to do. Don’t turn this into a police station.”

Marisol held her mother’s gaze. “I’m not trying to arrest anybody.”

“Then stop asking like you already picked a sentence.”

Carina set down the foil and crossed her arms. “You want truth? Here. You come here when you need material. If it isn’t material, it’s guilt management. If it isn’t guilt management—”

“That’s not—”

“—it’s you raiding my fridge and calling it family time. I’m wrapping leftovers, Mari. Don’t sell me neutral.”

It was a cheap shot and also not cheap at all. Marisol felt it land in the same place shame always landed, a fist-sized knot just under her sternum. She could have fought back with chapter and verse: who paid whose MetroCard in which year, who sat through which detox call, who answered whom at three in the morning. She could have listed love as labor and demanded receipts. Instead she said, “That’s fair,” and heard Carina startled into silence.

Nobody spoke for several seconds. The fridge motor kicked on. Rain thickened against the window. Leo shouted from the bathroom, “I can’t find my dinosaur toothbrush,” and the world lurched back to scale.

“In the mug, by the sink,” Carina yelled, still watching Marisol.

Marisol picked up one of the takeaway containers before Gloria could offer again. “I should go. Early start tomorrow.”

“You always leave right when dishes get hard,” Carina said, but the edge had gone out of it.

“I’ll wash two pans fast.”

“Forget it. I’ll do it.”

Gloria wiped her hands on a dish towel and moved to the door with Marisol, limping slightly now that the evening adrenaline had worn off. In the hallway light she looked older than she had at dinner: jaw set, eyes ringed dark, hairpins still locked like armor.

“At least take coffee bread,” Gloria said.

“I already took rice.”

“Take both. Your landlord can’t evict you for pastry.”

The line was almost a joke, almost not. Marisol accepted the wrapped slice and let Gloria tuck it into her tote, mothering by compulsion even while defending a wall.

At the door Gloria touched Marisol’s cheek with two fingertips, brief and practical as checking for fever. “Pray before you sleep,” she said. “When your head gets loud, prayer helps.”

“My head’s fine.”

“Your head is never fine.” Gloria opened the door. “Go before the rain gets stupid.”

In the stairwell Marisol stopped one flight down and leaned against the cool painted cinderblock. She took out her phone and looked at the two photographs she had just taken. Front: Gloria and Salvador on a boardwalk, young enough to still believe the camera was harmless. Back: Noviembre 1989. After shift. G + S.

She zoomed in until the ink broke into pixels. She zoomed out until both of them fit in her palm.

For years she had held Salvador at a usable distance: the famous painter, the manipulator, the dying man with theatrics and morphine and a talent for other people’s weak spots. He had been external pressure, a storm moving in from somewhere else. The photo changed the geometry. He had stood in her mother’s weather before Marisol had language for weather at all.

Upstairs, someone laughed, then coughed, then dragged a chair across tile. Gloria’s apartment door opened and closed. Life resumed its normal rude volume around the secret like water around a submerged stone.

Marisol put the phone away. The images were contraband on a device her family borrowed all the time to check bus routes, school emails, and Leo's game settings. One swipe from Carina would detonate the secret before Marisol had enough proof to survive the blast. Gloria would call her a thief, Carina would cut off Leo, and every old photo in that apartment would disappear by morning.

She descended the rest of the stairs carrying leftover rice, coffee bread, and proof that she had crossed a line she could not uncross. Passive trust was over. At first light she would take the B41 back toward the museum, with evidence and fallout in equal measure.


# Chapter 4

By the time Marisol got off the B41, the cuffs of her jeans were dark with street water and de-icing salt had ghosted her boots in a powdery crust. Her phone rode in her inside pocket with Gloria's two photographs, screen dark and dangerous. The museum steps held yesterday's cold in the stone. She climbed them with one hand inside her coat, fingers on the chipped blue plastic medallion in her pocket that marked thirty months sober, the way somebody touched a saint's medal before crossing traffic. The wind coming off Eastern Parkway smelled like wet metal and diesel. Inside, the lobby heat hit her face too hard, and then security sent her down a side hall where the air went dry and sharp and chemical. Solvent and old canvas dust. The smell always made her think of both repair and damage at the same time.

She signed the conservation log with the short golf pencil chained to the clipboard. Her visitor badge was orange instead of staff blue, printed with her name and a black stripe that meant escort required. The guard at the door did not look up from his monitor when he buzzed her through. He tapped a Dominican bachata rhythm against the desk with a pen cap while a tiny Mets radio clipped to his collar whispered postseason call-in chatter and offseason trade rumors through static. Marisol caught herself listening for the score and then hated that her mind could jump to baseball when her chest had been tight since dawn.

Ada met her at the end of the corridor beside a rolling cart of boxed nitrile gloves. She had her hair twisted into a low knot with two graphite pencils stuck through it like pins, and she wore the same black fleece she always wore in the lab, cuffs shiny from repeated washing. When she reached for Marisol's badge lanyard to scan it against the interior reader, Marisol saw the thin scar across Ada's right knuckle, white against dry skin that no amount of hand cream ever softened in winter.

"You made good time," Ada said. Her voice stayed low in this part of the building, as if louder vowels could disturb varnish.

"I left before my neighbor started cursing at the boiler. Felt like a miracle."

Ada smiled once, quick. "Don't call miracles in this room. We only believe in process. Come on."

The conservation imaging bay was colder than the hallway and almost perfectly quiet except for the HVAC push above them and a scanner fan that clicked at regular intervals. The triptych sat on an adjustable easel under wheeled lamps, three panels wide, its riverbank scene held in a gray noon that made every figure look one breath from speaking. "Witness at the River" was what the placard said upstairs. Down here it looked less like witness and more like argument. Brushwork thick in the left panel, thinned and glazed through the middle, then suddenly raw at the right where the reeds cut across a body of water so dark it could be paint or oil.

Marisol did not step closer until Ada nodded.

"Ground rules first," Ada said. She pulled a tablet from a drawer and held it between them, screen angled so Marisol could read the bullet points. "Supervised viewing only. No raw file transfer. No photography in the bay. No screen recording. You can take handwritten notes and nothing else leaves this room unless it goes through curatorial approval."

"You said maybe we could export stills."

"I said I would ask." Ada's mouth flattened, not angry, just precise. "Nora said no this morning. She gave us a ninety-minute window and a witness note in the access log. That's already further than policy likes."

Marisol felt heat rise up her neck. She could hear the words she wanted to throw out, all of them reckless, all of them likely to close the door for good. Instead she pressed her thumb into the edge of her notebook hard enough to leave a dent in the cardboard cover.

"Okay," she said. "Show me what we can do."

Ada set the tablet down and looked at her directly. Under the overhead lights her eyes were darker than Marisol remembered, almost flat black.

"Before we start," Ada said, "you need this clear so we don't burn time arguing later. Conservation can prove sequences. We can prove that one layer sits on top of another, that a passage got scraped, that a pigment wasn't available before a certain decade, that varnish was stripped and reapplied. We can prove material events. We can't prove motive. We can't prove intention. We definitely can't prove a family story."

She let that sit, then added, even quieter, "And we can't prove who held the brush unless someone signed under the paint like an idiot."

Marisol gave a short laugh that sounded wrong in the room. "You're saying you'll give me facts and no halo."

"I'm saying I'll give you what the surface tells the truth about. Nothing more, nothing less. Can you work with that?"

It landed harder than an accusation would have. Marisol nodded because the answer had to be yes. If she said no, then she was admitting she came here for confirmation theater, not evidence. She had spent too many years watching people turn dead men into banners to pretend she did not know the difference.

Ada handed her cotton gloves. "Then glove up. Start with raking light. Left panel first."

The lamp rolled across the paint at a low angle, and the river scene shifted into topography. Impasto became ridges and ravines; a shoulder line looked like a broken plaster ledge. Marisol bent at the waist, careful not to breathe directly onto the surface. The oil smelled faintly sweet under the solvent layer, like old linseed and dust warmed by electricity.

"See this here," Ada said, pointing with a capped bamboo skewer. "Edge of the cheek on the kneeling figure. Follow the contour down."

At first Marisol saw only texture, random as cracked mud. Then the light caught a fan of parallel abrasions, each one shallow and deliberate, slanting from temple to jaw.
