"Good. Narrative brilliance is irrelevant without honest amends." He said it flat, not dramatic. "I don't care if you can write this chapter so pretty it makes strangers cry. Did you tell the truth to the people you hit?"

The question landed harder than any insult could have. She looked down at her ink-stained fingers wrapped around the coffee cup.

"Not yet," she said.

"Then that's your job before noon. Celia already heard the first truth. You owe the room the second one."

"You mean tonight."

"No, I mean the ten-thirty at St. Luke. You're coming now."

She laughed once, short and sharp. "I'm still sweating vodka through my pores."

"Great," he said. "Then you'll sound less polished."

She hated him for that sentence and loved him a little for it.

Before they left, he made her call the copy shop and say she could not cover a noon shift because of a personal emergency and she would make up the hours Thursday. Grant, the manager, swore about the timing and then relented after she offered to close. Luis listened without expression, then nodded once when she ended the call.

On the train to Prospect Heights, she stood because sitting made her stomach churn. The car smelled of wet wool, old french fries, and somebody's coconut hair oil. A man in a paint-splattered jacket slept with his head against the pole, mouth open, one boot untied. Two teenagers argued over a cracked phone screen in quick Dominican Spanish. At Atlantic, a woman carrying three reusable grocery bags squeezed in and immediately began humming a hymn under her breath, same four bars looping, steady as a metronome.

Marisol held the overhead rail and let the rhythm of the train knock through her wrist bones. She wanted a drink with an animal clarity that embarrassed her. It was not because she felt sad. It was because she remembered exactly how the first swallow had dissolved the static in her chest, how the heat had spread down her arms and turned every accusation in her head into a low harmless murmur for fifteen minutes.

She thought of the kids at Cypress waiting in plastic chairs with spiral notebooks on their laps, pretending not to care while checking the door. She imagined Celia stalling them with a writing prompt she hated. Write about weather as mood. Write about home as object. Hold still while an adult fails you politely.

At St. Luke's basement, the coffee was already burnt and the folding chairs were in a loose oval that said this was not one of the stricter meetings. A handmade sign on blue poster board read WED MORNING OPEN - NO CROSSTALK - KEEP IT HONEST.

Eileen was at the door with the basket of chips, a tiny woman in a Giants beanie and a denim skirt over thermal leggings, her right thumb wrapped in a cartoon bandage printed with strawberries. She always smelled faintly of Vicks and wintergreen gum. She had a habit of writing names in marker on paper cups so nobody had to ask twice who took what coffee.

"Hey, poet," she said when Marisol stepped in. "You look rough. Sit close to the heat."

Marisol nodded and could not manage words yet.

Luis squeezed her shoulder once and took a chair one seat away, close enough to catch her if she bolted, far enough to let her stand on her own feet.

People came in by twos and threes. A home-health aide in pink scrubs with glitter pens clipped to her badge. A young guy in steel-toe boots who kept rubbing the scar under his chin when anyone looked at him. Mr. Patel from the laundromat near Church Avenue, who never shared but always stacked the chairs when the meeting ended and brought stale butter cookies in a tin shaped like the Taj Mahal.

By ten-thirty-five there were sixteen people in the room. The heater hissed. Somebody coughed deep and wet in the back row. Eileen read the opening in a voice that could have announced bus routes and funerals with equal calm.

"We're here because alcohol beat us and we couldn't beat it alone," she said. "Take what helps, leave what doesn't, and tell the truth in this room. A lie in here is a drink with your name on it."

Marisol felt her pulse in her gums.

Introductions began around the circle.

"I'm Darnell, alcoholic." 

"I'm Priya, alcoholic." 

"I'm Hector, alcoholic." 

Names moved like beads through hands.

When it reached her, she stared at the floor's chipped beige tile and heard herself say, "I'm Marisol, alcoholic." Her voice came out steady enough to pass.

Pass for what, she thought. For alive? For honest?

The topic was accountability after a slip. People spoke in uneven lengths: one man talked ten minutes about his nephew's quince and the open bar; a woman in postal uniform said she had eaten cough syrup by the spoon last week and spent three days pretending she had the flu. Nobody interrupted. Nobody rescued.

Luis spoke when his turn came.

"I'm Luis, alcoholic," he said. "Denial is black ice on Flatbush before dawn. Road looks fine till the bus fishtails. So I run the same checklist: who you called, what money you blew, who got left waiting at the stop. If I start writing voice-over, I'm already in the skid."

He did not look at her once while he spoke. The decency of that made her throat hurt.

By the time the basket reached her row for chips and medallions, she had chewed the inside of her cheek raw. Eileen held up a blue twenty-four-hour chip between finger and thumb.

"Anybody need to restart today?" she asked.

Silence filled the room, thick and electric.

Marisol's left foot began tapping without permission. She put her heel flat and still could not stop the tremor in her calf.

You can wait until tonight, she told herself. You can call Luis after. You can take a private reset. You do not need an audience.

That voice had elegant diction. It used words like audience and private reset and made cowardice sound strategic.

She stood.

The chair legs scraped loud in the room and every head lifted.

"I'm Marisol," she said again, and this time her voice cracked on the second syllable. "I drank yesterday. Vodka. Alone. I missed work and fucked up a youth workshop shift. I'm back on day one."

Chairs creaked and coat sleeves whispered as people shifted. Priya nodded once like she had just heard a weather report she trusted. The young guy in boots stared at his hands. Eileen stepped forward and placed the chip in Marisol's palm.

It was lighter than she remembered and colder, a flat blue coin stamped with ONE DAY AT A TIME around the edge. The metal pressed a clean circle into her skin.

"Welcome back," Eileen said.

Marisol sat down too fast, knees weak. Heat flooded her face, then drained. She wanted to cry and could not. She wanted to run and stayed seated. Her fingers closed around the chip hard enough to leave half-moons from her nails.

For the rest of the shares she kept her eyes on a black gum scar fused to the tile and listened to names of pills, bars, and cousins she had never met. The chip sweated in her fist. When Eileen asked for announcements, a woman in a navy parka by the radiator raised two fingers. "My grandson was at Cypress yesterday," she said, still facing the circle instead of Marisol. "He took two buses from Canarsie. Y'all canceled after he got there." A few heads turned. Somebody's key ring clicked against a chair leg. Marisol's ears burned. "You're right," she said, voice thin. "I left him there with no teacher. I'm sorry." The woman gave one short nod. "Don't give me sorry. Give him Thursday at four-forty-five, if they ever put you back in a room."

After the meeting ended, chairs scraped and winter coats rustled. A few people came over one by one, no speeches.

Priya touched Marisol's elbow and said, "I reset last month after NyQuil and lies. Day thirty-two now. Call if tonight gets loud." She wrote her number on a grocery receipt already covered with pediatric appointment notes for her son and folded it twice before handing it over.

Darnell said, "Good job not waiting till Monday," and went back to helping stack chairs.

Mr. Patel gave her a butter cookie from the tin, serious as communion.

Outside, the cold hit like a slap. Traffic on Atlantic roared past in wet bursts. Luis stood with her under the church awning while people lit cigarettes down the block.

"You did the hard part," he said.

"No," she said. "I did the visible part."

He nodded. "Correct. Hard part starts this afternoon." He lifted two fingers and counted. "One: call Cypress director, not just coordinator, and own it without a performance. Two: daily check-ins with me, nine p.m., no exceptions for 'creative flow' or museum drama. Three: no solo nights this week. Go to your sister's if she'll have you, or my niece's spare couch if she won't. Four: food, water, sleep like it's medicine."

"My sister barely tolerates me when I'm stable."

"Then ask anyway."

"What if Ada asks why I'm weird?"

Luis gave her a look that was mostly pity and mostly not. "You're asking me for permission to keep lying in prettier sentences. Not granted."

She laughed despite herself, then covered her face with her hand. The laugh bent into something close to a sob and then back again.

"I hate this," she said into her palm.

"Of course you do. Shame wants you riding solo. Recovery's route work: same stops, every day."

"Is that in the Big Book?"

"No. That's in the MTA employee handbook." His mouth twitched. Then he turned serious again. "Mari, listen. You didn't lose your life yesterday. You lost your streak and three hundred dollars and some trust. That's real damage. It's also repairable if you stop making art out of hiding."

She let the words settle without arguing. That was new and humiliating and maybe useful.

They walked to the corner bodega because Luis refused to leave her with only coffee in her system. He bought two Gatorades and a container of rice and beans from the steam table that had been sitting there since breakfast. At the register he put a MetroCard refill on the counter and nodded at her.

"Put twenty on it," he said.

"I can't let you keep paying for me."

"This isn't charity. This is logistics. You pay me back next check."

She wanted to refuse on principle, then remembered the balance in her account and shut up.

Back in the cold, she checked the time. 12:14 p.m. She had just enough courage to call Marcy at Cypress before her body talked her out of it.

Marcy answered on the third ring. Her voice was clipped, East New York brisk, with the breathless pace of someone doing payroll while eating lunch over a keyboard.

"Marisol, I was gonna email."

"Please don't do email first," Marisol said. "I need to say this live. I drank yesterday and I no-showed. That's on me. I'm in recovery. I reset this morning. I know I lost the Tuesday section and I understand why. I still want to keep working with the program if you'll let me reapply after policy window."

Silence on the line except distant office chatter and a microwave beeping.

"Thank you for saying it straight," Marcy said finally. "But staffing can't run on maybe. You're off the paid Cypress roster for December."

"I know."

"Hold a second," Marcy said. "Kiara and her aunt are here for drop-in because of yesterday. They asked to talk to you. You're on speaker."

A breath, then a younger voice. "Ms. Vega? It's Kiara from Tuesday."

"Hey, Kiara."

"You said you'd line-edit my college statement before midnight. Deadline's tonight. I turned it in with my comma stuff still wrong."

Marisol pressed her thumb hard into the edge of the MetroCard in her pocket. "You're right. I dropped you."

Another voice came in, older and clipped. "I'm her aunt, Ms. Ortiz. I left my shift early and paid fourteen bucks in train fare so she could make that workshop. We stood in that hallway forty minutes."

Marisol looked at the slush ridges by the curb and kept her voice flat. "You shouldn't have had to do that. I failed both of you."

Marcy came back on. "All right. Kiara, I have your draft. Go sit with Celia."

Paper rustled. A door shut somewhere on Marcy's end.

"Policy is this," Marcy said. "No paid teaching for you this month. If you want January consideration, you do two unpaid observation Saturdays, send me thirty signed meetings in thirty days, and sit for a conduct review on January fifth with me and site ops. Review is not reinstatement."

"I understand."

"Good," Marcy said. "Own it in writing by five p.m. today: what happened, who got hit, and what you do next. If that email reads like performance instead of accountability, we're done."

"It won't," Marisol said.

After she hung up, Marisol stared at the wet sidewalk, gray slush gathered at the curb in ridges like dirty snowdrifts. No replacement shift. No quick money. A month of unpaid proving ground and a review date that could still end in no.

Luis watched her face. "Well?"

"No paid slot," she said. "Maybe a January review if I do unpaid observations and bring thirty meeting slips."

"Then that's your route," he said. "No shortcuts."

He had to head back to the depot by one-thirty. Before he left, he made her put the nine p.m. check-in alarm in her phone while he looked at the screen.

When he finally turned toward the subway, he lifted one hand without looking back. His left leg dragged half a beat on the first step down.

Marisol stood alone under a sky the color of unprimed canvas and opened her fist.

The blue chip sat in her palm, cheap alloy, scratched at one edge, ordinary as a subway token and somehow heavier than her keys. She rubbed it once with her thumb until the stamped letters warmed.

One day at a time did not sound noble. It sounded like rent math and phone calls and saying the same ugly sentence until it stopped tasting like poison.

I drank yesterday.

She said it once more, softly, to the traffic and the church wall and the version of herself that kept trying to turn accountability into performance. Then she put the chip in the small zip pocket of her backpack, next to a pen, a bus transfer, and the folded receipt with Priya's number, and walked toward the train, already drafting the text to Ada because Luis was right about one thing she hated: no solo nights.


# Chapter 8

The F train doors folded shut behind Marisol and smeared her reflection into the dark glass: coat collar wet at the neck, hair still flat from the hat she had shoved into her tote, eyes too bright for someone on day three. She stood with her hand around the center pole and counted stations the way she counted minutes in meetings, an old trick Luis had taught her for cravings and panic and any other kind of hunger that lied about urgency. There were seven stops left, then six, then five. The text she had drafted by the church wall finally became a plan: she'd bring dinner, and she spent eleven dollars she could not spare on pork dumplings and two bottles of yuzu seltzer because she was not going to walk into Ada's apartment with wine, not even as a joke, not even sealed.

Outside the station the wind came off the avenue and slid under her sleeves. Sunset Park looked rinsed clean and mean in winter: halal carts wrapped in steam, nail salons still lit past nine, men smoking in front of shuttered storefronts while a delivery bike rattled by with a milk crate tied down by old bungee cords. She climbed Ada's block with her shoulders up by her ears, fighting the urge to check her phone for unread messages from work, from Luis, from nobody. Her account balance had dipped below three digits after the workshop penalty and rent transfer, and she could feel that fact as a second heartbeat every time she touched her wallet. She wanted tonight to be one room with one person and food that tasted like anything besides burnt coffee from church basements.

Ada opened the door in gray sweatpants, one sock on and one bare foot planted on the hardwood because she had been painting her toenails and forgotten to finish. A bottle of dark green polish sat on the floor beside her, cap off, brush balanced across the mouth like a bridge. She looked at the dumpling bag, then at Marisol, and said, "You brought enough for six people. Were you expecting an ambush?"

"I panic-bought," Marisol said.

"At least you panic-bought correctly. Come in before my hallway turns into a wind tunnel."

The apartment was one long rectangle with the kitchen in front and a bed shoved behind a folding screen painted with cranes. Ada had pinned grocery receipts to the side of her fridge with tiny magnets shaped like tools: a scalpel, a clamp, a mini palette knife. Each receipt had one number circled in pen. Marisol had asked once what the circles meant, and Ada had said, deadpan, "Evidence that six dollars for scallions is a hate crime." Tonight there were three fresh circles and a fourth note in block capitals: `BUY RICE BEFORE CIVILIZATION COLLAPSES`.

Marisol laughed, genuinely, and felt the laugh surprise her body on the way out.

"I knew you'd appreciate that one," Ada said. "Take your shoes off. The downstairs neighbor filed a formal complaint about mysterious ceiling impacts. I think she means me dropping books, but I'm not giving her ammunition."

Marisol stepped out of her boots and lined them beside Ada's sneakers without being asked. She set the dumplings on the counter and reached for plates.

"Don't touch that drawer," Ada said lightly. "That's where all the cursed takeout chopsticks live. They stab through plastic and then hold a grudge."

"You sound like my mother talking about pressure cookers."

"Pressure cookers deserve fear. Chopsticks deserve respect. Different category."

It should have stayed easy. The easy part almost made it harder.

Ada filled a pot, added ginger slices, scallions, and miso, then slid frozen dumplings into the simmering broth with the same careful hand she used on varnish swabs, thumb steady, wrist loose. Marisol watched her from the narrow strip of counter where the cutting board fit and tried to listen to the domestic sounds like proof that life could still be ordinary: knife on wood, radiator hiss, a muted Korean drama through the wall next door, somebody upstairs dropping what sounded like a bowling ball and then apologizing in Spanish.

"I had to break up a fight today," Ada said.

Marisol looked up. "At work?"

"At the bodega. Two grown men screaming about whether an avocado could be trusted. Not price. Trustworthiness. One of them held it like evidence in court."

Marisol barked a laugh into her sleeve. "What was your ruling, Dr. Park?"

"I told them both to squeeze less and buy more. They ignored me because I was carrying tofu and apparently that disqualifies you from fruit law."

"You'd look good on a bench. Tiny gavel. Terrifying eye contact."

Ada nudged a bowl toward her. "Eat before the noodles die."

They sat at the small table pushed against the window. Outside, the laundromat sign across the street blinked `OPEN 24 HRS` with one dead letter, so it read `OPE 24 HRS`, and Ada claimed it looked like the city had become a minimalist poet against its will. Marisol nearly snorted broth through her nose when Ada performed a solemn reading of the sign in an avant-garde voice and then bowed to nobody.

It was humor unrelated to anything, with no strategy and no extraction. Marisol could feel how rare that had become by how quickly her shoulders dropped.

Halfway through the bowl Ada set her spoon down and wiped her fingers on a napkin. The shift in her face was small, almost polite, which meant she had rehearsed this part.

"I need us to say a few things out loud," Ada said. "Now, not after dessert, because we don't have dessert and also because I hate fake casual transitions."

Marisol felt the old warning flare along her ribs. "Okay."

"First boundary. I'm not your back door into conservation files. No screenshots, no metadata questions, no 'just tell me what this code means' at midnight." Ada spoke quietly, eyes on Marisol, no sharpness in it and no wiggle room either. "If it's museum-restricted, it's restricted. If that makes me less useful to you, then we should know that now."

Marisol looked at the table grain and then up again. "You're right."

"Second boundary." Ada folded the napkin in half, then in quarters, precise and almost absent-minded. "Don't show up at my lab. Don't show up here without asking first unless somebody is bleeding. I need one place in my life that is not under donor panic. This apartment is that place."

"I haven't done that."

"You haven't. I'm saying it before we hit the ugly part of stress and start pretending nobody saw the cliff."

Marisol nodded. She could feel heat climbing the back of her neck, part shame and part relief, because the rules were clear and clarity beat guessing.

"Third," Ada said, softer now. "I can't be your only support line when things go bad. I can be one person. I can't be the whole system."

Marisol swallowed broth that had gone lukewarm. "You mean Luis. Meetings."

"Yes. I mean your life bigger than me." Ada tapped her own knuckle scar against the edge of the bowl once, a small metronome click. "And I mean this personally too. If we're in this, I need your words before your performance. If you're wrecked, say wrecked. If you're angry, say angry. Don't give me polished suffering."

That line landed cleanly because it was exactly the trick Marisol used when cornered: a beautiful sentence in place of the fact.

"Okay," she said again, but the word came out thin.

Ada studied her for a beat, then asked, "Did you drink after your mother cornered you outside the meeting hall?"

The room did not move. The radiator clicked. Someone in the building corridor laughed and coughed and kept walking. Marisol's hands went cold all at once.

She had rehearsed six versions of this confession on the train, each one calibrated for maximum honesty and minimum damage. None of them survived contact with Ada's face.

"Yeah," she said. "I did. Vodka. In a bus shelter on Church. I drank enough to black out the corners and then I went home and kept going. I threw up in my sink. I missed a paid shift the next day and lost the workshop slot. I reset at a meeting two nights ago. Day one chip."

Ada did not flinch, did not rush in with comfort. She asked, "What day are you on now?"

"Three."

"Did Luis hear it from you?"

"He did."

"Are you planning to hide this from me after tonight?"

Marisol almost said no too quickly, and stopped herself. "No. I don't want to."

"Those are different sentences." Ada leaned back in her chair and crossed her arms, not closed, just steady. "Pick one you can keep."

Marisol looked at the steam ghosting above the bowls, then back at Ada. "I won't hide it from you."

Ada nodded once. "Good."

There was no speech about bravery and no halo for confession. Ada's spoon tapped the rim once, and Marisol felt how one evasive answer could empty this room.

"I'm not going to play rescuer," Ada said. "I don't have that in me, and even if I did it would still be bullshit. I can do care. I can do honesty. I can do hard stops if you start lying or using me to dodge your program."

"That's fair."

Ada gave a small shake of her head, spoon hovering over broth gone cold. "Necessary." She set the spoon down again without eating. "And I need one more thing clear because this part matters to me. If we touch tonight and you leave your body, we pause. No guessing game, no pushing through because you think you owe me. I will ask you direct questions and I expect direct answers."

Marisol's breath snagged high in her chest. "Okay."

"And if you want to stop, you say stop. If I want to stop, I say stop. No wounded-artist tragic monologue after."

Marisol laughed once in spite of herself. "You really know me."

"Unfortunately," Ada said, dry as chalk, and reached for her bowl.

They finished eating in a quieter rhythm that was neither broken nor repaired. Marisol helped clear plates. Ada let her wash and took over drying, standing close enough that Marisol could feel warmth through two layers of cotton every time they passed a bowl between them. They talked about nothing that mattered to the mystery: the laundromat cat that had started stealing socks, the museum gift shop trying to sell a tote with a typo that read `IMPRESSIONISM IS A MOO` under a Monet cow painting, Ada's failed experiment with instant pot congee that had fused to the pot like masonry.

"I had to soak it overnight," Ada said, affronted. "Then my neighbor texted to ask if I was doing construction."

"You were. Rice demolition."

"I respect that this is bullying, but I also deserve it."

She smiled when she said it, a quick flash that made Marisol feel both wanted and called to account at the same time.

When the kitchen was clean Ada turned off the overhead light, leaving only the lamp near the bed and the city glow through the window. The room narrowed around them. Marisol leaned against the counter and watched Ada cross the apartment to feed a sourdough starter in a glass jar labeled `JUROR 7` with blue painter's tape.

"You named your starter after a person who sends people to prison?" Marisol said.

"After a person who asks one annoying, useful question before making a decision," Ada said. She stirred flour and water into the jar with a chopstick. "I aspire to due process in fermentation."

Marisol laughed again, then felt her chest pull tight so fast she almost winced. She wanted to step forward, to touch Ada's shoulder, to stop standing in the doorway of every room in her own life. She also wanted to run for the train and call it mercy.

Ada set the jar down and faced her. "Where are you right now?"

There it was, that blunt question stress always drew out of Ada.

"Half here," Marisol said. "Half in that bus shelter."

"Do you want to stay here?"

"Yes."

"Do you want me to touch you?"

"Yes." Marisol's voice scraped on the word. "But slow."

Ada crossed the room and stopped within reach without closing the distance all the way. "Show me slow."

Marisol put one hand on Ada's wrist first, then on her waist, waiting for a flinch that never came. Ada's skin was warm from dishwater. Her hands smelled faintly of soap and solvent, the museum living in her pores no matter how many times she scrubbed. Marisol kissed her once, careful, and Ada answered with her palm at the back of Marisol's neck, not pulling, just present.

They moved to the bed because there was nowhere else to go. The springs complained when they sat. Marisol's coat and sweater landed on the floor in a heap next to Ada's single polished toe, dark green and imperfect. Ada touched the burn scar on Marisol's left wrist with two fingers and waited. Marisol nodded. Ada kissed the scar, then the inside of her elbow, then her mouth again with more pressure.

For a while Marisol stayed in it: the friction of denim against her thighs, the cool air on her stomach when her shirt lifted, the rasp of Ada's breath when Marisol bit her lower lip too hard and Ada made a noise that was half laugh, half warning. Marisol slid her hand under Ada's T-shirt and felt ribs, muscle, the shallow dip above her hipbone. Ada shivered and said, "Yeah," like permission and surprise at once.

Marisol wanted to be good at this in a way that had nothing to do with sex and everything to do with deserving. She caught herself performing even now, trying to calibrate touch for effect, trying to earn safety through technique. The thought turned sour and familiar.

Ada pulled back enough to look at her. "You're gone again."

Marisol blinked. The ceiling came into focus: one water stain shaped like a coast she could not name. Her jaw ached from clenching.

"I know," she said.

Ada rolled away a few inches but kept one hand on Marisol's forearm. "Do you want to stop?"

"No."

"Do you want to keep kissing and stay above the waist?"

Marisol shook her head.

"Do you want to keep going the way we were?"

Marisol took a breath that hurt on the way in. "I want to keep going, but I need to not feel like I'm being graded."

Ada's mouth twitched. "By me?"

"By me."

"Okay." Ada tucked a strand of hair behind Marisol's ear with unnecessary gentleness. "Then no grading. Tell me one thing you want right now. Specific."

Marisol closed her eyes, opened them. "Your hand on my throat. Not tight. Just there."

Ada's eyes searched hers. "Like this?"

Her palm settled under Marisol's jaw, thumb resting by her pulse, pressure light and steady. Marisol let out a sound she had been holding since she stepped off the train. Her body came back in pieces: skin first, then breath, then hunger without apology.

"More?" Ada asked.

"A little."

Ada adjusted and kissed her again. This time Marisol stayed. She pulled Ada's shirt over her head and ran her mouth along the scar on Ada's right knuckle when Ada braced herself on the mattress, a small pale line that caught the lamplight. Ada hissed, then laughed into Marisol's shoulder.

"That is absolutely unfair," Ada said.

"You can file a complaint with the avocado court."

Ada kissed her hard enough to shut her up, and Marisol thanked God and chemistry and dumb luck for humor surviving in this room.

They undressed each other with occasional clumsy elbows and one near-disaster when Marisol's foot caught the fitted sheet and nearly launched them both off the bed. Ada snorted into Marisol's neck and said, "Romance," then guided her back with a hand flat between her shoulder blades. Marisol felt the weight of that hand and trusted it.

When Ada slid down the bed and kissed the inside of Marisol's thigh, Marisol's first instinct was to apologize for every inch of herself: the old razor nick near her knee, the bruise blooming yellow where she had knocked into a copier tray, the ghosted bloat still lingering under her navel from relapse dehydration. Instead she grabbed the sheet and said, voice shaking, "Yeah. Stay there."

Ada looked up once for confirmation, got it, and kept going, unhurried, attentive, ruthless in the best way. Marisol's fingers tangled in Ada's hair. She did not go elegant. She went loud enough that Ada reached up and pressed two fingers to Marisol's mouth, smiling without breaking rhythm.

"Thin walls," Ada murmured.

Marisol bit those fingers lightly and laughed against them, then lost the laugh to a moan she could not contain. The room narrowed to touch and sound and the smell of miso still hanging in the air.

Afterward Ada climbed back up, breathless, cheeks flushed, and lay half on top of Marisol with one knee between hers. Marisol rolled them and kissed Ada until Ada's control frayed, until Ada grabbed Marisol's wrist and whispered, "Ask me," through clenched teeth.

"Can I touch you here?" Marisol asked, hand hovering at Ada's hip.

"Yes."

"Can I keep going?"

"Yes, keep going."

The words were simple and exact and hotter than anything performative. Marisol moved the way Ada asked, listening. Ada's body answered in shifts: breath stuttering, shoulders going rigid then loose, a short curse in Korean that Marisol did not understand and did not need to. When Ada came, she covered her own mouth with the heel of her hand and laughed helplessly into the pillow after, hair stuck to her face, looking younger and more wrecked than Marisol had ever seen her.

They lay still for a long minute, both of them sweating despite the winter draft. A siren passed somewhere three blocks away, then faded. The radiator knocked again like a stubborn tenant.

Ada was the first to move. She reached for the water bottle on the floor, took a drink, handed it to Marisol. "You okay?"

"Yeah." Marisol took water and tasted metal from the cap and salt from her lip. "I'm okay, actually."

Ada raised an eyebrow. "Look at you, using contractions after all."

Marisol laughed weakly. "I deserved that."

They sat with their backs against the wall, sheet around their waists, knees touching. The lamp cast a yellow wedge across Ada's thigh and the edge of the folded screen. Marisol could feel the old instinct to seal the night in pure feeling, to call this healing and stop there. Ada had already taken that escape route away from her, and Marisol was grateful.

"I need to say one more thing," Marisol said.

Ada nodded but did not rescue her with an easy expression.

"When I drank, I wasn't trying to have one bad night and then bounce back pretty. I wanted annihilation for about forty-five minutes. I wanted to not be a person with a mother and a body and rent due. I bought the bottle because I knew exactly how fast it would shut things off." She rubbed the heel of her hand over her sternum. "I'm saying this because if I pretend it was a slip, I'll lie again."

Ada listened with her chin tucked, eyes narrowed in focus. "Thank you for not making it pretty," she said. "And I need to match that." She pulled the sheet higher over her chest and pinched the hem between two fingers. "I don't have capacity for chaos right now. Work is already a pressure cooker and Nora is three emails away from combusting. If this turns into missing nights, mystery texts at 2 a.m., or me guessing whether you're alive, I'm out. That's me protecting my own oxygen."

Marisol took that in without arguing. "That's fair."

"Necessary," Ada corrected, gentler this time.

"Necessary."

"Here's what I can do," Ada said. She stopped counting on her fingers and set her palm flat on the sheet. "Look at me. We plan dinners. You send honest texts. When I ask if you're craving, you answer straight. If you go shiny and evasive, I call it. If you vanish, I step back. Clear?"

Marisol felt heat rise again at the bluntness, but she nodded.

"Files are off-limits," Ada said. "I'm not your nightly crisis hotline, and I'm not pretending this is low stakes because we're sleeping together."

"I hear you."

Ada angled her body closer until their knees locked. "Tell me your side. What are you offering?"

No one had asked Marisol that in years without hidden trapdoors.

She took her time. "Okay. I'll tell the truth first, even when it makes me look feral. If I'm at the edge, I call Luis before I text you. I'll hit meetings daily this week. Your lab stays yours unless you invite me. And if I start using you like a keycard, no, like a crowbar against my own panic, call it right there. Don't wait to be polite."

Ada's mouth softened at one corner. "I was never going to wait to be polite."

"I know. That's one of your charms."

"Top three, easy."

They both smiled, small and tired. The smile felt earned, not decorative.

Ada leaned over and kissed Marisol once, light. "I also have a petty rule."

"Oh no."

"If you ever bring those chili-lime tortilla chips into this apartment again, we're done."

Marisol stared. "That's your hard line?"

"They smell like citrus cleaner and disappointment." Ada shuddered theatrically. "I won't live like that."

Marisol laughed so hard she folded forward and the sheet fell off her shoulder. She had not expected laughter after confession and sex and hard promises and risk, but there it was, loud and unpretty and real. Ada laughed too, then grabbed the sheet and wrapped it around both of them like a truce flag.

Later, after another glass of water and a quick shower where they took turns under the weak pressure and argued about whether body wash could legally call itself "ocean breeze" in a city that smelled mostly like diesel and wet stone, Ada sat at the edge of the bed rubbing lotion into her solvent-dry hands. She did each finger slowly, methodically, as if counting bones. Marisol watched and thought about care as labor, not aura.

"What meeting tomorrow?" Ada asked.

"Seven a.m. basement on Lincoln."

"Text me when you're out. One line is enough. I want to know your day started on your feet."

"I will."

Ada clicked off the lamp and settled under the blanket, back against the wall, knees up, making space for Marisol to curl into her side. In the dark, the city reduced itself to hum and occasional horn. Marisol listened to Ada's breathing settle and let her own follow.

She felt the bargain for what it was: no halo, no guardrail, just Ada's knee pressed to hers under the sheet, warm and solid, and one lie away from vanishing by breakfast.

Marisol lay awake another ten minutes, maybe fifteen, feeling the blue plastic edge of her day-one chip in the pocket of her jeans on the floor, knowing it would still be there in the morning, small and hard and impossible to poeticize. She reached for her phone on the nightstand, typed one message to Luis before she could talk herself out of it.

`Saw Ada tonight. Told her about the drink. Day 4 tomorrow. Going to Lincoln 7am.`

He answered fast enough that she knew he was on his break route.

`Good. Keep it boring and honest.`

Marisol set the phone face down and slid back under the blanket. Ada, half asleep, reached for her without opening her eyes and found Marisol's wrist, thumb resting on the scar as if taking a pulse. Marisol let that touch anchor her. Outside, a truck downshifted on Fourth Avenue. Somewhere in the building, someone laughed in their sleep.

In the dark she felt the night settle into a harder shape. Ada knew the worst recent fact and stayed, and at seven a.m. Marisol would have to prove it again with her feet on a church-basement floor. Rain started needling the window just before sleep took her.

She closed her eyes and listened for morning.


# Chapter 9

By the time Marisol reached the roof garden, her socks were wet above the ankle and her left hand smelled like pennies from gripping the subway pole too hard. The museum had shut the public side of the roof for the season. Burlap sacks tied around planter boxes made the fig trees look like headless bodies. A strip heater clicked and failed to catch. Eastern Parkway hissed below in that steady wet-tire sound that made every car seem farther away than it was.
She had done the 7:00 a.m. Lincoln basement meeting, kept day four alive all day without announcing it to anyone, and still arrived here soaked and shaking.

She held her cassette recorder inside her coat as if it needed body heat to stay honest. It was a cheap secondhand Sony she had bought from a retired DJ who now ran a copy machine repair desk two blocks from her shift job. The eject button had a hairline crack. She had fixed it with transparent nail polish and a prayer she did not believe in.

Leti, Salvador's night nurse, met her at the service door with a paper cup of mint tea in one hand and a blood-pressure cuff looped around her own wrist like a bracelet. She wore cartoon frog clogs under hospital scrub pants and had one silver hoop in each ear, one larger than the other.

"You're seven minutes late," Leti said, and then, because she watched Marisol's eyes drop to the cuff, she added, "I squeeze this thing when I want to punch somebody. Better for everyone." She gave the cuff one hard squeeze and the Velcro sighed. "He's lucid. I don't know for how long. Do not let him talk himself into a war story if you need straight answers. He can still charm an entire court into acquittal."

"I don't need charm," Marisol said.

Leti snorted. "Everybody says that before they hear him." She nodded toward the glass door. "He asked for the heater and then complained it made him feel like roast chicken. He turned it off himself and then blamed me. That means he's scared."

Marisol said, "Thanks," and Leti touched her elbow once, brisk and almost maternal, then went back down the hallway humming an old bolero under her breath.

Salvador sat wrapped in a charcoal wool blanket at the far edge of the roof, where the city lights flattened against low cloud. The yellow in his skin had deepened over the past week into something almost metallic. His right hand trembled where it rested over the arm of the chair, two fingers twitching in rhythm with pain he did not acknowledge out loud. He had put on a black scarf that might once have been elegant and was now pilled at the ends. His chair was angled not toward the view but toward the service door, as if he expected someone to come rescue him from his own invitation.

"You brought the toy," he said when she approached.

"You said tape, not phone."

"Phones can vanish. Tape embarrasses everyone equally." He lifted his chin at the recorder. "Come on, poet. Start your machine before my pancreas files another complaint."

Marisol set the recorder on the steel cafe table between them. She put down two spare cassettes, a legal pad, and the museum visitor map she had been using as a coaster all week. Her fingers had stopped shaking on the train and started again here.

"Date and time first," she said.

"God, yes. We love a ledger."

She pressed record. The wheels inside the cassette clicked and settled into their low hiss.

"Interview nine," she said. "Tuesday night. Roof garden service terrace. Marisol Vega interviewing Salvador Rivas."

He lifted a brow. "You left out your title."

"I don't have one that applies to this conversation."

"Daughter of Tomas Vega."

The word struck behind her sternum, dull and hard, and she kept her eyes on the recorder.

"We're here to document your full testimony," she said. "No riddles tonight."

"I've never spoken in riddles."

"You've spoken in performances. Different problem."

A breath of amusement moved one corner of his mouth. He approved when people could hit back. It had been true on day one and it made her hate him a little more now because she recognized the satisfaction in it.

"Ask," he said.

She read from the page she had drafted in the copy shop stockroom between toner jams. "In earlier interviews, you claimed my family's account of Tomas's death was false. You also said you participated in preserving that account. I need specifics. What did you do. When. With whom."

He closed his eyes for a beat. The lids were thin and papery, almost blue at the edges.

"You sound like a prosecutor," he said.

"Then answer like a witness."

He laughed once and then coughed until his face reddened under the jaundice. When he caught his breath, he took longer to begin.

"Tomas died in a motel off Atlantic," he said. "Not at a march. Not in front of cameras. He died on a mattress with cigarette burns in it and a curtain rod that would not close all the way. I know this because I was there before the body was cold."

Marisol's hand clenched around the pen so hard the plastic bent. She had heard overdose from Gloria. She had not heard a room. A curtain rod. Cigarette burns. The details made it impossible to float above the fact.

"Why were you there," she asked.

"Because Gloria called me. Because she said your father was not waking up and she did not trust the men who were still in the room." He opened his eyes. "Because we had practiced emergency for years and this was finally the real one."

"Practiced emergency," she repeated.

"Poverty is rehearsal," he said. "For loss, for humiliation, for paperwork." He flexed his tremoring hand as if it belonged to somebody else. "By the time I got there, Tomas was gray at the lips. One of the men had a syringe cap stuck to his shoe. I kicked him out. Gloria sat on the floor by the bed and held your father's foot through the sock like if she held on hard enough he could not leave."

Marisol swallowed against the metallic taste rising in her mouth. "And then what."

"Then there was police. Then there was a report. Then there was a life to salvage." He looked past her shoulder at the wrapped trees. "You think lies arrive as fireworks. Usually they arrive as a list of options, all of them bad, and you pick the one that keeps your child fed."

"Don't call it a lie like it's weather." The words came out flatter than she expected. "You built a saint out of a man who needed treatment and got a funeral poster instead."

"He wanted that poster." Salvador's voice sharpened for the first time. "Do not turn him into your passive victim because it helps your anger. Tomas did not want his daughters introduced to the world as the children of an addict who died with a needle nearby. He said that to me when he was still lucid enough to bargain with the future."

"You were there for his bargaining too?"

"I was there for many things your family would prefer I was never near." He leaned forward, winced, and eased back against the chair. "Listen to me. Mercy is not clean. Mercy is ugly and pragmatic and full of bad timing."

Marisol felt her pulse in her gums. "Mercy for who."

He did not answer immediately. The wind pushed damp cold under her coat and she realized she was sweating anyway.

"For Gloria first," he said. "She could barely stand. She had two girls and rent due and no legal cushion if police decided to make a theater of her. For Tomas, because shame is a second death in our neighborhoods. For you and your sister, because children hear the one sentence adults repeat and build a spine around it."

"You gave us a spine that bent around a fiction."

"I gave you a story that did not force you to carry his needle marks in first grade."

"You gave me a story that made me worship damage." She heard her own voice climbing and did not care. "You gave me a script where suffering equals virtue and then you hired me to write your legacy in it."

He looked at her for a long second, not theatrical now, just old and tired and alert in the cruel way of people who still wanted control after their body had defected.

"Yes," he said. "I did that."

The admission landed heavier than a denial would have. She put the pen down because her hand hurt.

"Why me," she said.

"Because you can cut through varnish. Because I thought if anyone could hold contradiction without turning it into sermon, it was you." His mouth twisted. "Because I was selfish. Pick whichever answer lets you keep recording."

She stared at him and then glanced at the recorder. The red light stayed steady, indifferent, faithful.

"You said there was more," she said.

"There is always more."

"Say it plain."

He rubbed his thumb along the edge of the blanket as if testing fabric quality in a store he no longer had energy to walk through.

"I may be your father," he said.

The city noise dropped out of range. She could still see headlights moving below, but the sound came delayed, through water.

"No," she said, before she knew she was speaking.

"I said may."

"Don't hedge with me."

"I'm not hedging. I'm respecting uncertainty where uncertainty exists." His Spanish accent thickened when he was exhausted; vowels stretched, consonants softened. "There was a period. Before your mother married Tomas. Then another period after, when things were already broken between them and nobody had the courage to admit it. Dates blur when people are trying to survive."

"You could have gotten a test." She heard how childish it sounded and kept going. "You had money. You had clinics who did house calls."

"By then you were already six. Seven. You had a father in every legal and social way that protected you. I was the man in the next room at openings."

"So you decided not knowing was noble."

"No. I decided certainty would cost more than it gave." He coughed again, wiped his mouth with the back of his hand, and checked for blood even when none was there. Habit. Fear. "I told myself I was preserving your life."

"Whose life was easier," she asked, "mine, or yours?"

He gave a short laugh that was almost a surrender. "Mine too. Of course mine too. I did not have to answer to either role. I could be generous uncle, patron, witness, whatever mask was least expensive that season."

The honesty in that sentence made her chest ache in a way rage had not.

"Then don't call it mercy," she said. "Call it cowardice with a good tailor."

He looked at her sharply and then, to her irritation, nodded as if she had corrected his color choices on a painting.

"Fair," he said.

Silence stretched. The recorder kept eating tape.

She thought of Gloria's knees swelling in winter, of Carina sorting Leo's school forms at midnight, of Tomas in every story she had ever written as a man on a bright street with righteous blood in his mouth. She had drafted three books toward that image and won grants for his halo. The halo now lay in a motel room with a torn curtain and a syringe cap in a stranger's tread.

"Did my mother know you were saying this tonight," she asked.

"No." He hesitated. "She asked me not to."

"And you waited until your body was collapsing to ignore her."

"I waited until collapse made me less strategic," he said. "Do not grant me purity I did not earn."

She laughed without humor. "You're still curating yourself sentence by sentence."

"I'm a painter. Composition is a disease." He shifted in the chair, wincing, and pressed two fingers below his ribs. "But I'm not lying to you now."

"You expect me to believe that because you're dying."

"No. I expect you to verify. You have become very good at verification." He nodded at the recorder. "You wanted full testimony. Take this too: I stood with your mother in a church basement and we decided, together, not to correct anyone who called Tomas a martyr. We let a mural happen. We let scholarships happen. We let your adolescence happen inside that language."

"You let my writing happen inside it."

"Yes."

"You let me make a career out of a wound you helped stage."

He closed his eyes again, and for a second she thought he had gone somewhere she could not follow. Then he said, quieter, "I read every poem anyway."

It was the tenderness she had not asked for and could not metabolize. She stood up too fast and the chair legs grated against concrete.

"Don't do that," she said.

"Do what."

"Do the little father thing now. Don't hand me affection like it erases the math."

His eyes opened. There was hurt there, and calculation, and something like pride.

"It does not erase the math," he said. "It adds to it."

The recorder felt throwable, one clean arc over the roof edge. Her body pitched toward Eastern Parkway, toward the bodega with the broken bell, toward a pint swallowed behind the ATM where nobody would have to look at her face. For one brutal minute she pictured calling Tomas and asking if any of it had ever belonged to him.

Instead she sat back down and asked, "Who else knows."

"Gloria. Me. Maybe Carina suspects the shape without the label."

"Nora?"

"No." His mouth tightened. "Nora knows enough to smell risk, not enough to draw blood."

"Did you put this in the notebooks."

"Not directly. I used initials and cowardly metaphors."

"Convenient."

"Survival," he said, then corrected himself with a tired smile. "Cowardice in a costume."

The heater clicked on unexpectedly and blew a dry wave of warmth across her shins before dying again. It smelled like dust and old wiring.

"Say this on tape," she said. "Say, in one sentence, what you did."

He breathed in through his nose, out through his mouth, like he was measuring whether the sentence would cost him another hour of pain.

"I helped create and preserve a false public story about Tomas Vega's death," he said, voice steady now. "I did it with Gloria Vega, and I justified it as protection for the family when it was also protection for me."

She wrote it down verbatim. Her handwriting looked like somebody else's.

"And the paternity claim," she said.

He nodded once. "I believe I may be your biological father. I never confirmed it. I chose silence because I told myself certainty would do more harm than good."

"And now."

"Now I'm out of future, and you're not."

She stared at him until he had to look away first.

Leti reappeared in the door, not stepping out, just checking the angle of his shoulders and the color of his mouth. She tapped her wrist where no watch sat and raised a brow. Salvador lifted one finger without turning. Two minutes.

Marisol pressed stop. The cassette wheels slowed, clicked, and halted.

"We're done," she said.

"For tonight," he said. "Label it clearly. People lose truth by misfiling."

"People lose truth by choosing comfort," she said.

He almost smiled. "Also that."

She ejected the tape, wrote with a Sharpie on the sticker panel: `RG-09 / FULL TESTIMONY / 7:42 PM`, then, because her hands would not stop moving, underlined `FULL` twice.

When she stood to leave, Salvador reached out with his steady hand, the left one, and touched the back of her knuckles. Skin against skin for less than a second. His fingers were warm despite the cold.

"Whatever name you call me," he said, "don't let them flatten this into one villain and one saint."

She pulled her hand back. "You don't get to assign my ethics tonight."

"No," he said. "I suppose I don't."

She walked through the service corridor with the cassette in her fist so hard the plastic corners marked her palm. Leti caught up at the elevator and handed her a wrapped butterscotch candy from her scrub pocket.

"For your blood sugar," Leti said. "Or your homicide prevention plan."

Marisol almost laughed. She pocketed it and nodded.

On the ride down, she unwrapped the candy anyway, held it on her tongue, and felt her jaw ache from the sweetness.

Outside, the air had gone from damp to cutting. Her phone showed 8:31. She had three missed calls from an unknown number and one text from her landlord: `Need update on arrears by Friday.` The amount sat in her head like an unpaid verdict: twenty-four hundred by December fifteenth or she lost the room.

She could call Luis. She could go to a late meeting. She could walk until her calves burned and her thoughts lined up into something usable.

She got on the B63 and rode south with the cassette in her coat pocket and rage rising like fever.

By the time she reached Sunset Park, her throat hurt from not speaking. She passed the liquor store on Fifth with the bright green lottery sign and did not go in. She did not slow down enough to make it heroic. She crossed at the light because she had promised herself, after chapter six, she would not make dramatic gestures out of basic survival.

Her sublet smelled like radiator steam and garlic oil from the downstairs family dinner. The kitchen window had a crack in the corner she kept forgetting to tape. She dropped her bag on the chair, took off her wet socks, and stood barefoot on cold linoleum, listening to the pipes knock in the wall.

She played the cassette once from the beginning.

Her own voice came out thinner than she expected. Salvador sounded like two people trading the same mouth. In one sentence he was careful, generous, and almost fatherly. In the next he staged his own indictment with the composure of a man arranging lights before a portrait.

`I helped create and preserve a false public story...`

She stopped the tape before the end because she could feel the want for vodka wake up behind her ribs: not an idea, not a moral failing, just the specific mechanical want of anesthesia. Her jaw clenched so hard her molars rang.

She opened the cupboard for seltzer, found one warm can, and drank half of it in four swallows. Then she sat at the table with her laptop and began typing before she had decided whether she was writing notes, a confession, or damage.

The first lines came ugly and direct. No craft distance. No revision ritual. Her fingers outran syntax and then caught it by the collar.

She titled the file `MERCY CLAUSE`.

She wrote:

I was raised on a mural of a dead man with clean hands.

I was raised by women who had to choose between shame and rent and picked rent with both fists.

I was hired to polish the witness.

The witness had jaundiced fingers and called the lie kindness.

She deleted `jaundiced` and wrote `yellow` and then put `jaundiced` back because it was true and she was done translating for people who preferred vague pain.

She kept going.

A man can hide inside genius

for forty years and still call it shelter.

A museum can frame a wound

and call it education.

Daughters inherit scripts,

not money.

She stopped and stared at the last line until it felt too neat. She changed it.

Daughters inherit scripts and overdue notices.

Better.

She thought of Luis telling her facts before feelings, then heard herself answer him from inside this room: feelings were a fact tonight. Her hands shook less when she was typing fast. The poem swung between accusation and inventory. She gave it no names and too many fingerprints. She mentioned a roof garden in winter, a famous triptych with an erased figure, a church basement decision, an artist whose hand trembled when he said mercy. Anyone near the museum orbit could identify the people inside ten minutes.

At 10:56 she copied the poem into her public feed, then into a second platform, then into the newsletter she used when she needed to hit donors and grad students in one blast. She added one line above it:

`For everyone who has been asked to carry a polished lie and call it gratitude.`

No draft folder. No scheduled post. She hit publish with her wrist resting on the blue day-one chip in her pocket like pressure on a bruise.

The response was immediate and obscene in its velocity.

Hearts. Shares. A former workshop student wrote, `holy shit are you okay.` A stranger commented, `this is obviously about Rivas.` Another wrote, `lawyers are already in your mentions, take this down.` Someone clipped the stanza about museums framing wounds and posted it over a photo of the retrospective banner. The line traveled faster than context.

At 11:08, her phone rang with Nora's name.

Marisol let it ring twice, then answered.

"Tell me you didn't just post that," Nora said.

Her voice had the flattened edge of someone reading while talking. Marisol pictured her in the curator office with the overhead lights off, one desk lamp on, eye drops on the blotter, her spare ballet flats on because the heels had finally become impossible.

"I posted a poem," Marisol said.

"You posted a direct allegation wrapped as a poem." Nora did not raise her volume. She never needed to. "Our board vice chair sent it to me before you picked up this call. Donors are texting screenshots in all caps."

"Then they can read."

"Do not do that performance with me tonight. This is contractual exposure."

Marisol barked a laugh. "Exposure. Right."

"Marisol." Nora inhaled hard enough that the phone crackled. "I know you're angry. I'm not dismissing that. But this post creates legal risk for you and for the institution. Take it down now and we can contain this before counsel starts dictating terms."

"Containment is your religion." Marisol stood and paced the kitchen, bare feet sticking on a spot of old syrup near the stove. "I spent the night listening to a dying man call fraud mercy. Your first instinct is still reputation management."

"My first instinct is to keep you employed long enough to finish the work."

That landed. Not because it softened her, but because it was true in the blunt way Nora reserved for emergencies.

"Then maybe don't ask me to retract truth in public because rich people are uncomfortable," Marisol said.

"I'm asking you to stop handing them evidence that you breached your own residency terms." Nora's voice went colder, docket-clean, and Marisol felt her own jaw tighten as the legal nouns lined up. "Defamation. Confidentiality. Morality clause. I'm saying those words because counsel already has."

"Did counsel hear the tape?"

"What tape."

Silence. Marisol had not meant to say it. She closed her eyes.

"Nothing," she said.

Nora spoke more quietly. "Take the post down, Marisol. Tonight. We can revisit language tomorrow with representation in the room."

"No."

"I need to hear that as your final answer."

"It's my answer."

A long pause. In the background, Marisol heard the soft clack of keyboard keys and, faintly, the whine of the office printer warming up.

"Then this moves to formal response," Nora said. "I don't want that, but I don't get to want much right now. You'll receive calls."

"From who."

"From people who bill by the quarter hour and call panic strategy." She exhaled. "Eat something."

The line clicked dead.

Marisol stared at the phone as if it might explain itself. Her post count kept climbing. Three thousand views. Five. Someone had screen-recorded it in case she deleted. One account tagged the museum official page and wrote, `Care to comment on this?`

At 11:19, an unknown Manhattan number called. She answered on speaker while opening the fridge. Half an onion, mustard, old rice, no eggs.

"Ms. Vega, this is Daniel Pritchard from Halden Pike, outside counsel for the museum and Mr. Rivas's representation."

The voice was crisp, expensive, and young enough to still believe in billable certainty.

"You all move fast," she said.

"Risk moves fast," he said, as if proud of the line. "I'm requesting immediate removal of your recent online publication pending legal review."

"Requesting or demanding."

"At this stage, requesting." A paper shuffled near his mouth. "Your residency agreement prohibits publication of potentially defamatory material derived from non-public interviews and internal institutional context."

"Potentially defamatory means true and inconvenient now?"

"It means unverified and injurious."

"Unverified by who."

He paused for the first time. "By an agreed process."

"There it is," she said. "Process."

"Ms. Vega, if removal does not occur tonight, we will send a preservation and cease letter before morning. I would prefer not to escalate."

"Then don't."

"You're making a consequential choice in an elevated emotional state."

"You don't know my emotional state."

"I can infer from the content of the post."

"Infer this," she said, and hung up.

The craving hit hard after the call, mean and specific. Her body did not care about principle. It wanted numbness, blunt and immediate. She went to the sink, ran cold water over both wrists, then bent and splashed her face until her scalp tingled. She dug in her coat pocket for the butterscotch Leti had given her and found only the waxy wrapper. She must have chewed it on the bus without noticing.

Her phone vibrated again. Not a call this time. A text from Ada.

`Did you post that poem about him?`

A second bubble appeared before Marisol could answer.

`I'm not asking as staff. I'm asking as me. Are you safe tonight?`

Even in text, Ada's pressure logic held: practical question first, feeling threaded through it, no melodrama.

Marisol typed, `Safe enough. Not steady.` She added, `Can't do a long call.` and sent it before she could sand it down.

Then she opened Luis's thread and wrote: `Craving hard. Posted. Need check-in.`

He called in under a minute. Depot radio chatter hissed behind him; someone shouted route numbers and a bus coughed into reverse.

"You drink."

"No."

"Give me the full sentence."

"I haven't had a drink."

"Good. Keep it that way." His voice stayed calm, workmanlike. "You got socks or shoes on?"

"Not yet."

"Fix that first. Then heat food. You don't run a route on an empty tank."

"Nora and legal already hit me."

"Of course they did. They hit with words first, pay second. Tonight your job is body maintenance and no bottle. Tomorrow you deal with paperwork."

She swallowed. "He told me he might be my father."

Luis went quiet for one beat, not more. "That can be true and still not get to drive this hour. Stay with the sequence. Socks. Rice. Water. Text me proof in ten."

She did exactly that: dry socks, microwave rice, six hot bites too fast, burned tongue, tap water. She sent Luis a photo of the empty bowl and got back, `Good. Set a ten-minute timer and breathe till it rings.`

She texted Ada, `Ate. Still here.` Ada answered with one line.

`Stay here.`

At 11:47, another email landed. Subject line in all caps: `URGENT NOTICE - ONLINE PUBLICATION REMOVAL REQUEST`. She opened it and read enough to catch the phrases: potential breach, reputational harm, immediate deletion, preserve all related communications.

At 11:52, the first voicemail from the same lawyer arrived, transcribed by her phone into sterile fragments:

`...advising immediate compliance...`

`...failure may affect compensation disbursement...`

`...further participation in the residency under review...`

Compensation disbursement. The phrase translated itself into rent, MetroCard, groceries, one less month before the credit card minimum collapsed into fees she could never outrun. The phone heated in her palm; she nearly pitched it at the wall. Another surge had her naming donors in her head, rehearsing the motel details until somebody choked.

At 11:58, Daniel Pritchard sent another email with a PDF attached. Subject line: `NOTICE OF INTERIM STIPEND HOLD`.

`Effective immediately, the December residency disbursement scheduled for Friday is suspended pending compliance with the removal demand delivered at 11:19 PM.`

Her stomach dropped so fast she grabbed the counter to steady herself. She opened her banking app. Balance: `$183.27`.

She reopened her landlord's text and saw the number again: twenty-four hundred by Friday.

She opened the poem post, thumb hovering over `Delete`, and read her own first line one more time.

I was raised on a mural of a dead man with clean hands.

She locked the phone.

She did not delete.

At 12:06, she took the cassette out again and set it on the table beside the laptop. Plastic rectangle, forty-five minutes per side, the whole night compressed to something that could fit in a coat pocket and still detonate a season.

She placed the blue day-one chip on top of it like a coin on a closed eye.

Through the thin wall, her neighbor laughed at some television joke, sudden and full and normal. A radiator banged once, then twice, then settled. The city did not pause for private revelations, for paternity claims, for museums entering crisis mode. It moved and demanded fare.

Marisol refreshed the page again.

The poem was still up.

So was she.

At 6:14 a.m., a museum email landed: `Please report to Conference 5B at 8:30 regarding immediate residency compliance review.`


# Chapter 10

At 8:27, the employee gate read Marisol's badge, paused, and flashed amber instead of green. Abdul glanced from his monitor to the email timestamp still open on her phone and then back to her face, treating all three like evidence. The message had come at 6:14 a.m.: `Please report to Conference 5B at 8:30 regarding immediate residency compliance review.` No greeting. No signature line, only the museum domain and a legal cc chain that looked like tiny handcuffs.

She had not slept. She had lain awake in her Sunset Park sublet listening to the upstairs radiator knock like somebody trapped in a pipe, replaying the poem she had posted and then refused to take down. She could still see the comments climbing under it in bright little blocks, half praise, half bloodsport. She had muted the thread and then unmuted it three times before dawn, each time pretending she was checking for factual corrections and not for punishment.

Inside the security vestibule, the guard with the Knicks thermos nodded at her without meeting her eyes. Abdul normally told her who had won last night and complained about his lower back from standing. Today he took her ID, scanned it, and kept it one second too long. His thumb tapped twice on the edge, a habit she had seen when he was deciding whether to ask for a supervisor.

"You can head up," he said finally. "Fifth floor conference corridor."

"You need the badge back?" she asked.

"Keep it for now."

For now. The two words sat on her tongue like aspirin chalk.

The fifth-floor corridor had polished stone that always made shoe steps sound richer than the people making them. Conference 5B was glass on one side and frosted halfway up, enough privacy for expensive decisions and enough exposure to keep everyone polite. Nora was already inside, coat off, reading from a printed packet with yellow tabs. She had switched from heels to her spare flats, black suede with the toes rubbed dull, and her eyes were pink at the inner corners from whatever strain had kept her up later than donor dinner required.

At the far end of the table sat a man Marisol did not know, broad shoulders inside a navy suit that had been altered recently and not well, one sleeve still long enough to crease over his knuckles. He had lined up three sugar packets next to his legal pad by color, white then blue then pink, and had not touched his coffee. Beside him, a silver-haired woman in a plum coat unbuttoned and rebuttoned the cuff around a compression glove on her left hand, checking her fingertips for color and the room for delay.

Nora looked up when Marisol entered. Her voice was softer than the room deserved.

"Thank you for coming quickly."

"I live on the subway now," Marisol said, trying for dry, missing by a mile. "You wanted immediate."

No one laughed. Nora gestured to the chair opposite counsel.

"This is Elliot Kline, outside counsel. This is Genevieve Pratt from the Advancement Council."

Genevieve gave one small nod, the kind that acknowledged a person without inviting one.

Marisol sat. The conference chair dipped lower than expected, leaving her slightly below eye line. She hated furniture that did that.

Elliot folded his hands, priestly and practiced.

"Ms. Vega, this is a formal administrative review under Section Twelve of your residency agreement, reputational harm and contractual compliance."

His voice had the polished plainness of people paid to make damage sound inevitable. He slid a packet toward her. The first page read `NOTICE OF IMMEDIATE SUSPENSION - PENDING INVESTIGATION`. Her own name looked clean and wrong in fourteen-point serif.

She did not touch the packet yet. "Are you suspending me or reviewing me?"

"Both," Elliot said. "Suspension effective immediately. Review as process."

"Over a poem."

Genevieve leaned forward. Up close, Marisol saw dried acrylic blue in the seam of the donor's thumbnail. Not museum blue, brighter, craft-store bright.

"Because people can identify living individuals tied to this exhibition," Genevieve said. "Because your post implies criminal conduct. Because partners are already asking whether we can still steward this responsibly."

"Partner confidence," Marisol repeated. "You mean donors."

"I mean everyone who keeps the lights on."

Nora spoke before Marisol could answer. "Three committed gifts are on pause until we respond. One lender has already asked to reopen insurance terms on the triptych. I'm not speaking in hypotheticals here. Legal exposure is real."

Marisol finally picked up the packet. Page two had screenshots of her post, highlighted in yellow like evidence in a cheap trial. She recognized the line breaks, recognized the way she had arranged the accusation to cut on breath. Beneath the images sat contract language she had initialed in intake week while eating a stale vending-machine muffin because she had skipped breakfast to make her first orientation on time.

`Resident shall refrain from public communications that could reasonably be interpreted as defamatory toward museum staff, donors, artists, lenders, or associated families.`

Reasonably. Could. Interpreted. Rubber words. Wide enough to strangle anything.

"I didn't name the museum," she said.

"You didn't need to," Elliot replied. "Context completed the identification."

"I didn't name Salvador either."

"You tagged the roof garden location," Nora said. "And the title of your residency project."

The room went quiet in the way rooms do when they are waiting for shame to finish its first round.

Marisol flipped to the next page. Page three was tables.

`Stipend total authorized: $4,000.`

`Disbursed on intake: $1,000.`

`Undisbursed balance subject to immediate freeze: $3,000.`

`Future disbursement contingent on completion of review and institutional determination.`

`Archive credentials: suspended. Badge access revoked at 10:00 AM today.`

She stared at the numbers until they blurred into the shape of her landlord's voicemail. Two thousand four hundred owed by December fifteenth. Credit card balance six thousand eight hundred and climbing, minimum due like an ankle weight every month. Copy shop shifts that covered groceries if nothing broke. The stipend had been the only money with enough air in it to let her breathe.

"You're freezing all of it," she said.

"The undisbursed balance, yes," Elliot said.

"And that's because I said what happened."

"Because you published allegations without evidentiary clearance through institutional channels."

"And those channels are the problem."

Elliot did not blink. "That statement reinforces our concern."

Marisol turned to Nora. "You asked me for a truth-facing manuscript. Those were your words in the interview room. You said you didn't want tribute language."

Nora pressed the bridge of her nose with two fingers, then let her hand fall flat on the table. "I asked for rigor. I didn't ask for a public accusation while legal review was active."

"Legal review was slow-walking me until he dies."

"Don't make this about speed," Genevieve said. "This is about trust."

"Trust is what people with money call obedience."

Genevieve's chin lifted, a tiny movement carrying years of gala etiquette. "My son is in residential treatment for fentanyl right now. Don't tell me what I call things because I have money."

The words landed harder than Marisol expected. For a second the room changed shape. Genevieve was not donor plaque metal; she was a woman checking her phone every twenty minutes for updates from a facility upstate that took seven thousand a month and still smelled like bleach and panic. The compression glove suddenly made sense.

Marisol swallowed and felt the old reflex rise, apology as survival, apology as bribe. She did not give it.

"Then you know what a lie costs," she said.

Genevieve's face tightened. "I know what public accusation does before proof."

Elliot flipped to another page. "We are not adjudicating historical truth in this meeting. We are adjudicating contract compliance and risk posture."

"Risk posture," Marisol said. "I'm not a forklift you're parking."

"No," he said. "You are a resident in breach."

Nora's jaw worked once. "Marisol, listen. I fought for this residency model because artists deserve paid access. If this turns into litigation, the board guts that model for everybody after you. I'm asking you to hear the institutional scale."

"I hear it," Marisol said. "I hear that my body is the floor tile you're saving the building from."

She pushed the packet back toward Elliot, then pulled it to her again. The paper was heavier than printer stock, expensive cotton blend, soft like skin and impossible to tear clean.

"What are my options?" she asked. "Not your script. My options."

Elliot glanced at Nora, then answered. "Option one: accept suspension, cease all public commentary connected to the museum and its affiliates, surrender active research materials for legal hold, and await determination. Option two: decline terms and trigger immediate termination for cause."

"There isn't an option where I keep working?"

"Not while this review is live."

"Supervised access only. Reading room, no reproductions, no public statements. Give me ten days."

"Denied," Elliot said.

"Five days."

"Denied."

"Forty-eight hours."

"Ms. Vega, stop bargaining this down by the hour," Genevieve said. "This decision isn't a timer."

Marisol laughed once, short and ugly. "Everything in this room is a timer. Salvador's pancreas is a timer. Your donor calls are a timer. Rent is a timer. You're pretending this is about decorum so you don't have to say what it's about."

"What is it about?" Nora asked quietly.

Marisol looked at each of them in turn, wanted one face to crack.

"You need the story pretty until the opening."

Nora did not answer at once. Her silence had always been tactical. Today it looked like fatigue.

"I need the institution alive long enough for any story to be told at all," she said.

"Even if it is false."

"Even if it is incomplete."

The distinction was careful and brutal. Incomplete could be sold. False required blame.

Marisol felt heat in her chest, then the cold that followed when anger hit an immovable wall. Her hands were steady on the table. The steadiness scared her more than shaking would have.

"I want counsel of my own present if this continues," she said.

"You are entitled to counsel before a final determination hearing," Elliot said. "Today's action is administrative and immediate."

"Convenient."

"Lawful."

She turned to Nora again, because Nora had once texted her at midnight about a line break and because betrayal from someone who had seen your drafts hurt differently.

"Say one sentence," Marisol said. "One sentence without legal nouns. Do you think I'm lying?"

Nora held her gaze. In that held second, Marisol saw the strain lines deepen around her eyes, saw the woman who kept spare flats in her tote because twelve-hour days turned ankles into liabilities.

"I think you published before you could protect yourself," Nora said.

It was not an answer and it was the most honest thing anyone had said in an hour.

Elliot slid a signature tab across the packet.

"Sign acknowledgment of receipt."

"Is it consent?"

"No. Receipt only."

Marisol read the line three times. `Signature confirms delivery. Signature does not waive rights.` She signed. The pen skipped on the first stroke of her last name and left a snag of ink like a scar.

Elliot gathered his copy, Genevieve's copy, and Nora's copy into separate folders, color coded, decision already archived before her body had fully stood.

"You must surrender your building keys and research-room locker keycard now," he said.

Marisol unclipped the ring from her bag and set it down. The little brass key for locker C-17 had a strip of blue tape around its neck from when Ada organized the drawers by project. Marisol watched Elliot drop it into an envelope labeled `PROPERTY RETURN`, and for one dumb second she wanted to reach across the table and bite his hand.

"Your digital account access will be restricted by noon," Nora said. "You may request personal files through IT."

"You mean poems are now discoverable."

"I mean metadata is now legal terrain."

"Same thing."

Genevieve stood first, flexing the gloved hand against her palm before buttoning her coat. "For what it's worth, Ms. Vega, I did read the piece twice. You're not untalented."

The sentence had the shape of kindness and the weight of a paper cup.

"For what it's worth," Marisol said, "talent doesn't pay Con Ed."

Genevieve's mouth twitched, not quite a smile. "No. It doesn't."

When they filed out, Nora remained one beat longer. She gathered the yellow tabs from the table in a neat stack, then looked up.

"Don't try to borrow someone else's badge," she said. "Security has your photo flagged."

"You think I'm that dumb?"

"I think desperation makes smart people improvisational."

"You're still talking to me like a case note."

"I'm talking to you like someone who doesn't want to watch you get arrested in a lobby."

Nora picked up her tote, the corner where a pair of flats protruded from the zipper, and left.

Marisol sat alone in Conference 5B until the motion lights dimmed and brightened again, deciding whether a room had become empty yet.

At 10:02 she took the elevator down to the conservation level instead of leaving. The hall there always smelled like acetone and wet wool in winter, like everyone had come in from weather carrying small personal storms. She knew Ada's schedule for Tuesdays: solvent testing at nine, photo documentation at ten-thirty, break at eleven if the intern remembered to spell the pigment names right.

She stepped up to the secure door, swiped her badge, and got a red light with a low buzz that sounded too satisfied. She swiped again, and the light stayed red.

She tried not to make a face in front of the camera.

Inside the glass corridor, two interns in nitrile gloves wheeled a crate past, heads bent toward each other, their talk visible and silent.
