On the far side, Ada emerged from Lab Two in a gray T-shirt under her coat, hair pinned up with the same pencil she used for condition notes. She had a crescent of gesso on her sleeve and her right knuckle scar looked split open again under the fluorescent light.

Marisol lifted her hand, small wave, and Ada saw her. Relief hit Marisol so hard it made her knees loose.

Ada took one step toward the door.

Then Maya DeRosa from HR appeared out of the side office like she had been waiting for exactly that step. Maya wore a green cardigan with a tiny bee pin at the collar, chipped red polish on both thumbs, and the distracted breathing pattern of someone who had left a child with a fever and was pretending not to check her phone.

She touched Ada's elbow, not gentle.

"Dr. Park, one moment."

The badge reader speaker was good enough that Marisol could hear every word through the glass.

"As discussed," Maya said, opening a folder, "no communication with Ms. Vega outside documented channels on restricted materials, residency proceedings, or artist records. Off-channel contact is noncompliance. Noncompliance can trigger immediate leave pending termination. Do you understand?"

Ada did not look away from Marisol while Maya spoke. She looked like she looked at damaged varnish, still, exact, furious at the chemistry.

"I understand the sentence," Ada said.

"I need a verbal yes on compliance."

Ada's jaw moved. "I hear you."

"That's not the question."

Ada finally looked at Maya. "Yes. I understand."

Maya checked a box on her form, then offered a pen. Ada signed with the carpenter-pencil she had tucked in her hair, quick diagonal script, and gave the pencil back to herself like a trick.

Marisol stood on the wrong side of glass watching paperwork decide the range of another person's mouth.

Maya turned and saw her, offered the kind of professional smile that had no blood in it.

"Ms. Vega," she called through the door speaker. "Please direct all communication through legal during your suspension period."

Marisol lifted both hands, empty palms. "I came to talk to my girlfriend, not steal a Rembrandt."

Maya did not react. "Please leave this access point."

Ada moved toward the door before Maya could stop her and pressed her hand flat to the glass opposite Marisol's shoulder, not touching, close enough that Marisol could see the solvent cracks around Ada's cuticles.

"Go home," Ada said through the speaker, voice low. "Please."

"I need five minutes."

"You won't get five. Not here."

"Then outside. Noon. Anywhere."

Ada swallowed. "I can't."

Maya stepped back into frame, eyes on Ada now, not Marisol. "Dr. Park."

Ada dropped her hand from the glass. "Go," she said to Marisol, softer. "I'll text."

Marisol wanted to ask if "I'll text" meant tonight, tomorrow, ever. She wanted to say she was not asking for files, she was asking for a person. She wanted to say her body still remembered the shape of Ada's shoulder blade under her palm from four nights ago and that memory did not recognize policy.

Instead she nodded once and walked away before the wanting made her beg.

In the locker room off the loading dock she emptied what was still hers from a cubby with a paper tag now marked `SUSPENDED`. She found two notebooks, a cracked lip balm, a MetroCard with nine dollars left, and the blue day-one chip wrapped in the same tissue she had grabbed at dawn after lifting it off the roof-garden cassette on her kitchen table. She had tucked it in this cubby before the meeting because she could not stand carrying both talisman and evidence in the same pocket. The cassette stayed in her apartment, under clean T-shirts, where no one had jurisdiction yet.

She sat on the metal bench and opened the stipend table again, then took out her phone and opened her banking app beside it, lining up numbers like the meeting had taught her.

Checking: $183.12.

Savings: $0.00.

Credit available: humiliatingly high, functionally gone.

Rent arrears deadline: thirteen days.

The fluorescent light above her flickered with a high insect buzz. She thought about the first drink with a level of detail that felt pornographic: bottle sweating against her palm, the little metallic hiss when the cap broke seal, the fast medicinal fire from tongue to sternum. She could map it minute by minute, body memory more reliable than any witness statement.

She took the blue chip out of tissue and pressed its ridged edge into her thumb until it hurt. One day at a time, stamped in cheap metal, a sentence that had sounded corny the first year and then impossible and then, somehow, useful. Today it sounded like a joke told by someone with direct deposit.

When she left the museum, the rain had thickened into sleet that made the crosswalk paint look greasy. She went down to Eastern Parkway-Brooklyn Museum and took a Manhattan-bound `2`, then transferred at Atlantic Avenue-Barclays to a Bay Ridge-bound `R` for Sunset Park. Her phone stayed silent through the first two stops.

On the `2`, a man in a construction jacket argued with his bank on speakerphone about a pending hold. In the tunnel between Atlantic and Pacific, two teenagers compared fake IDs in whispers too loud to be private. As the `R` pulled out of 36th Street toward 45th, Marisol's phone buzzed, and Ada's name lit the screen.

The message filled almost the whole screen.

`I can't do calls or in-person contact for seven days. One text check-in at night only. If you show up at work again, I lose my job. I'm sorry.`

Marisol read it once and missed the grab bar when the train jerked, shoulder slamming the door partition hard enough to make her hiss. She read it again and bit the inside of her cheek until she tasted metal. By the third read her left hand had curled so tight around the phone her thumb went numb.

She typed `I get it.` Deleted.

She typed `Are you safe?` Deleted.

She typed nothing and locked the phone.

The train car heater coughed dusty heat over everyone's knees. Outside, grocery awnings snapped in the wind and a guy in a halal apron used a broom to shove slush away from his threshold, each scrape a stubborn bargain he kept losing. She watched him until the train pulled out and left him behind, still pushing at water that kept coming.

At 45th Street in Sunset Park, she bought eggs, a sack of rice, and the cheapest coffee from the bodega where the owner always called her profesora even when she came in looking like she had slept in a stairwell. Total came to eighteen dollars and some change. She paid with debit and felt the machine think before approving.

Up in her sublet, she set the groceries on the counter, took off her wet socks, and spread the suspension packet across the kitchen table under the one good lamp. The paper smelled faintly of toner and the museum's citrus hand soap. She used a red pen to circle every phrase that translated into money, then wrote the actual body-cost beside it.

`Undisbursed balance subject to immediate freeze: $3,000.`  
That amount would have covered three weeks of rent and then some, and now it was gone.

`Archive credentials suspended.`  
She had no lab access, no logs, and no witness chain.

`Public communications embargo until determination.`  
Silence was the condition of maybe.

`Failure to comply may result in termination for cause.`  
That word, cause, functioned as both weapon and stain on every future application.

She read until language flattened into noise, then lay her forehead on the table and listened to the upstairs radiator start hammering again. It sounded like knuckles on a locked door.

At 6:40, Luis called for their scheduled check-in. She let it ring twice before answering.

"Hey," he said. Traffic hissed behind him, dispatcher office noise and somebody cursing about a route delay. "You make it through?"

"Depends what through means."

"Means you didn't drink in the museum bathroom and you're still breathing."

"Then yes."

He was quiet for a second, then: "You ate?"

"Eggs in ten minutes."

"Good. Give me concrete."

Marisol looked at the pages spread on the table like autopsy photos.

"They froze three thousand. Badge dead. Ada got HR'd into a corner. She sent one text."

"What text."

"No calls or seeing each other for seven days. Night check-in by text only."

Another pause, shorter.

"That hurts," Luis said.

"Brilliant analysis."

"You called me, so you get analysis."

She rubbed the burn scar on her left wrist with her thumb until the skin warmed. "I didn't call. You called."

"And you picked up. Same difference."

He cleared his throat. "Meeting tonight at seven-thirty. Church basement on Nostrand. You're going."

"I smell like wet dog and lawsuit."

"People have shown up smelling worse."

"I don't want to hear gratitude inventory from men who still have jobs."

"Then don't listen for gratitude. Listen for people not dying."

Her laugh came out cracked. "You can't sponsor me through contract law, Lou."

"Nope. I can sponsor you through Tuesday."

She closed her eyes.

"Okay," she said. "Tuesday."

"Bring the blue chip."

"It's in my hand."

"Good. Eat first. Then shower. Then train."

"You should have been a prison warden."

"Too much paperwork."

He hung up before she could reply. That was his way when he thought more words would turn into excuses.

Marisol stayed at the table another minute, looking at her own annotations, the neat red circles around terms designed to sound neutral while they took away oxygen. She thought about Nora saying I think you published before you could protect yourself. She thought about Ada signing a compliance form with a pencil from her hair. She thought about Genevieve Pratt's gloved hand, rich and shaking, and the line of blue paint under a donor's nail.

Then she stood, cracked two eggs into a pan, and watched the whites seize from clear to opaque. The hiss in the skillet was the closest thing to certainty she had heard all day.

After she ate, she laid out her state ID, a copy of her birth certificate, and the yellow legal pad she had started using as a timeline ledger, then slid the blue day-one chip back into her coat pocket before bed.


# Chapter 11

By morning, with the blue day-one chip still in her coat pocket, Ticket B-214 stuck to Marisol's damp thumb while the waiting-room monitor crawled from B-179 to B-180 and buzzed like a dying fly. She had packed her folder like she used to pack workshop handouts: clear sleeve for her birth certificate, copy of her state ID, notarized letter she had paid twelve dollars for at a shipping store that smelled like toner and nail glue, and the yellow legal pad where she had written every date she knew about Tomas in two columns, "family version" and "paper version," because she did not trust memory anymore. She had crossed out the word truth in her notebook and replaced it with verifiable three times. The third crossing had gone through the page.

The municipal records building sat in the rain like a block of wet limestone. People lined up under a shallow awning, shoulders curled against the wind, holding envelopes with bent edges and plastic shopping bags full of old papers. A woman in a red puffer coat had her father's army photo in a freezer bag. A man with two toddlers kept checking the online appointment page on his cracked phone even though the sign on the door said WALK-INS WELCOME, DELAYS LIKELY in block capitals that made likely look like a threat.

Marisol joined the line and counted the money in her coat pocket without taking it out. She had forty-eight dollars in cash. Her debit card had eighty-nine and some change until the copy-shop check cleared on Friday. Certification fees were listed online with the kind of fake clarity that turned into surprise as soon as you reached a window. She had been living inside surprise for two weeks: stipend frozen, badge dead, text from payroll with bullet points about "disbursement review," her landlord's voicemail she kept deleting without listening all the way through.

Security opened the glass doors at eight-thirty and started feeding people in six at a time. The guard at the magnetometer was a broad man with nicotine-yellow fingertips and a tiny radio clipped to his shoulder that played bachata between dispatch calls. His name tag said "R. CEDENO", and he wore a hand-knit scarf in Mets orange and blue that had a loose end he kept tucking back into his collar with patient irritation.

"Folder on the belt, phone in the tray," he said. His voice was rough but not unfriendly. "No coffee past this point. Whoever sneaked coffee in yesterday, you made enemies."

A few people laughed. Nobody admitted it.

Marisol put her bag through the scanner. The tray came out jammed under her tote handle, and Cedeno knocked it loose with the side of his fist, then pointed her toward a kiosk with tickets curling out in pale blue strips. She took number B-214. The monitor above the waiting room showed B-179 in green digits and buzzed every time it advanced, a tired electric fly sound that burrowed under her skull.

The room had plastic chairs bolted in rows, a vending machine with one dead fluorescent tube, and a mural of old New York maps behind scratched plexiglass. The air smelled like wet wool, copier heat, and somebody's menthol cough drops. Marisol sat, opened her folder, checked everything again, and watched people stand and sit and stand and sit while the numbers crawled forward in twos and threes. By nine-fifteen she had rewritten Tomas's date of death four times on a scrap page just to keep her hand moving.

At B-203 she texted Luis: "At records office. Long line. Trying for cert + toxicology abstract."

He replied three minutes later: "Eat something when you can. Facts first. Then breathe."

She had two peanut-butter crackers from the bodega and ate them dry, the salt sticking to the roof of her mouth. She stared at the map mural and thought about martyr paintings, the ones with arrows neat as calligraphy and blood arranged like drapery. Tomas had never gotten that kind of composition. He had gotten a story people could live with.

B-214 flashed at 10:02 and sent her to Window 7.

The woman behind Window 7 had a headset pressed crooked over one ear and a pen tucked into her bun. She did not look up while Marisol stepped to the slot.

"Application," the clerk said.

Marisol slid over the form she had printed and filled at home, plus copies of her ID.

The clerk glanced once, flipped the form back, and tapped the top box with a nail cut short and square. "This is DOH-108. Wrong agency for this office."

"Your website linked that PDF from the death records page."

The clerk shrugged without lifting her eyes. "Website links lots of things. We process MVR-41 for certified legacy record pulls."

"Legacy meaning what exactly?"

"Meaning historical municipal records when you need the full legal certificate, not informational printout. Fill this out." She slid a pink carbon form under the glass with enough force that it buckled. "And you need proof of direct relationship plus notarized kinship affidavit. Originals or certified copies. Next."

"I'm his daughter. I brought my birth certificate and an affidavit."

The clerk had already turned toward the next person. "You can attach those to the right form."

Marisol felt heat rise up her neck. "Can I keep this window? I waited almost two hours."

"Ma'am, if you're still at this window when I call next, security will move you."

She stepped aside with the pink form in her hand and the taste of blood from biting the inside of her cheek. The top of MVR-41 read REQUEST FOR CERTIFIED VITAL RECORD - MUNICIPAL ARCHIVE COPY in dense black type. The boxes were narrow, demanding exact dates and borough codes she did not have. Field 11 asked for "Purpose of Request (check one)." The options were "insurance", "pension", "court order", "genealogical", "other (specify)". The instructions below said "genealogical copies are not certified for legal use".

She chose "other" and wrote: "documentation related to contested public record and family legal interests". She hated the sentence, every careful noun.

Field 14 asked for "Cause of Death Access Requested?" with two checkboxes:
"No (suppress medical details)"
"Yes (if authorized)"
She checked yes so hard the carbon copied through three pages.

Then she hit Field 17: "Known Substance Use at Time of Death (if any)." Not required, the form said, but "may aid retrieval in accidental toxicity cases." There were tiny boxes for "alcohol", "opiates", "stimulants", "unknown", and a line marked "habitual abuse noted in source record: Y/N".

She stared at habitual abuse noted until the words blurred.

At first she thought she had misread. She brought the form close to the fluorescent light and read it again, each letter steady and mean. The phrase habitual abuse sat there like a verdict. It was built to flatten a person into a pattern, to make a whole life fit a checkbox. It made Tomas sound like a stain you circled in red.

Marisol took a breath, stood, and got back in the line for Window 7. There were ten people ahead of her at first, and then there were twelve. The monitor clicked B-221, B-222. When her turn finally came, a different clerk sat there.

Her nameplate read "IMELDA CRUZ". She looked mid-fifties, with dark lipstick gone at the center, silver hoops, and a navy cardigan with one elbow patched in black thread. At the edge of her desk sat a small plastic dinosaur with a chipped tail, neon green. Every time she stamped a page, the dinosaur jumped a millimeter.

"What do you need?" Imelda asked.

Marisol slid the form under the glass. "I need a certified death certificate with cause of death and informant line visible. Tomas Vega. I've got relationship proof. I also need whatever municipal abstract lists witness statements tied to the death event."

Imelda scanned the form, then looked up directly for the first time any clerk had all morning. Her eyes were ringed with blue pencil liner that had smudged at the corners, as if she had rubbed them while reading too many screens.

"You filled most of this right," she said. "Line 6B needs requestor status code, not \"relationship: daughter,\" and you left borough code blank."

"I don't know where he died."

"Then write UNK and give me probable jurisdiction. If you leave it empty, system rejects automatically."

Marisol crossed out daughter in 6B, wrote the status code Imelda recited, then wrote UNK in block letters.

Imelda tapped Field 17 with her pen. "You can leave this blank."

"Why is it on the form?"

Imelda gave a short breath that might have been a laugh if it had less exhaustion in it. "Because this template is older than my son. Because nobody deletes old questions. Because forms survive administrations. Pick any reason and it will be true."

Marisol kept her voice measured. "It says habitual abuse."

"I saw it." Imelda lowered her tone. "You don't have to dignify that line. Leave it empty."

She did, then handed back the packet.

Imelda sorted the pages into two stacks with fast square motions. "Kinship affidavit is notarized by a shipping store notary. That's legal, but this office requires county seal when the request includes medical-detail release."

"What does that mean in practice?"

"It means you need to walk to Room 318, County Clerk satellite desk, get your affidavit authentication stamp. Fifteen-dollar fee. Bring it back before three and I can keep your intake date today. If you miss three, it rolls to tomorrow, and this receipt expires at close. You'd pay the search fee again."

Fifteen plus thirty-eight put her one missed bus fare away from rationing dinner again. She heard rent in every number.

Marisol blinked. "Can I pay all fees now and hold my place?"

Imelda looked past her at the line, then back to her file, then at Marisol's face with the slow assessment of someone deciding whether effort would be wasted. "Cashier is Window 2. Pay search fee now, thirty-eight dollars. Keep receipt. Don't lose it." She stamped the top form with RECEIVED-PENDING in red ink. "Take this stamped sheet upstairs. Show them this and they should move you ahead of walk-ins."

"Should?"

Imelda lifted one shoulder. "I don't control upstairs. I control this stamp."

She slid the packet back. Her ring finger had a deep groove where a wider ring used to sit. The skin there was pale compared to the rest of her hand.

Marisol moved fast. Window 2 ate twelve minutes while the receipt printer stalled and restarted. She paid thirty-eight in cash, watched the clerk count the bills twice, and got a thermal-paper receipt thin as onion skin. She folded it into the clear sleeve with her birth certificate and took the elevator to 3.

She touched the sleeve through her tote twice before the elevator doors opened, checking for the receipt the way she checked for her keys after blackout years.

Room 318 was half office, half hallway overflow. A handwritten sign on the door said "AUTHENTICATIONS - BACK IN 15" even though someone was behind the desk. Marisol waited through an apostille argument before the clerk, a young man with a fresh barber line and a pink Hello Kitty mug, waved her up.

"County seal for municipal records release affidavit," she said, already extending the paper.

He checked the notary block, stamped a blue square seal at the bottom, and slid a payment slip under her hand. "Card or cash."

"Card."

The machine failed twice before accepting. At 1:27 it finally approved, and the release in her chest was so sharp it almost felt like grief.

The elevator down was out of service. She took the stairs, four flights in wet boots, knees burning by the last landing. At Window 7 the line had swollen again. She thought about stepping out for coffee when her hands started to tremble, checked the clock, and stayed put. Missing Imelda's side-wall signal meant losing today's intake and burning another thirty-eight dollars she did not have in cash. Imelda caught her eye from behind another customer's shoulder and lifted two fingers without smiling. Marisol waited by the wall while three numbers passed. At 1:51 Imelda called, "B-214 return."

Marisol stepped up and slid the county-sealed affidavit and receipt under the glass.

Imelda checked each page, then entered data into a terminal that looked old enough to have voted in the nineties. Green text crawled across black. She typed with two fingers, paused, typed again.

"System found three Tomas Vegas," she said. "One Bronx, one Queens, one Brooklyn. Give me date range."

Marisol opened her legal pad. "I inherited two dates. At church we marked June 1992 every year like that was when he died. At home everyone said November 1992. The only hard anchor I've got is a museum handling log that puts him alive in November, so June reads like memorial drift, not death date. I need October through December to catch month swaps and late filing."

"It sounds like family." Imelda clicked a field. "Date range?"

"October first to December thirty-first, 1992."

Imelda entered it. "Mother's name?"

"Gloria Vega. Maiden name Morales."

"Father?"

Marisol held still. "Tomas Vega."

Imelda nodded, eyes on screen. "You want cause-of-death field unsuppressed. That requires Section C release acknowledgment. Sign here, here, and here."

There were three signature lines. Marisol signed all three in the cramped space, ink catching on the paper grain. The second line required initials next to language that read "Requester understands medical terms may be graphic, stigmatizing, or outdated in archival records." She initialed anyway.

Imelda printed a search pull request and fed it to a fax machine with a curled cord. "Archive stack is off-site in Long Island City. Normal pull time is ten business days. Same-day only happens under Administrative Rule AR-17: documented administrative harm, active response window, and a record that's already in the pilot index."

Marisol felt the room tilt a degree. "My residency got suspended. Badge deactivated, stipend frozen, and counsel wants a documentary response this week before review. Does that meet AR-17?"

"If you can document it."

Marisol slid over the suspension notice printout and payroll email she had folded into the back sleeve. Imelda scanned both, lips moving once over a date.

"You said residency. Museum?"

"Brooklyn Museum."

Something crossed Imelda's face, quick as a shadow. "My niece interns there on weekends. She was told not to say your name this week. That's gossip, not criteria."

Marisol's stomach dropped anyway.

Imelda leaned back, stared at the dinosaur, then turned to the terminal. "Criteria are AR-17 plus index availability. It's not guaranteed. It's not a favor. It's a procedural category most clerks avoid because it creates extra audit work."

"What do you need from me?"

"One paragraph, handwritten, describing immediate harm from delay. No poetry. Dates, consequences, plain language."

Marisol almost laughed at the no poetry but swallowed it. "I can do plain language."

Imelda pushed a ruled sheet through the slot. "Print, don't script. Attach to your signed acknowledgment."

Marisol wrote: "On November 29, 2026, my residency stipend and archive badge were suspended by Brooklyn Museum leadership after public dispute related to the circumstances of Tomas Vega's death. I need certified municipal records to verify cause of death and informant identity for pending legal and contractual responses. Delay prevents correction of false public and family record and may cause additional financial harm." She kept adjectives out. She kept rage out. She printed each letter like a first grader.

Imelda read it once, stapled it to the form, and keyed in another code. The printer at her elbow spit out a slip marked "EXPEDITE - ADMIN HARM REVIEW".

"Take this to Records Pull desk, Room 112. Give it to Ms. Chao only. If she's at lunch, wait. Don't hand it to anyone else."

"Why only her?"

"Because she actually reads what she signs." Imelda stamped the slip and pushed it over. "And because I said so."

Room 112 had no windows and two humming scanners the size of washing machines. Boxes of bound ledgers sat on steel carts tagged with barcodes. A woman in a gray turtleneck and fingerless gloves looked up from a stack of index cards.

"Ms. Chao?" Marisol asked.

The woman nodded, took the expedite slip, and read it without expression. Her hair was pinned with two chopsticks, one plain wood and one bright plastic with a little cat face. She circled a code with red pencil and pointed to a bench.

"Sit. We check pilot index first. If no digital mirror, same-day isn't possible."

Marisol sat on the bench and listened to scanner fans and the slap of rubber stamp pads. On the opposite wall a whiteboard listed pull times:
"Standard: 7-10 business days"
"Certified with medical release: 10-15"
"Expedite (approved): same day if indexed"
Someone had added in marker under that: "if the moon cooperates".

At 2:34 Ms. Chao called her name.

"We found one matched certificate. Brooklyn jurisdiction. Date of death November 12, 1992." She did not soften the next sentence. "Cause field is present. Informant field is present. You still want full release?"

"Yes."

Ms. Chao studied her for a beat, then nodded. "Sign final acknowledgment. You'll receive one certified copy and one municipal incident abstract because your request listed witness linkage."

She signed again.

The printing process took almost twenty minutes. First came a plain paper preview that Ms. Chao reviewed with a ruler, line by line, checking that each field aligned with template boxes. Then a heavier cream sheet fed through a different printer and came out with a faint watermark you could only see when tilted. Ms. Chao pressed a raised seal at the lower right corner, then ran a narrow blue ribbon through two punched holes and crimped it with a metal eyelet.

When she slid the document toward Marisol, Marisol did not touch it right away. The paper lay under plexi like an insect pinned for school biology.

"Name of Decedent: VEGA, TOMAS A."
"Date of Death: 11/12/1992"
"Age: 38"
"Manner of Death: ACCIDENT"
"Immediate Cause: ACUTE INTOXICATION - FENTANYL AND HEROIN"
"Contributing Conditions: chronic opioid dependence"

The old term was still there in a notation box on the incident abstract:
"known narcotics abuser per collateral witness"
Typewriter font, all caps where the typebars had hit harder.

Marisol felt the back of her tongue flood with the metallic saliva she got before she cried or threw up. She swallowed and read the lines again, slower, because the first read had been pure impact. The certificate said acute intoxication. It did not say martyrdom. It did not say anything noble. It implied needles and dope and a motel room and a body that stopped because chemistry did not care what story came later.

Ms. Chao tapped the lower half of the incident abstract. "Witness line is here."

Marisol tracked down with her finger.

"Witness/Informant: RIVAS, SALVADOR E."
"Address: 199 Prospect Park West, Brooklyn, NY"
"Relationship to decedent: friend/employer"

The friend/employer slash looked obscene in its neatness.

She had expected a cop, a paramedic, maybe an anonymous desk sergeant. She had not expected Salvador's full name, full address, typed by someone who had never met any of them and still preserved the most intimate betrayal in municipal ink.

Her knees went loose. She sat back on the bench because standing suddenly felt ambitious.

Ms. Chao waited, not unkindly. "Do you need water?"

Marisol shook her head.

"You can request amendment language review if archival terminology is offensive," Ms. Chao said. "It doesn't alter originals. It appends modern terminology notice."

"How long does that take?"

"Usually six to eight weeks."

Marisol gave one dry laugh. "Of course."

Ms. Chao held out a second sheet. "Given the wording note, I'm attaching the current language notice template now. It states that terms reflect period usage and are not endorsed by current department standards. It won't change the document, but it gives context if you're presenting this packet."

"Thank you."

"It's routine." Ms. Chao's mouth tightened at one corner. "It should have been routine before you had to ask."

Marisol took the packet with both hands. The seal was still warm from the press, a faint heat against her fingertips. Paper had weight she could feel in her forearms, absurd and real. She could take this to a lawyer. She could lay it on Gloria's table and point. She could mail a copy to museum counsel and make them answer nouns instead of vibes and donor panic.

None of it made her less alone in that room.

She got back to Window 7 to close intake at 3:18, and the waiting room had thinned into end-of-day silence. Imelda was stacking forms in color-coded bins. She took one look at Marisol's sealed packet and nodded once, like an umpire calling a strike.

"You got it."

"I got it."

Imelda handed over a final receipt with a barcode and clerk code "IC-77". "Keep that with the certificate. If anyone challenges chain of custody, this ties pull, payment, and release."

Marisol tucked it into the sleeve.

Imelda hesitated, then opened a desk drawer and pulled out a tiny tube of hand cream, the cheap unscented kind from drugstores. She rubbed some into the raw skin at the base of her thumbs before speaking again. "My brother died in 2004. Different paperwork, same vocabulary. I filed amendment language notice because my mother couldn't stand seeing "habitual" on anything with his name. It did not fix grief. It fixed one corner of the room."

Marisol met her eyes. "That matters."

"It did to my mother." Imelda capped the tube and slid it away. "Go eat before you read it again."

Marisol almost said she had no appetite, no money for more than a coffee, no idea where she would put all this anger tonight without drinking. Instead she said, "Thank you for the workaround."

Imelda picked up the dinosaur and moved it to the top of the monitor, where it looked like it was climbing. "I gave you a code path you already qualified for. That's not the same thing as mercy."

"Okay."

"Still." Imelda gave the briefest smile. "Use it well."

Outside, the rain had stopped but the sidewalks shone black under a lowering sky. Marisol stood under the awning and checked the seal once, then walked two blocks to a copy shop near Borough Hall. She spent twenty-two dollars on duplicates and fresh clear sleeves, keeping the originals flat in the middle of her tote between notebook and sweater so they would not bend. The city smelled like wet metal and exhaust and roasted nuts from a cart on the corner.

She walked to the station and did not open the packet on the platform even though every cell in her body wanted to. She waited until she had a seat on the train, knees braced against the opposite bench, and then looked again at the witness line until the letters stopped jumping.

"RIVAS, SALVADOR E."

The train doors chimed. A teenager across from her was doing algebra homework on his phone with one earbud in, mouthing numbers to himself. Two construction workers argued softly about whether the Knicks would trade anyone before February. Their voices blurred into rail squeal and station announcements while Marisol held the document flat on her lap like she was handling a thin pane of glass that could cut either way.

She typed a message to Ada:
"I got certified records. It's worse and clearer."
She stared at it, then deleted it before she could decide whether contact counted as help or harm under the museum order.

She called Luis instead. He picked up on the second ring, background noise full of dispatch radios and someone laughing too loudly.

"You good?" he asked.

"I got the certificate and incident abstract."

"You sitting down?"

"On the train."

"Read me the facts. No speech."

She looked at the page and forced her voice steady. "Date of death November twelve, ninety-two. Manner of death: accident. Immediate cause: acute intoxication, fentanyl and heroin. Witness slash informant: Salvador Rivas, full address listed. Relationship marked friend-employer."

Luis let out air through his teeth. "Okay. That's hard fact."

"It feels like a weapon."

"It's a document. You decide how to use it."

She watched her reflection in the dark window between stations, her face doubled over the text. "I thought getting proof would make me less crazy."

"It might make you harder to gaslight. That's not the same as feeling better."

She pressed her thumb against the raised seal through the plastic. "I can go to Gloria tonight."

"You can. Should you?"

She closed her eyes. Her body wanted speed, confrontation, relief, the old burn and then the quiet. Under that she could feel a smaller, steadier thing: the part of her that counted days and knew that urgency was where she broke.

"I don't know."

"Then don't make the biggest call on an empty stomach and a flooded brain. Get off at Atlantic. Eat something hot. Go to seven-thirty meeting in Park Slope. Bring copies tomorrow, not tonight."

She hated how reasonable he sounded. "You always make it sound like transit schedules."

"Because transit schedules keep people alive. And because you listen when I talk like that."

A pause. Dispatch squawked his name in the background.

He lowered his voice. "You did the work today. Don't throw it away by chasing immediate relief."

She swallowed. "I won't."

"You want me to meet you at the meeting?"

"No. I can do it."

"Call after."

The line clicked off.

At Atlantic Avenue she stood on the platform while warm air gusted up from the tunnel. Her hands were shaking now that the objective was complete. Not from craving exactly, though craving sat nearby like a neighbor's dog barking through the wall. From the drop after sustained effort, from the way the nervous system punished you for surviving the task.

She bought a bowl of noodle soup from the Korean place near the station and ate half of it standing at a steel counter because seats were full. The broth tasted mostly of salt and scallion. Her stomach unclenched enough to make room for thought.

When she reached her sublet in Sunset Park, she set the packet on the table and built a second one from the Borough Hall duplicates: copy of certificate, copy of incident abstract, chain-of-custody receipt, and the language-note addendum. She labeled each sleeve in black marker:
"A - Death Cert (Certified)"
"B - Incident Abstract (Witness)"
"C - Retrieval Receipts"
"D - Terminology Notice"

She made a third packet with red tabs for "family," then stopped before filling it. She was not ready to decide which evidence to weaponize and which to carry like confession.

Outside the window, somebody in the next building practiced trumpet scales badly, stopping every few bars to curse and start again. Marisol sat at the table with the sealed envelope and listened to him fail at the same measure until he got it clean. Then he ran it once more, softer.

At seven-ten she put the documents back in her tote, zipped it, and touched the blue day-one chip in the side pocket for no reason except contact. She wanted metal, paper, skin, and facts with edges.

In the church basement meeting room, folding chairs scraped linoleum and burnt coffee steamed in a stained urn. Marisol took a seat near the back and kept her bag looped around her ankle. When it was her turn she did not narrate the whole day, did not turn municipal forms into story. She said, "I spent six hours getting records that proved my family lied about how my father died. I thought I'd feel vindicated. I mostly feel alone and wired. I'm not drinking tonight."

A woman across the circle in a denim jacket nodded once. "Good. Keep coming."

A man near the coffee urn, maybe fifty, Mets cap with a sweat-dark brim, leaned forward. "Keep coming, sure. But don't confuse being right with being safe. I went out over custody paperwork because I needed to win the argument more than I needed to stay sober."

The line hit like a slap. Marisol felt her jaw lock.

The chair looked at her. "What's your plan for the next two hours?"

She almost said she'd be fine. Instead she heard herself answer, "I'm going straight home, no detours. Tea, shower, call my sponsor before bed. And can someone hang on to my bag while we stack chairs? If I keep rereading those pages tonight, I'm going to spin out."

The woman in denim held out her hand. "I got you. You ask me for it when you're ready to leave."

When Marisol let the strap slide off her ankle, the pulse in her throat dropped half a notch. She stayed for cleanup, stacking metal chairs until her hands stopped shaking hard enough to rattle them.

After the meeting, people traded leftovers from a box of supermarket cookies. Marisol stepped into the cold with the packet under her arm and the city opening around her in wet light and bus brakes and sirens two blocks over.

Proof sat in her bag like ballast. It was leverage, finally, something that could not be argued away at a dinner table or in a donor-safe conference room.

It did not call anyone home.
Three days later, she'd carry it to 2B anyway.


# Chapter 12

Three days later, Marisol stood outside 2B with the folder braced against the hallway wall, clear sleeves already arranged: death certificate, toxicology summary, incident report with witness signature, shift log, motel invoice. She wanted the first thing in the room to be paper, not another speech.

Certified copy paper had a different weight from printer paper. Thicker, powdery, official in the hand. She had learned that in Municipal Record Room three days earlier, while a clerk named Imelda watched her fill out line 6B again because she had put “relationship: daughter” where the form wanted “requestor status code.” The clerk had slid the corrected form back and said, almost kindly, “Don’t fold these. If they crease they look tampered.”

She had not folded them. She had spent twenty-two dollars at a copy shop near Borough Hall to make duplicates and keep the originals in clear sleeves. She had highlighted nothing. She had promised Luis on the phone she would not turn evidence into performance. “Read them straight,” he had said. “No speech. Let the words do the cutting.”

The lobby light was out again. On the second-floor landing, somebody had taped a notice about package theft over an older notice about rent increase, both curling at the corners from steam.

She listened first. Pot lid clatter, Leo’s voice trying to remember the planets, Carina saying, “Start over and breathe.” Gloria’s rosary beads clicked once against glass. That house sound, all of them layered, made Marisol press two fingers against her sternum and count to four before she knocked.

She knocked.

Carina opened the door with a dish towel over one shoulder and a school district lanyard still around her neck. She had changed out of office shoes into socks with little basketballs on them, but she was still in the navy slacks she wore to P.S. 261. Her chipped front tooth flashed when she inhaled through her mouth, not a smile.

“You said seven,” Carina said.

“It’s six forty-eight.”

“You’re keeping minutes now?”

“I brought the documents.”

Carina looked past her toward the stairwell and then back at the folder under her arm, the clear sleeves catching hallway light. “Come in. Wipe your boots. Leo, shoes off the table.”

Leo sat at the kitchen end of the table with his science folder open and a glue stick cap between his teeth. He had drawn three pigeons in pencil margins, each with one leg banded in red marker. Next to his notebook was a jar lid full of sunflower seeds. He had started feeding pigeons off the fire escape and had begun assigning each one attendance marks like they were students. He was nine and took inventory the way accountants and children did: with zeal and no mercy.

He glanced up when Marisol came in. “Tia Mari, I got one to land on my hand yesterday, but Mama said don’t tell Abuela because she thinks they got diseases.”

“Everything’s got diseases,” Gloria said from the stove. “That is why God made soap. Wash your hand again.”

Gloria stood with her weight tilted off her right knee, stirring a dented pot of arroz con gandules with one hand and pressing the other into the small of her back. Her hair was pinned into the same tight knot she wore even when she slept. Two prayer candles burned at the corner shelf: Sacred Heart and Virgen de la Caridad, wax rivers down both sides. The apartment smelled of sofrito, café bustelo reheated too many times, and hot wax.

Marisol put her tote down and set the folder on the table, not the edge where spills happened, the center where homework happened and bills got sorted and Leo lost his baby teeth. “I need both of you to sit,” she said.

Carina snorted without humor. “You need?”

“I asked for tonight because I’m done doing this in hallways.”

Gloria did not turn around. “If this is about accusing, you can accuse after Leo eats.”

“It is about evidence.”

“Everything is evidence with you now.”

Leo looked between faces, alert in the way children got when the weather changed indoors. “Am I in trouble?”

“No, mi cielo,” Carina said at once. “Eat your chicken.”

He stabbed a piece, chewed, and kept watching.

Marisol pulled out three sleeves and laid them side by side. Left to right: death certificate, toxicology summary, incident report with witness signature. Then she added the Red Hook shift log copy, the one with Tomas’s name and clock-out time the night before he was supposed to have died at a protest uptown. She laid a fifth sheet, motel invoice with date and room number, because she had found it in Gloria’s old accordion folder when she came for laundry last month. She had not told Gloria she had copied it.

Paper spread across plastic tablecloth printed with faded oranges. An exhibit, yes, but also impossible to ignore.

Gloria finally turned off the burner and came to the table, wiping her hand on her apron. Carina remained standing, one palm on Leo’s shoulder. Her thumb moved back and forth over the seam of his T-shirt without her seeming to notice.

Marisol tapped the first page. “Certificate number 26-11-78403. Cause of death: acute combined fentanyl and heroin toxicity. Location: Harbor Motor Lodge, room 214. Date: November 12, 1992.”

The year scraped through her on the way out. Thirty-four years of family story, and the number on the page did not blink.

Leo stopped chewing.

Carina said, “Not in front of him.”

“He hears all of it anyway.”

“Then give him one thing this house doesn’t do: a filter.”

For half a beat, Marisol wanted one too. She wanted Leo to keep one clean shelf in his head for another year.

Gloria leaned over the table and read the heading without touching the page. Her lips moved around the English words before sound came. “You went to get this certified.”

“Yes.”

“You paid for this.”

“Yes.”

“With what money?”

The question landed like a slap. Even now Gloria could move the argument from death to bookkeeping in one sentence.

Marisol stared at her. “That’s what you took from this?”

“I asked a question.”

“Copy shop shift. Credit card. Doesn’t matter.” She moved to the third page. “Witness line. Read it.”

Gloria did not.

Carina did. She bent from the waist and read fast, like she read school emails she already knew she would hate. “Witness: Salvador Rivas. Address—” Her jaw tightened. “He was there.”

The speed of it told Marisol Carina was not surprised by drugs, only by the name in ink.

“He signed it.”

Leo pushed his plate away. “Who’s dead?”

The question hit the back of Marisol’s neck like a hand. She had wanted truth; she had dragged timing in by force.

No one answered.

Carina straightened. “Leo, bedroom. Take your folder.”

“I didn’t finish—”

“Now.”

He looked at Marisol once, then grabbed the folder, jar lid, glue stick, and dragged his backpack down the hall. His socks whispered on linoleum. A minute later his door clicked shut. No music started. He was listening.

The room lost its only soft thing.

Gloria lowered herself into a chair with a small breath she could not hide, knee stiff. “You wanted us in one room,” she said. “We’re here. Say what you came to say.”

Marisol sat too, because standing made it feel like court and she was tired of pretending she was judge. “I came because this can't stay in pieces. I needed you and Carina at the same table with the same pages. No more separate stories.”

Carina pulled out the chair opposite and sat hard. “You got your little tribunal. Congratulations.”

“I'm not trying to humiliate anybody.”

“Then stop laying out my father like case files.”

“Our father’s story has been case files since before either of us could read.”

Gloria’s eyes cut to Marisol. “Watch your mouth.”

“Why? Because it sounds ugly? It's ugly.”

Gloria tapped the death certificate once with one finger. “You think this paper tells everything? That one line is a life?”

“No. It tells enough to stop the lie.”

“What lie?” Carina asked, but there was no curiosity in it, only anger putting on a coat.

Marisol looked at her. “Don’t do that.”

Carina held her gaze for a beat, then looked away first. She grabbed the dish towel, folded it into a square, unfolded it again. “Fine. I won’t do that. You want plain? I knew he overdosed.”

Marisol felt the table edge under her palm as if it had tilted. “Since when.”

“Since I was seventeen.”

The heater pipe hissed and went silent.

Carina kept her hands busy with the towel. “Ma told me after I found the motel receipt in the church cookbook. She said if you found out then, you’d blow up your life and maybe ours. She was probably right.”

Marisol laughed once, a dry split sound. “So both of you watched me give readings about him getting beaten by cops. You watched me stand in front of rooms and build him into a saint and you said nothing.”

Carina’s eyes flashed. “You were building him before anybody could stop you.”

“I was thirteen, Carina.”

“And by thirty-four you were still cashing it. Grants, panels, residency, all of it under Vega Daughter of the Martyr.”

Marisol flinched as if slapped. “You think I wanted this?”

“I think you liked being the one with a tragic origin story that got applause.”

“Don’t.”

“You don’t get to ‘don’t’ me. You dragged this into my kitchen while my kid does homework in the next room.”

Gloria raised a hand. “Enough, both.”

Marisol turned to her. “No. Not enough. You don't get to referee after thirty-four years of fabrication.”

Gloria’s voice came soft first, which was always danger. “Fabrication fed you.”

Marisol blinked. “What?”

“You heard me.” Gloria sat straighter, command tone clicking into place. “When Tomas died, he had no legal job papers worth anything, no union death benefit we could claim clean, no insurance, no savings. He had drugs in his blood and a borrowed social number in half his records. You think I walked into an office and told them all that and they handed me help?”

She pointed at the death certificate with two fingers, nails short and squared. “You put this cause of death on benefit forms in 1992 and you get denied. Widow emergency cash? Denied. Rent relief? Denied. Church fund? Maybe not denied but cut in half because now it's ‘his fault.’ Then, when you girls hit school, forms start asking extra questions. Immigration asks why addresses don't match and why signatures don't match and where your husband worked and under what name. One question turns into ten, ten turns into a visit.”

Marisol opened her mouth.

Gloria did not let her. “You were a baby. Carina wasn't even born. I had one knee that already hurt from cleaning twelve floors at Kings County and a landlord who wanted cash by Friday. I did math, not poetry. I did survival math.”

Gloria set both palms on the table. The plastic cloth popped, and Leo's fork jittered against his plate.

Carina stared at Gloria now, not at Marisol. “You told me it was for her. You never said benefits.”

“What good would that do you at seventeen?”

“I wasn’t stupid at seventeen.”

“I know you weren’t. That's why I told you enough, not all.”

Marisol felt heat climb her neck. “You forged protest flyers.”

Gloria shrugged once. “We printed memorial cards with church people. Not flyers.”

“You told me the police beat him and dumped him.”

“I told you a story you could carry to school without getting spit at.”

“No one would spit at me for having a father with addiction.”

Gloria barked a laugh that held no amusement. “You were not in those offices with me. You were not in line at HRA when the woman at window three said, out loud, ‘Another junkie widow.’ You were not at St. Dominic’s when one old man asked if you girls had inherited sickness. Don't tell me what no one would do.”

Marisol looked at the candle shelf because if she looked at Gloria’s face she would either scream or fold. Wax had spilled down and hardened in milky ribs. She remembered being nine, tracing those ridges while Gloria filled out forms at the table, numbers and more numbers, sometimes stopping to shake her cramped hand.

Memory was cruel: it gave you detail too late.

Carina swallowed. “You still let her write poems about state murder.”

Gloria answered without turning. “By then the story had roots. If I yanked it, what did I replace it with? ‘Your father died in a motel with a needle and a witness who disappeared’?”

Marisol said, “His name is here. He didn't disappear.”

She tapped Salvador Rivas’s signature. Ink slanted, old but legible. As she shifted the page, the plastic sleeve nicked her index finger. A bead of blood rose fast. “He told me he was there. He told me other things too.”

Carina narrowed her eyes. “Like what.”

Marisol felt the room tilt again, this time from inside. She had not planned to say it tonight. Luis had said stay with documents, no detours.

“Tuesday, roof garden, eight-seventeen, wind hard enough to shake the light strings,” she said, then stopped and swallowed. “He said there’s a chance he’s my biological father.”

Silence cracked open so hard she heard Leo’s pencil drop in the back room.

Carina stared as if Marisol had switched languages. “What?”

“He said he and Ma were together the spring before I was born. He said Tomas knew there was doubt.”

“I know they were together,” Carina snapped. “That's neighborhood history. He means what, exactly?”

“He means he might be my father.”

Carina looked to Gloria, then back to Marisol, then to Gloria again. “Did you know this?”

Gloria’s face did not move. “I knew there was doubt.”

Carina went very still. “Doubt as in gossip, or doubt as in you believed it?”

“Doubt as in I believed it,” Gloria said. “I never proved it. Tomas knew and told me we were raising you as his. That was the decision.”

Marisol stared at her. The cut on her finger stung where she had smeared blood on the sleeve. “So you finally said it. Out loud.”

Carina pushed her chair back an inch. “You both just dropped paternity into my kitchen with Leo ten feet away.”

“It's not a grenade. It's relevant.”

“It's relevant to my son hearing names through drywall and trying to do planets while this house comes apart.”

Carina flicked two fingers toward the folder when she said “relevant,” like the word itself was greasy.

Marisol rubbed her left wrist over the old burn scar, hard enough to hurt. “You think this is career management?”

“I think everything is content to you now.” Carina’s voice shook and she hated that it shook; Marisol could see her fighting for control the way she fought with jammed copy machines at school, knuckles white on the lid. “You came in with a clear sleeve packet like we're your panel discussion. You say ‘evidence’ every three minutes. You already blew up the museum with that poem, now you’re back for family footage.”

“I didn't record this.”

“Not with a mic. With your head.”

Gloria said quietly, “Carina.”

“No, Ma, let me finish.” Carina turned fully to Marisol, words fast, clipped, whole. “I held your hand after your first detox, remember that? I shaved your legs because you were shaking too hard. I lied to your students and said you had the flu when you were in bed sweating through blankets and cursing at shadows. I did that because you were my sister, not because it would make good pages. I'm not giving you this house to burn for another ‘raw truth’ post.”

Marisol felt the old shame come up like bile, acidic and immediate. She tasted metal. She made herself keep eye contact. “You want my accounting? Fine. I drank. Chapter six of my life, congratulations, everybody got receipts. I lost the workshop. I lost stipend access. I'm paying interest on debt I can't touch. I'm not here because this helps me. I'm here because if we keep lying, Leo grows up under the same script and then we do this all over again with him.”

From the hallway, Leo said, “I can hear you.”

Carina closed her eyes for one second. “Headphones, baby.”

“I forgot where they’re at.”

“Top drawer.”

“Not there.”

Carina stood, went to the hall, and came back with a cracked pair wrapped in blue tape. She handed them through the doorway and waited until his door shut again. When she returned to the table she did not sit.

Gloria’s hands were flat on the plastic cloth. “No one is giving Leo the script you got. I will die before that.”

Marisol said, “Then tell him his grandfather used.”

“At nine?”

“At whatever age he starts asking why grownups whisper.”

Gloria shook her head. “There is truth and there is timing.”

Marisol’s laugh came out as almost a sob and she hated that sound. “You have been saying timing for thirty-four years.”

Gloria’s eyes flashed wet for the first time. “Because there was never a safe time. There was only less dangerous and more dangerous.”

She took a breath and went on, slower, each clause set down like plates. “I did not lie because I liked drama. I lied because paperwork wanted clean widows and clean children, because immigration wanted names that matched and I had none that matched, because landlords wanted no police in their buildings, because schools asked no questions if you said martyr and looked them in the eye. That word opened doors. Overdose closed them.”

Marisol said, “And what did that word do to me.”

Gloria looked at her, long enough that Marisol had to stop tapping her thumb against the folder edge. “It gave you a father you could love without flinching until you were strong enough to flinch.”

The sentence was almost beautiful, which made Marisol angrier.

“You don't get to call this love and walk away clean,” Marisol said.

“I'm not clean.” Gloria pushed up her sleeve and showed the dented white line near her elbow where blood draws had scarred. “I worked nights mopping chemo vomit at the hospital and came home to two girls and one story that held. I held with it. That's not clean. That's work.”

Carina looked exhausted all at once, as if the argument had physically climbed onto her back. She picked up the toxicology page, read two lines, put it down. “I can’t do this with Leo here,” she said.

“Then send him to Mrs. Colon downstairs,” Marisol said.

“It's a school night.”

“It's also the rest of our lives.”

Carina gave her a look sharp as broken glass. “Everything is apocalypse with you.”

“And with you everything is logistics so you never have to pick a side.”

Carina’s mouth opened, closed. She laughed once, the old cutting-joke laugh with no joke inside. “You think I don't pick sides? I pick Leo eating breakfast. I pick lights staying on. I pick Ma not limping to a second shift because you needed catharsis at dinner.”

Marisol shoved the folder away from her, papers sliding two inches across plastic. “I'm not asking for catharsis. I'm asking you to stop helping her keep this alive.”

Carina leaned over the table, both palms down. “You don't get to tell me what helping looks like. You disappear for weeks into museum drama and lovers and meetings and then storm in here with documents like subpoenas. You're not the only person this happened to.”

Marisol stood so fast her chair legs screamed on linoleum. “I know I'm not.”

“Then act like it.”

She realized she was breathing through her mouth, quick shallow pulls that made her dizzy. She forced herself to sit back down. Luis’s voice in her head: Slow is not surrender.

“Okay,” she said, quieter. “Tell me what that looks like.”

Carina blinked, not expecting the drop in volume. “It's not turning every wound into an argument you win.”

“I'm not trying to win. I'm trying not to hand Leo the same fake prayer card we got.”

“You always are.”

“I'm trying to get one honest page in this house before he memorizes the wrong one.”

Carina said, “Survive where?”

Marisol frowned. “What?”

“You heard me. You keep talking like this is a panel. Fine. Practical question. Where are you sleeping tonight, after that paternity bomb with Leo listening through drywall?”

“I've got options.”

“You have debt and a frozen stipend.”

“I've got options.”

Carina’s stare did not move. “Name one.”

Marisol did not answer fast enough.

Carina gave a small nod as if confirming a number she already knew. “Exactly.”

Gloria rubbed her forehead with two fingers. “Nobody is throwing anybody out. We're angry. We'll talk tomorrow.”

Carina turned to her mother. “No. We won’t.”

“Carina.”

“No. I mean it.” Carina faced Marisol again, voice steady now, frighteningly steady. “After what just came out, you can't stay here tonight.”

Gloria sucked in a breath through her teeth. Down the hall, Leo’s bed frame thumped once, then went still.

Marisol stared. “You’re joking.”

“I'm not.”

“Where am I supposed to go, Cari?”

“I don’t know.”

“You don’t know.”

“I don’t. I'm done choosing between my son trying to sleep and this paternity fight eating my whole apartment.”

Gloria half-rose from her chair, winced, sat back. “Carina, enough.”

Carina did not look at her. “Ma, if she stays, this keeps going all night. We’ll still be at this table at midnight arguing who fathered who while Leo pretends to sleep. Tomorrow he has spelling and I’ve got payroll submission and you’ve got Mrs. Kaplan at seven. I can't keep running a paternity hearing and a home in the same twelve feet.”

Marisol felt her heartbeat in her gums. “So that’s it. You eject me and call it boundaries.”

“I'm setting one boundary.”

“Tonight?”

“Yes. Tonight.”

Marisol looked to Gloria. “Say something.”

Gloria’s eyes were red-rimmed and hard at once. “If you both keep shouting, neighbors call landlord. We already got one warning in October. I can't lose this apartment.”

“That's your answer?”

“My answer is everybody breathe.”

Carina said, “My answer is pack.”

Marisol pushed back from the table and stood. Her legs felt boneless. She wanted to throw the folder, tear the pages, drink, run, none of it in that order. Instead she said, “Fine.”

The word came out flat and wrong.

She went down the short hall to the room she used when she crashed there, technically Leo’s second bedroom now, really a storage room with a narrow bed and stacks of church bulletins Carina designed on weekends for extra cash. The suitcase under the bed still had one wheel missing from the summer trip to Queens she had taken and never written about. She dragged it out. Dust streaked her palm.

She packed like someone else was moving her body: three pairs of jeans, two sweaters, underwear rolled tight, notebook, charger, toothbrush, small bottle of ibuprofen, blue day-one chip from the nightstand, AA meeting list folded in quarters. Her hand hovered over the framed photo of Tomas holding toddler Marisol at Orchard Beach. She left it. She took the photocopy instead, thinner, less claim.

In the living room Gloria and Carina were speaking low and fast in Spanish, the way they did when they wanted precision without audience. Marisol caught fragments through the hall: `nene`, `renta`, `mañana`, `por favor`. Leo’s door opened a crack. One eye in the gap.

“Are you leaving for work?” he whispered.

Marisol crouched so they were level. “No, bug. Just for a few days.”

He looked at the suitcase. “Did you and Mama do a fight-fight?”

She almost lied. The lie came easy, muscle memory. She stopped it with effort that made her jaw ache. “Yeah,” she said. “We did.”

“Can I text you my pigeon chart?”

A laugh caught in her throat and turned into something rougher. “Please do. I need updates on every bird criminal.”

He nodded solemnly. “The one with the red band steals from the fat one.”

“Write down the evidence.”

“I always do.”

He closed his door gently.

Marisol stood and lifted the suitcase. The broken wheel made it drag crooked, a scratch-scratch against the hall floor. She carried it the rest of the way.

At the kitchen table the pages were still laid out, damp now at one corner where a glass had sweated. Death certificate, toxicology, witness signature, shift logs, motel receipt. Nothing disputed except what they meant.

Carina had her keys in hand. “I’ll walk you down.”

“Why, you think I’ll steal the silver?”

“We don't have silver.”

“Exactly.”

“Stop.” Carina’s voice cracked for the first time all night. “Please just stop.”

Marisol saw then how much she was shaking, tiny tremors in both hands. Not rage now. Fear and guilt and exhaustion braided tight.

Gloria touched the back of a chair, not sitting, not standing fully. “Take your scarf,” she said to Marisol. “It's twenty-eight degrees.”

The ordinary instruction undid her more than the eviction had. She grabbed the scarf from the hook without trusting herself to answer.

At the door, Carina said, low enough for Gloria not to hear, “Don't call me for a while.”

Marisol looked at her. “How long is a while.”

“I don’t know.”

“Emergency?”

“If Ma’s in the hospital, I’ll call.”

“That's not what I asked.”

Carina swallowed. “No calls. No popping in. No surprise apologies at school pickup. I need quiet. Leo needs quiet.”

The hallway smelled like bleach and somebody’s fried fish. The light buzzed overhead.

Marisol nodded once. “You got it.”

Carina opened the door and stepped back.

On the landing Marisol turned, expecting Gloria to have come out anyway, to press leftovers into her hand or say one of the old goodnight prayers she used to mutter when Marisol came home late from readings. Gloria stayed in the kitchen doorway, one hand on the frame, face unreadable from that distance.

“Ma,” Marisol said.

Gloria answered in Spanish, voice tired, almost formal. “Ve con cuidado.” Go carefully.

Not come back. Not call me. Go carefully.

Marisol carried the suitcase down two flights because the wheel kept catching. Outside, wind came hard between buildings and made her eyes water at once. She stood by the curb and checked her phone.

Battery thirty-one percent.

Bank app: checking balance $46.12.

Credit card available: $112, which was not available, not really.

One text from Luis from an hour earlier: `How'd it go? Keep me posted before bed.`

One from Ada from yesterday, still unanswered: `Thinking of you. No pressure to respond.`

She typed to Luis: `Bad. Got kicked out. No drink.` She erased `No drink` because it sounded like a child asking for credit, then put it back because facts were facts. She hit send.

The suitcase handle pinched the skin between thumb and forefinger. The cold made the metal taste of panic rise again in her mouth.

A cab slowed, saw the suitcase, sped up when she did not raise a hand fast enough.

She called Mr. Han, the Sunset Park landlord. Straight to voicemail. Twenty seconds later his auto-text hit: `Rent past due. Pay $400 tonight or lock changes 10:00 a.m.`

She typed to Ada: `I got put out. Any chance I can crash on your couch tonight?` The message flipped to delivered and sat there. No typing bubbles. No reply.

The bus shelter at the corner had one panel cracked and taped in an X. She rolled the suitcase in, sat on the bench, and pulled her scarf up over her mouth. Two teenagers in puffer coats shared earbuds and laughed at something on a phone. A woman in scrubs counted singles from her wallet twice, then a third time, lips moving with each number.

Marisol took the folder out and checked the pages again by streetlight, as if one might have changed while she was upstairs.

Witness: Salvador Rivas.

Cause: acute combined fentanyl and heroin toxicity.

Room 214.

Facts sat still. People did not.

The bus app said the next B41 southbound would arrive in twelve minutes, then updated to fourteen, then eleven, algorithmic mercy. Marisol opened Notes and typed three words: `No stable housing.` She stared at them until they blurred and added a fourth: `Tonight.`

She put the phone down and pressed thumb and forefinger against the bridge of her nose until stars burst in her vision. She was not drunk. She was not safe. She was not done.

When the bus finally came, she stood, lifted the crooked suitcase, and climbed on, heading south with Mr. Han’s deadline ticking and no clean plan for where she would get off.


# Chapter 13

The sublet in Sunset Park heated itself like a grudging old animal. The radiator clanged twice, hissed, went quiet, then clanged again as if it had changed its mind. Marisol sat at the card table that passed for a desk and tucked her feet under her thighs to keep feeling in her toes. Her one suitcase stood open by the wall where she had left it three nights ago, half unpacked, with a sweater sleeve hanging out like a tongue. The landlord had taped a polite reminder to her door that morning, the kind with a smiley face and an amount underlined twice: 2,400.
Three nights earlier she had gotten off the B41 with that same crooked suitcase, paid Mr. Han four hundred from the sidewalk before the lock deadline, and taken the key before he could change his mind.

She moved the notice under her water glass so she could not look at it while she worked. Then she looked at it anyway.

The draft file on her laptop was called "catalogue_essay_v6_FORREAL.docx," because by version four she had started naming files like dares. The first paragraph still punched hard and false.

Salvador Rivas built a cathedral out of stolen grief.

She read it aloud into the room and heard exactly what Luis would hear: theater, heat, no risk. The sentence let her stand outside the blast radius. It turned him into a cartoon priest with blood on his hands and turned her into the clean witness who had arrived just in time with a flashlight. She knew better than that by now. Cathedral went first, then stolen, then the whole first line, and she sat with the white space.

Outside, a B63 bus exhaled at the corner. A delivery guy swore at a frozen lock and kicked it until the metal gave him an answer. The room smelled like stale coffee, damp wool, and the cheap lemon cleaner the super used in hallways when he wanted to seem diligent. She pressed her palm to the burn scar on her left wrist, the old reflex when she needed to keep from pacing, and opened three windows side by side: death certificate, Red Hook shift logs, and the transcript she had made from the roof garden tape before counsel started sending warnings.

She had always pretended she wrote by ear, by accident, by weather. That was the mythology she could sell to students who wanted permission to wait for lightning. The truth sat in front of her now: timestamps, columns, and choices that made enemies.

By late morning she printed the draft at the corner copy shop where she still picked up two shifts a week. She used her employee code and watched thirty-one pages crawl out of the machine, her own words looking more expensive than her bank account. Back upstairs she taped page one to the wall and page two under it. A thick line down each sheet split the columns she labeled "Documented" and "Claimed."

Line by line, she put every sentence on trial.

When she wrote, "My mother weaponized holiness," she wrote a question mark in red and asked herself, Where is the document for intention. There was none. There were only actions and consequences: a forged narrative, twenty-eight years of repetition, a daughter built inside a lie sturdy enough to survive school forms and church anniversaries. She crossed out weaponized and replaced it with repeated.

When she wrote, "Tomas chose heroin over us," she felt her throat close on the cruelty of the sentence and the accuracy of the drug. The certificate did not contain the word chose. It held the colder phrase acute combined fentanyl and heroin toxicity, typed in municipal black ink above a signature block that did not shake. She wrote that phrase into the essay exactly as printed. Then she added the room she did know: motel carpet, fluorescent bathroom light, a man she loved and never met alive, and the witness line where Salvador Rivas had written his full name and home address in careful cursive.

She left the paternity question in brackets for now.

By noon her neck ached from leaning forward and her right thumb was gray with graphite. She shut the laptop, pulled on her coat, and walked to the church basement on Fourth Avenue where the noon meeting met in a room that was always one degree too hot. Her socks were still damp from yesterday's slush. Folding chairs squealed against linoleum. The coffee on the back table smelled burnt enough to count as ritual. Someone had put out off-brand cookies that tasted like powdered drywall.

Luis was already there, heavy coat unzipped, transit uniform layered underneath: navy fleece, reflective stripe, MTA patch rubbed soft at the edges. He moved with the careful hitch in his left leg that got worse in cold weather, and he stirred sugar into his coffee with the rounded end of a plastic knife because he said the thin stirrers snapped like promises. When he saw her, he tapped the chair beside him with two fingers and did not ask how she was. He knew better than that opener when she looked as wired as she felt.

After the meeting, people drifted toward daylight in twos and threes. A man with nicotine fingers kept talking about baseball statistics to nobody in particular. A woman in a chef's coat asked if anyone had a spare MetroCard swipe and got one. Marisol and Luis stayed seated while volunteers stacked chairs around them.

"You brought pages?" he said.

She slid five printed sheets from her tote. "I brought the part that might get me sued."

"Everything gets you sued now. Read me your first paragraph."

She read the current opening, voice low because the room carried sound: "This essay is not a eulogy. It is a record of edits made to survive shame, poverty, and institutions that call myth protection. It concerns one painter, one family, and one dead man whose body was used to purchase dignity after dignity after dignity."

Luis held up a hand. "Stop there. That last part is clean writing, and you know I don't care about clean writing. Whose dignity are you naming?"

"My mother's. Mine. Carina's. Maybe his too."

"Maybe is lazy in a sentence like that. You scared of saying his name?"

"I just said his name in line one."

"No, you said painter. You made him a type. I'm asking if you can write Tomas and leave it there without turning him into your project mascot."

She hated him a little for being right in public fluorescent light. "I can write Tomas."

"Write him like a person then. Not a symbol you inherited, and not a cautionary poster."

She looked at the pages and found the margin note where she had written "DON'T ORNAMENT THE DEAD" in all caps. "I'm trying."

He gave her the look he used when bus drivers blamed traffic for routes they had left late. "Try is not a route. What did you do this morning. Specific."

She answered the way he liked, in sequence. "I cut adjectives that made me sound righteous. I replaced motive claims with actions. I added my relapse as fact, not confession theater."

"Where."

"Page four."

"Read it."

Her mouth went dry. She read anyway. "On November twenty-second, after confronting my mother with freight logs and a death certificate, I bought a half-pint of vodka from a deli on Rogers and drank in a bus shelter while pretending to wait for the B44. I reset my sobriety date the next morning and told my sponsor before noon."

Luis nodded once. "That is closer. Do you see what changed?"

"I put myself in it."

"You put cost in it. Exposure without cost is gossip with better fonts."

She looked up. "You think this is gossip."

"I think you're smart enough to weaponize truth when you're hurt." He leaned back and winced as his knee popped. "I also think you can choose not to. That's why we're still talking."

The volunteers had finished stacking chairs and turned off half the fluorescent panels. The room dropped into a softer buzz.

"Tell me the line you're proudest of," he said.

She pointed. "Here. 'He repainted the witness out of his own canvas and called the surface finished.'"

"Fine line. What is it doing in this paragraph."

"It's the thesis."

"No. It's a punchline." He tapped the paper. "You throw that line, everybody claps for your anger and nobody asks what you owe the people still alive."

She wanted to tell him she owed nothing, that they had spent decades spending her childhood like petty cash. Instead she heard herself say, "What if exposure is the amends."

He took a long breath through his nose. "Sometimes it's. If you stole money and you return the money with interest, that's amends. If you break a thing and tell everybody who broke it, that might be amends too. If you tell the story so you can feel superior, that's performance."

"How do I prove what this is."

"You can't prove motive clean. You can prove method." He folded his hands over the paper, thick fingers scarred at the knuckles from a job he never talked about. "Ask practical questions. Did you verify. Did you include your own damage. Did you cut what you can't support. Did you ask for consent where consent matters. Did you leave room for the dead to stay human."

He pushed the pages back toward her. "I dispatch buses all day. If one stop lies, the whole line bunches and people freeze on corners. You're building a route. Don't fake the stops because you want applause."

She laughed once, harsh and brief. "You always do transit parables when you're mad at me."

"I'm not mad. I'm busy." He stood, favoring his left leg, and shrugged into his coat. "Call me before you send anything. If you dodge that call, I will assume you're drinking or grandstanding."

"I don't love those options."

"Love better options then."

He left her with the burnt coffee smell and the stack of marked pages. She sat another ten minutes, listening to pipes knock inside the church walls, and wrote in the margin of page one: "EXPOSURE IS NOT AMENDS UNLESS YOU PAY."

Back in the sublet she opened a new file named "catalogue_essay_v7_accountability." She kept the old drafts in a folder called "BAD HALOS" so she could not pretend they had never existed. Window light thinned to dishwater gray. Her fingers stiffened in the cold between radiator cycles. She typed in bursts, then stood to tape revised pages over old ones, building a paper sediment on the wall until the original version disappeared under edits.

She cut the self-congratulatory scaffolding first. The sentence about "speaking truth to power" went into the trash. So did "as a daughter of immigrants," which she had been using as shield and credential in the same breath.

Instead of another ethics paragraph, she drafted the parish-hall memory she had kept dodging: Sister Elena pressing a white envelope into Gloria's palm after the martyr speech, the room smelling like pernil and candle wax, Carina staring at her shoes while Marisol said thank you and did not correct a single fact. The envelope paid their gas arrears. By Christmas, the story had hardened into family liturgy.

For the relapse section she forced herself to stay in sequence. The deli clerk unscrewed the half-pint because her hands were shaking too hard for the cap. Vodka hit her cracked lip, sweet then chemical. Fifteen minutes later the warmth flipped to tremor so hard she sloshed liquor down her coat sleeve and missed two calls from Luis she was too ashamed to return.

Under that she named the motive she had kept dressing up: she posted the accusatory poem not only from rage but because she wanted to beat counsel to the story before legal language could lock it down.

As the room tipped toward evening, a subject line from estate counsel flashed across her screen: NOTICE OF PRESERVATION OF CLAIMS. Any use of roof-garden audio beyond already contested excerpts, the email said, would trigger immediate injunctive action. She cursed out loud, opened a new footnote column, and tagged every volatile line to a document number.

The Gloria section fought back. One draft turned her mother into a tyrant with a rosary. Another turned her into a saint of sacrifice. A third balanced both so neatly it sounded like an ethics textbook. She killed all three, opened the legal pad where she had copied Gloria's exact words from the parking lot confession and from the dinner explosion, and underlined the verbs. Not believed, felt, intended. Verbs with weight: forged, told, hid, paid, worked, prayed, lied.

She built the paragraph with those:
My mother did not invent poverty, addiction, or the state's appetite for neat stories. She did choose repetition. She told the martyr version at school assemblies, parish dinners, and family birthdays because each retelling bought us a little social oxygen. The lie became rent currency, scholarship language, and a shield against the shame that follows overdose into every room.

It still sounded like indictment, but not like revenge porn. She kept it.

Her phone rattled across the table. The landlord left a voicemail in English and then Spanish: eight hundred by noon tomorrow or he would start showing the room and change the lock if she was out.
