Marisol checked her balance, swallowed, and sent three hundred anyway. The confirmation email landed instantly; her chest stayed tight.

She reached four pages she did not hate before she remembered to eat. After microwaved rice and eggs swallowed standing up, the count hit seven and a headache throbbed behind her right eye. She reread the section on Salvador until the words blurred.

That section was the hardest because his facts came wrapped in performance. She had tape evidence of his admissions, but she also had his habits: deflect with aesthetics, flirt with remorse, offer one concrete fact only when cornered. If she wrote him as a monster, she lied. If she wrote him as tragic genius, she lied harder.

She went back to the tape transcript and marked every place where he changed tense. Present tense when he described paint, past tense when he described people. She built a paragraph around that fracture.

Salvador Rivas spoke about pigments as if they were organs and about people as if they were weather systems he endured. He called concealment composition. He called revision mercy. In the same interview where he admitted he signed as witness to Tomas Vega's death, he described his own silence as tenderness. The record shows both claims. This essay refuses to separate them for his comfort or mine.

She left the maybe-father line unclosed but stripped the fog: "He also claimed a biological tie to me that is not yet supported by DNA testing, an amended birth certificate, or any sworn filing; at this stage the claim rests on his interview statement and my mother's partial admission." She stared at partial admission until her jaw clenched and she bit the inside of her cheek hard enough to taste blood. Then she flagged the sentence for counsel review and kept drafting.

Not long after, her phone buzzed with a number she did not know. The area code was Manhattan. She let it ring twice before answering.

"Marisol Vega?"

The voice belonged to a woman who sounded tired and brisk at once, the tone of someone who had repeated bad news all week and still bothered to pronounce every name correctly.

"Yes."

"My name is Cora Batista. I'm on Salvador Rivas's palliative team tonight. I got your number from his paper folder, the blue one with your name in Sharpie and three exclamation points." She paused, and Marisol heard a monitor chirp in the background, then the soft clatter of pill cups. "He asked this afternoon whether the poet was coming back. I said I didn't know."

Cora spoke fast but not carelessly, with the slight rasp of a smoker trying to quit. Marisol pictured her before she saw her: scrub top under a cardigan, hair pinned up with whatever was handy, one eye on the clock and one on a patient who could turn on a sentence. Later she would learn the cardigan was bright yellow with two loose threads at the cuff, and that Cora kept a mechanical pencil tucked behind her ear because she did Sunday crossword puzzles during quiet stretches to keep herself awake.

"Is he lucid right now?" Marisol asked.

"For pieces. Not for long. I'm calling because those pieces are getting shorter." Cora lowered her voice, and Marisol heard a door close on the other end. "Mornings are your best shot. Between about eight-fifteen and nine, before pain meds stack. After that he drifts, and sometimes he starts arguing with people who are not in the room."

Marisol sat down hard on the chair edge. "How much time are we talking. Days. Weeks."

"I don't predict dates for families, and I don't predict dates for people who are not technically family. I can tell you trend. Trend is down." Cora exhaled. "He sleeps more, eats less, and forgets where his hands are. Last week he could hold a thread for twenty minutes. Tonight he lost one in four."

Marisol pressed her thumb into the burn scar until it hurt. "I need one more interview."

"Then ask for one, in writing, tonight. Keep your questions tight. No autobiographical orbits. He does best with concrete prompts." Cora made a small sound that could have been a laugh. "Think less memoir, more deposition with feelings."

Despite herself, Marisol smiled. "You're good at this."

"I'm good at triage. Different skill." Cora's voice softened for one beat. "Listen, my kid has a Regents math exam tomorrow and I still haven't figured out how to explain matrices, so I'm not in the mood for poetic delay. Send the questions. I'll put them in his morning folder."

"Thank you."

"Don't thank me yet. He might wake up mean." She cleared her throat. "But he still asks for you. That means something, even if we can't make it noble."

The line clicked off.

Marisol held the dead phone against her ear another second, listening to the absence. Then she opened her email and started a new message addressed to Cora, cc to the general account the nurse gave her, no museum addresses, no curator language.

Subject: "Request for Final Interview Window - Focused Questions Attached"

She typed the first sentence three times before it sounded like a person instead of an applicant.

Thank you for contacting me. I am requesting one final recorded interview with Salvador Rivas in the next available morning lucid window.

She attached a one-page document and forced herself to keep it to six questions.

1. On what date did you first learn Tomas Vega had died, and who notified you.
2. What factual basis do you claim for saying you are my biological father, and who can corroborate it.
3. Why did your name appear as witness on the municipal death record.
4. Who proposed the martyr narrative first, and what specific facts did each person agree to repeat.
5. What material support, if any, did you provide to Gloria Vega and her daughters in exchange for silence.
6. What did you repaint or remove in works that originally referenced Tomas Vega, and what responsibility do you admit now, in your own words, without metaphor.

She stared at question five. It could break everything open or get the interview canceled before sunrise. She left it in.

Before sending, she added one line beneath the list: "I understand fatigue and confusion may limit answers; I prefer short factual responses over polished statements."

She hit send at 9:12 p.m. and felt the old immediate panic after irreversible action. It felt too much like the night of the poem post, the same adrenaline spike, the same urge to check her phone every ten seconds. She put the phone in the sink and turned the faucet on cold for a count of thirty, forcing her hands under the water until the skin went numb.

Then she went back to the essay because dread without labor always turned theatrical in her body.

Before she opened the ending, Luis texted: "You send yet?"
"Sent request. 8:20 a.m. if stable," she wrote back. "I'm sober."
His reply came ten seconds later. "Call me after pages. If you spin tonight, you don't go in there alone."
She set an early alarm labeled CALL LUIS and turned the phone facedown.

The final section had to declare form and motive. She had drafted twelve endings already: one triumphant, one sorrowful, one clinical, one that tried to sound like Baldwin and one that accidentally sounded like a grant report. She opened a blank page and wrote in plain English first: "This is not an attempt to punish one man or absolve one mother. It is an attempt to stop lying in inherited patterns." She replaced punish with expose, then changed it back to punish because that was the truer word for what she had wanted at noon.

She rewrote the relapse paragraph with no rhetorical framing: "I drank on November twenty-second because I wanted speed and anesthesia more than I wanted honesty. The first swallow tasted like mouthwash and pennies. By minute fifteen my hands stopped shaking; by minute twenty they shook harder and I dropped my MetroCard into the gutter. I missed Luis's calls and called that silence privacy until morning proved it was lying." She read the lines aloud and waited for the internal objection that this was self-indulgence. The objection did not come. What came was a quieter fear: that readers would use her relapse as a discount code against every other claim. She wrote that fear into the draft too, one sentence, then cut it. The chapter did not need anticipatory defense.

A little after ten, Cora replied from her phone.

"Received. 8:20am tomorrow if stable. Bring ID. One recorder only. If he tires, we stop."

No greeting, no signature flourish, only terms. Marisol appreciated the clarity so much it made her throat sting.

She set three alarms and kept writing past midnight, shaping transitions, trimming verdict words, checking every assertion against paper evidence. Closer to one, she printed the full draft, fourteen pages now, and spread them across the floor. She crawled from page to page with a pencil, circling any sentence that told the reader what to feel instead of showing the cost. She crossed out nine circles.

On the final pass she hunted soft verbs: was betrayed became learned; was destroyed split into paid and hid and drank and posted; villain narrowed to witness twice removed. She left one sentence ugly on purpose because the polished version lied: "I wanted him to suffer before he died."

That sentence terrified her. It also made the rest of the chapter honest enough to stand.

Near two she saved the document as "catalogue_essay_v8_SUBMIT_after_interview.docx" and exported a PDF copy to guard against panic edits in the morning. She slid the printed stack into a manila envelope with the death certificate copy, the shift log excerpts, and the blue day-one chip she carried like a small hard planet in her pocket.

She did not pray. She sat on the edge of the bed with her coat still on and listened to the radiator knock the same uneven rhythm as before, like someone asking to be let in and then changing their mind.

Tomorrow had an hourly map: Cora's 8:20 window if Salvador stayed lucid, then Church Avenue by ten if she wanted Carina to open the door. Tonight she had a draft that no longer pretended innocence.


# Chapter 14

By the time the B44 finally wheezed to the curb on Church Avenue, Marisol had gone from cold to damp and back to cold again. Slush soaked the edge of her jeans where it licked over her boots, and the paper bag with the clementines had started to sag at one corner. She shifted it to her other hand, felt the hard coin shape of the blue chip in her coat pocket, and looked up at Carina's building like she had to relearn where the front door was.

Carina had texted at seven in the morning.

If you're coming, come by ten. Leo has math and catechism worksheet. Don't be late.

There was no emoji and no sister-softener, just logistics, which for Carina counted as mercy.

She had mapped the morning by the hour before she slept and still landed two minutes late.

A man in a Mets cap let her into the vestibule and held the second door with his shoulder while balancing two laundry baskets. The hallway smelled like bleach over old cooking oil, and then, one floor up, like coffee and candle wax and sofrito that had settled deep into plaster over fifteen years of dinners. Marisol paused outside 3B with her fist lifted, not because she forgot how to knock, but because her body remembered the last time she stood here in muscle and skin before her head caught up. She remembered the table with documents spread out, Carina's hand on the suitcase handle, and Leo standing in the doorway in his dinosaur pajamas, crying without sound.

She knocked anyway.

Carina opened fast, as if she had been waiting behind the door and did not want to look like she had been waiting. She had her hair in a tight ponytail and a pencil shoved through the elastic, and a stack of school forms hugged to her chest under one arm, already striped in pink highlighter. The chipped edge of her front tooth flashed when she said, "You're two minutes late. I'll count that as weather."

"Bus got trapped behind a delivery truck at Flatbush and Caton." Marisol held up the clementines like evidence. "And I brought fruit as a peace treaty."

"Leo only trusts fruit if it has stickers on it."

"Then we're doomed."

Carina stepped aside. "Take your boots off. He mopped because apparently he's grown now."

The apartment looked both exactly the same and slightly rearranged against her absence. Prayer candles crowded the corner shelf, half of them burned low, glass filmed with soot. A coat rack she did not remember leaned by the door under the weight of two kid-size puffer jackets and one church tote. Leo sat cross-legged on the living room rug with a green composition notebook open in front of him, tongue pushed against one corner of his mouth while he colored a pigeon with a red halo.

He had one sock on and one bare foot tucked under his leg. His sweatshirt said BOROUGH BIRDS in cracked white letters. A baseball card sleeve full of MetroCard swipes and glossy pigeon feathers lay beside him like scientific equipment.

"Tia Mari," he said without looking up, "you know this one by the laundromat that drags his wing? He stole half a churro from me and then pecked Diego's shoelace. He's still a good pigeon, though."

Marisol set the clementines on the table and crouched beside him. "What's his name?"

"Saint Kevin."

She blinked. "That's very specific."

Leo finally looked at her, solemn as a little judge. "Saint Kevin protects blackbirds and weird birds. I looked it up on the church tablet. This one is weird." He tapped the drawing. "Also criminal."

Carina snorted from the kitchen before she could stop herself. "Wash your hands, both of you. Math first, then religion sheet, then we deal with your bird conspiracy files."

Marisol stood and peeled off her coat. "I didn't know saints were handling pigeon crime now."

"Budget cuts," Carina said. "Everybody's cross-trained."

The laugh that escaped Marisol surprised her with its own force, and for one clean second Carina laughed too, loud and unguarded, head tipped back the way she used to at seventeen when somebody told a dirty joke in the church parking lot and Gloria was not there yet. It rang off the cabinets and made Leo grin because he liked noise that meant nobody was about to fight.

Then Carina caught herself, looked down at the sink, and the room reset.

"Table," she said. "We're already behind."

They worked through a packet of long division at the kitchen table while the radiator clicked and hissed like it had opinions. Leo wrote every number with a dramatic pause before committing pencil to paper. He wore a cheap plastic digital watch with the face cracked at one corner, and every three minutes he lifted his wrist and announced the time as if he were dispatching trains.

"Ten twenty-one."

"Ten twenty-four."

"Ten twenty-eight, but maybe it's lying."

"Clocks don't lie," Carina said.

Leo drew an X through a wrong answer hard enough to tear paper. "People set them wrong on purpose, though. That's lying by clock."

Marisol took the pencil before he could drill a hole through the worksheet. "Let's just fix number seven. You borrowed from the wrong column."

"I hate borrowing."

"Everybody hates borrowing."

Carina looked over from the sink where she was peeling potatoes into a metal bowl. "Says the woman with twenty-four hundred dollars in rent arrears."

Marisol felt the heat crawl up her throat before the words arrived. "Good morning to you too."

"I'm not insulting you. I'm saying borrowing sucks for everybody."

"You're excellent at tone, you know that?"

"I'm excellent at paying Con Ed before they shut us off." Carina rinsed the knife, wiped her hands on a dish towel, and set a yellow folder in front of Marisol. "Read Leo's writing prompt with him. I've got to answer six parent emails before noon and all of them end with god bless for some reason."

Marisol opened the folder. The prompt said WHAT MAKES A HERO? in thick photocopier ink. Leo had written three lines already in block letters:

A HERO TELLS THE TRUTH.
A HERO SAVES PEOPLE.
A HERO FEEDS BIRDS EVEN IF THEY BITE.

He tapped the page. "Can I put saints too?"

"If your teacher said heroes, write heroes," Carina called without looking up.

Leo frowned at Marisol instead. "But saints are like extra heroes with candles."

"That's one way to define them," Marisol said.

"Okay, then if a saint lies to save somebody, do they lose sainthood?"

The radiator clicked once, loud as a knuckle cracking. In the living room, somebody upstairs dragged furniture and the scrape came through the ceiling in long strokes. Marisol waited for Carina to jump in; the only answer was the dry click of Carina's thumbnail on cracked glass as she typed with both thumbs, too fast for accuracy, then erased whatever she wrote and started again.

"Why are you asking?" Carina said.

"Because Father Tomas said saints tell truth no matter what, and then in social studies Ms. Chong said Harriet Tubman used fake names and tricks, and that's lying but good, so who's right?" Leo looked between them. "And if you lie because grown-ups are scared, does God count scared as a reason?"

Carina stacked the forms, squared the edges against the table, and only then answered. "Finish your sentence first. Then theology."

"That's not theology, that's math with people," Leo muttered.

Marisol swallowed, tasted old coffee and toothpaste and shame all at once. "Write that heroes do hard things when the choices are bad," she said. "Write that sometimes telling the whole truth right away can hurt somebody, and sometimes hiding it hurts them later."

Leo's pencil stopped. "That's not an answer."

"It's the only honest one I've got."

He considered that, then wrote slowly, sounding out each word under his breath. Carina did not look at Marisol, but she stopped tapping her phone.

At eleven forty-five, Carina checked the microwave clock, planted both palms on the table, and announced the mission list in one breath.

"I'm dropping the laundry at the laundromat. We're going to Dollar Plus for poster board. Then we're hitting the pharmacy for Mami's knee refill. After that we're getting food, because I'm not cooking a damn thing tonight."

"I can pay for takeout," Marisol said.

"You can pay for your MetroCard refill and keep your lights on. I've got a coupon." Carina handed her one bag. "You can carry this and not wander off into detective mode."

"I wasn't planning to investigate anybody's frozen peas."

"Good."

The bus stop was three blocks away and the sidewalk had the kind of old snow that turned gray at the edges, packed hard where people had walked and polished slick where nobody had salted. Leo hopped over each dark patch as if lava lived under slush. He narrated pigeon sightings in real time. Marisol kept an anxious clock running anyway, hearing Carina's two-minutes-late joke replay in a harsher tone every time Leo jumped.

"Two on the awning, one limping but still bossy, one with oil rainbow neck."

"That's not oil," Carina said. "That's feather color."

"It looks like gas station puddle."

"Fair point," Marisol said.

The bus app said four minutes, then nine, then fifteen, then Delayed with no number. A woman with a stroller cursed quietly into her scarf. Two teenagers bounced a basketball against the bus shelter ad until the glass shuddered. Carina checked the app every thirty seconds and started reorganizing the contents of her bag purely to have something to control. An apology climbed into Marisol's throat and sat there metallic and hot, even though she had done nothing except wait.

"We can walk to the laundromat and catch the next one from there," Marisol said.

"With three bags and him? No. He'll run into traffic because he saw a feather."

"I don't run into traffic," Leo said. "I assess."

Marisol barked a laugh she could not hold in. Leo's solemn delivery cracked Carina too, and a second laugh broke out of her, smaller this time but real. She leaned against the shelter post, shaking her head.

"You hear yourself?" Carina said to Leo. "You're eight years old and talking like an insurance adjuster."

"Ms. Chong says vocab gets you scholarships."

"Ms. Chong's right."

The bus finally arrived so full that people at the back door shouted before it stopped. They squeezed in anyway, Leo tucked between them with his backpack on his chest. Every time the driver braked, the whole bus leaned as one body and then snapped back. The heater blew hot plastic air that smelled like wet wool and coins. Marisol braced one hand on the pole and the other on Leo's backpack zipper, counting stops to keep her chest from sprinting ahead into excuses.

At Snyder the bus stalled in place for six full minutes behind a double-parked van with hazard lights blinking like a shrug. Leo fogged a circle in the window with his breath and drew a pigeon beak on it.

"If church is about offerings," he said to the glass, "why are the altar flowers plastic and dusty?"

Carina closed her eyes for a beat. "We're not doing this on public transit."

"He asked a design question," Marisol said.

"No, he asked a trap. He got that from you."

"He got that from being awake in this family."

Carina's jaw flexed. She did not answer.

They dropped two loads at the laundromat and walked to Dollar Plus for poster board, glue sticks, and the specific black marker Leo claimed was essential to rendering pigeons with moral complexity. Carina stopped at the end of each aisle long enough to run numbers under her breath before nodding yes or no. Marisol carried bags and read label prices out loud before anything went into the basket, because that was how Carina shopped when money was tight: no surprises at checkout.

At the pharmacy, Carina tapped her card twice before it went through and told the tech, too bright, "The machine hates me personally." Marisol studied the gum rack and pretended interest so the embarrassment could stay private. The register beep hit the same pitch as detox monitors; heat crawled under her collar. The receipt printed long and mean. Carina folded it once and slid it into her wallet behind Leo's school ID.

By three, all three of them were tired and salt-streaked and mildly hungry in the way that made everyone mean if nobody ate soon. Carina called Golden Dragon from the curb, one hand over her free ear to hear above traffic.

"Two lunch specials, one kids lo mein, extra rice, no shrimp, yes I know it's after lunch, I'm begging, Frankie." She listened, then rolled her eyes. "No, my sister's not dead. She's carrying groceries."

She hung up and gave Marisol the smallest sideways grin. "He asked if I was ordering enough food for a funeral again."

"That's customer service with memory."

"That's Flatbush."

Back at the apartment, Leo dumped poster board and feathers and crayons across the table and declared he was making a map called PIGEONS OF MORAL CHARACTER. Carina put rice on plates, lined up soy sauce packets like ammunition, and handed Marisol a fork without ceremony. The cheap plastic bent at one tine, and the ordinary gesture hit Marisol harder than any speech: in this apartment, belonging came disguised as utility.

Cheap lo mein steamed in white clamshells, slick with oil and scallion bits and too much sodium, perfect in that specific exhausted way. The sesame chicken was mostly batter and sugar glaze. Nobody pretended otherwise. They ate standing up for the first five minutes because there was not enough elbow room until Leo shifted his project to one side.

"Don't drip on the board," Carina warned.

"Art needs risk," Leo said.

"Art can risk on newspaper, not on school project."

Marisol chewed and watched them argue about glue density like it mattered to federal policy. The ordinary texture of it pressed against her chest so hard she almost mistook it for pain. It was not strategy or testimony or cross-examination. It was just a Saturday where somebody needed tape and somebody needed dinner and somebody needed an adult to sign page three.

Leo slurped a noodle, frowned at his worksheet, and asked with his mouth half full, "If somebody says they're coming Saturday and the bus messes it up, do you mark them absent or give one more chance?"

"Chew first," Carina said automatically.

He chewed with exaggerated jaw movements, swallowed, waited.

Marisol wiped sauce from her thumb and bought herself three seconds. "You give one more chance if they tell the truth about being late. People can miss. The bad part is making everybody guess."

"That's a terrible system," Leo said.

"It is," Carina and Marisol said together.

Their forks paused on the same beat and an almost-smile tugged at both mouths, but this one did not make it all the way out.

After dinner, Carina shoved leftovers into containers for Sunday lunch and started a load of darks in the hallway machine. Leo sat with his math corrections and kicked the table leg in a steady rhythm when he got stuck. Marisol crouched beside him and walked him through regrouping twice, then made him explain it back to her to prove he had it.

When he finished, he leaned his head against her shoulder for exactly four seconds, then popped up as if affection were a race he did not want to lose.

"Can we feed Saint Kevin tomorrow?" he asked Carina.

"If it's not raining and if you wear both gloves."

"The blue glove has bad luck."

"The blue glove keeps your fingers attached."

"Can Tia Mari come?"

Carina kept folding laundry, moving from towel to shirt to socks and then stack. "We'll see."

"That's not yes."

"Correct."

Leo made a face at the injustice of adulthood and went to wash his hands for bedtime. The bathroom fan clicked on with its usual rattle. Water banged through old pipes. Carina kept folding for another minute before she spoke.

"He asked for you every night the first week after you left," she said. She did not look up from the laundry. "Then every other night. Then he stopped asking out loud and started asking if birds remember people."

Marisol felt the words land in her stomach like swallowed metal. "I'm sorry."

"I know."

"I shouldn't have made him watch that fight."

"You and me both." Carina set the half-folded sheet on the chair, sat across from Marisol, and let the room go quiet before she spoke again. "Listen. I'm not doing a reunion montage. We had one okay day. That's all this is."

"I get it."

"Do you? Then hear the whole list." Carina held her gaze, hands flat on her knees. "You can come Saturdays if you text Friday night and confirm morning of. If you're late more than fifteen minutes, don't come because I build my whole day around timing. You don't talk to Leo about Salvador, court papers, overdose details, paternity rumors, none of it. You don't miss and then swoop back with gifts like that fixes anything. If you drink, even once, you tell me before you step in this apartment. If you lie to me, we're done."

Marisol let the list hit without defending herself. The old reflex to argue definitions twitched and died. "Okay."

"Say the whole thing."

"Okay, Saturdays with confirmation. On time. No case talk with Leo. No disappearing-and-gifts routine. If I drink, I tell you first. If I lie, we're done."

Carina nodded once. "Good."

"Can I ask one thing?"

"Depends what thing."

"If I can't make a Saturday because of work, can I do Sunday morning park instead?"

Carina considered. "Maybe. You ask, I decide based on that week's chaos."

"Fair."

"I didn't say fair. I said maybe."

"That's your love language."

"My love language is paid bills and people where they said they'd be."

The bathroom door opened. Leo came out with wet hair combed flat and one pajama sleeve inside out. "Can I have one clementine?"

"After teeth," Carina said.

He groaned and trudged back toward the sink. Marisol started clearing the table to give her hands something to do. She stacked the catechism worksheet under the math packet, capped markers, and snapped glue sticks shut.

"You said intake got paid that week," she said. "Do you have the paperwork from Harbor House?"

Carina wrestled Leo's inside-out sleeve right side out without gentleness. "Of course that's your first question."

"I need to see what happened, Cari. I keep flattening Mami into one story, and it isn't holding."

Carina held her eyes for a beat, then jerked her chin toward the stove. "Check the top drawer. There's an envelope with the brittle rubber band."

Marisol opened the drawer and got the usual archaeology: dead batteries, soy sauce packets, keys to locks long gone, a broken rosary bead, coupons expired last spring, a church bulletin from Advent, three takeout menus, and the envelope Carina meant.

She lifted it out. Carina crossed the kitchen, took the envelope from her, peeled off the rubber band, and pulled a yellow slip from inside.
"You want records?" Carina said. "Start with this."

Marisol looked down and read.

PAWNBROKER RECEIPT
BAY RIDGE COLLATERAL
DATE: 03/18/2019
ITEM: WOMEN'S 14K WEDDING BAND, WHITE/YELLOW GOLD, ENGRAVED T.V./G.V.
ADVANCE: $480.00
CUSTOMER: GLORIA VEGA

The room narrowed to the bright rectangle of paper in her hand. The letters T.V./G.V. looked impossible and absolutely familiar, like somebody had carved a grave into a wedding and then pawned it for bus fare.

Carina saw her face and went still. "Keep your voice down," she said, low.

"What is this?"

"What does it look like?"

"It looks like Mami sold her ring."

"Pawned."

"For four hundred eighty dollars?"

Carina took a breath through her nose and glanced toward the bathroom where Leo hummed through brushing. "Not in front of him."

"Cari."

"Let me finish."

Marisol nodded once, ticket shaking in her fingers. Carina reached for the paper, then stopped, then reached again and took it carefully by two corners as if it could tear history if she gripped too hard.

"You were on intake list for Harbor House that week," Carina said. "They wanted a deposit before they held your bed. Insurance was fighting about in-network bullshit. You were shaking so hard you couldn't sign your own name. Mami had ninety-three dollars in checking and thirty in cash from Mrs. Ortiz's overnight shift. She took the ring to Bay Ridge the same afternoon."

Marisol stared at her. "Why didn't anybody tell me?"

"Because you were trying not to die." Carina's voice sharpened, then dropped. "And because Mami weaponizes sacrifice like it's a second language. Both are true."

From the bathroom, Leo yelled, "I brushed for a whole song!"

"Good job!" Carina shouted back, eyes still on Marisol. Softer, she said, "She made sure the intake got paid. Then she reminded us for years that she did it, except she always said she sold her chain, not the ring. I found the ticket when I was looking for tax forms last year."

"Did she get it back?"

Carina laughed once, without humor. "With what money?"

Marisol pictured Gloria's hand at the sink, bare where the ring should have been, and realized she had always explained that absence as practical discomfort, soap under metal, swollen knuckles from arthritis. She had not asked. Or she had asked once and accepted the first answer because there were always louder emergencies to tend.

"She lied about what she pawned," Marisol said.

"Yes."

"And she paid for rehab anyway."

"Yes."

"And then she used that debt as a leash."

"Also yes." Carina rubbed her thumb over the fold line in the ticket until the paper bowed. "Welcome to our mother."

Leo ran out in pajamas, mouth smelling like bubblegum toothpaste, and launched himself at Carina's hip. "Can I have two clementines because I did extra brushing?"

Carina folded the ticket in half, then half again, and palmed it before he could see. "One."

"One and a half."

"One."

"Fine."

He took his clementine to the rug and peeled it with intense concentration, building a spiral of rind like he was laying train track. Marisol watched him pull each segment apart and offer one to the pigeon drawing on his poster board before eating it himself.

"Saint Kevin doesn't eat citrus," he told the page. "He's stubborn."

Carina tucked the ticket into the yellow folder with Leo's school forms and closed it. "You're not taking this with you," she said.

"I wasn't asking to." Marisol paused. "Can I take a picture?"

Carina weighed it. "No posting. No poem lines. No coded captions. No using my kid's homework table as your evidence board."

"I'm not doing that."

"Swear."

"I swear."

Carina handed her the ticket for exactly long enough to photograph it. Marisol framed the shot with shaky fingers, made sure the date and amount were clear, then deleted the accidental blurry first image with the edge of her thumb.

"Done."

Carina took it back and slid it deep into the folder, beneath the parent email printouts and Leo's reading log. "You can think whatever you want about Mami after this," she said. "I just needed you to stop talking like she only ever controlled. Control, yes. Also this."

Marisol nodded because words were not stable yet. In her head, Gloria moved through that day in 2019 in brutal sequence: subway to Bay Ridge with her knees throbbing on stairs, waiting under fluorescent lights while a stranger weighed her wedding band, signing a form in careful English because official paper always made her grammar stiff, then getting back on the train to Harbor House with cash in an envelope and not enough money left for dinner. Marisol had been in withdrawal by then, sweating through two shirts and begging for ice chips like prayer.

She had remembered the ice chips. She had not remembered who paid for them.

Leo yawned so wide his whole face changed shape. Carina checked the microwave clock and exhaled. "Bed in ten."

"Can Tia Mari tuck me?"

Carina tapped the folder twice with two fingers, weighed the room, and made the call. "One story. Short one. No scary saints."

"I know one about a pigeon," Marisol said.

"Of course you do," Carina muttered.

In Leo's room, glow-in-the-dark stars peeled from the ceiling corners and one roller blind rattled whenever wind hit the window. His bed was pushed against a wall painted years ago with clouds that had faded to a thin blue-gray. He shoved three stuffed animals aside to make space for Marisol to sit.

"Story," he ordered.

"Once there was a pigeon who thought he was in charge of traffic lights," she said. "Every morning he sat on the red light and screamed at cars like they worked for him."

"What was his name?"

"Deputy Kevin."

Leo approved this with a solemn nod.

She made the story short like promised. Deputy Kevin learned nothing, stole half a pretzel, and still got fed by the old lady on the corner because love was not a merit system. Leo fell asleep before the ending anyway, fingers curled around the ear of a stuffed shark with one button eye.

When Marisol came back to the kitchen, Carina had packed leftovers into a container and set it by the door with a plastic fork taped to the lid.

"Take it," she said. "You'll get home hungry."

"I said I'd pay for takeout, at least let me--"

"Don't start. Just take the food." Carina glanced toward Leo's room, lowered her voice. "Text me when you get in. If you don't text, I call."

"Okay."

"And Mari?"

"Yeah?"

Carina rested both palms on the table, weight forward, all business again because business was safer than tenderness. "You don't get to use today to prove a thesis. Not about me, not about Mami, not about yourself. We had a day. Keep having days. That's the work."

Marisol put on her coat slowly, like rushing would crack something fragile. "I can do days."

"We'll see," Carina said, but her voice had softened at the edges.

At the bus stop, night wind came off the avenue carrying fryer grease and snowmelt and somebody's cologne too sweet for winter. The delay board glitched between Arrival and No Data. She stood with the leftovers warm against her palm and looked at the photo of the pawn ticket again, zooming in on the engraved initials until the pixels broke.

The engraved initials, T.V./G.V., filled the screen.

It was a wedding band, pawned for her bed in rehab.

It did not erase the lies. It did not absolve the years of staged sainthood, the rehearsed answers, the way Gloria could turn guilt into harness straps and pull. It also sat there, stubborn as fact, a piece of gold converted into four hundred eighty dollars and one locked unit bed where Marisol had shaken and sweated and not died.

When the bus finally came, she took a window seat over the wheel well and let the vibration climb through her knees. Outside, pigeons packed the lip of a closed bodega awning, feathers puffed against cold, still awake under streetlight glare. Leo would have named each one and assigned moral character by posture.

Her phone buzzed with a text from Carina before the bus cleared the next light.

He asked if pigeons dream. I told him ask you next Saturday.

Marisol typed back with cold thumbs.

Tell him yes. They dream about fries and world domination.

Three dots appeared, vanished, appeared again.

10 a.m. Don't be late.

Marisol put the phone in her pocket, pressed her thumb against the edge of the blue chip until the ridges marked her skin, and watched Flatbush slide by in wet neon bands. The city kept moving without verdict or clean absolution, just routes, receipts, and another Saturday she had to make.


# Chapter 15

Three mornings later, Marisol got off the B41 with the cuffs of her jeans wet and black from slush, and the paper folder under her coat already curling at the corners from her body heat. She had printed the release packet at the copy shop in three sets, ten-point font, legal margins, every clause she had fought over with a borrowed template and two nights of insomnia. The top page still smelled faintly of toner and the hand lotion she kept behind the register to stop her fingers from cracking. On the back of the folder she had written, in thick blue marker, the only instruction that mattered: GET THE SIGNATURE BEFORE MORPHINE.

She stood in front of Methodist's palliative entrance and pressed two fingers to the black glass. Cold needled through her skin. Her reflection floated there: a runner-thin face, hair braided badly because she had done it in the dark at six, and a burn scar near her left wrist half-hidden by the cuff of her sweater. She took out her phone. A text from her landlord sat on top of everything else, timestamped 7:12 a.m.: `Need at least $600 today or I post notice.` Under that, a group thread from Carina with Leo's latest pigeon drawing and no direct message to her. Under that, nothing from Ada.

Inside, the air changed from wet December metal to over-clean citrus and old fear. At the desk, a volunteer handed her a flimsy sticker with VISITOR in red block letters and told her to keep it visible. The volunteer had gray braids wrapped in a Yankees scarf and a voice like she sang in church, and she called everyone baby in exactly the same tone, even the man cursing at the vending machine down the hall. Marisol peeled the backing and pressed the sticker onto her coat pocket, careful not to crumple the folder.

Room 614 had Salvador's name on a printed strip half peeled from the plastic holder. The last two letters hung loose and breathed every time the heat kicked on. Through the glass panel she could see him turned toward the window, bed raised, the oxygen cannula slack against his upper lip like a misplaced thread. His skin had the papery yellow she still could not get used to, as if somebody had glazed him and forgotten to fire him. The right hand with the tremor rested on top of the blanket, fingers curved around nothing.

When she opened the door, a nurse in navy scrubs was adjusting the pump. Her badge said MINA ORTIZ in peeling white letters, and she had tiny planets painted on her thumbnail and ring finger, not careful little stars but full planets with crooked rings and visible brushstrokes. Mina looked up, recognized Marisol from the previous visits, and gave her a quick nod.

"You got him at a good window," Mina said quietly. "He's been awake since five because he says the birds in this city start fighting before sunrise." She tapped the folder's corner with one planet-painted nail. "You still doing papers."

"Yeah," Marisol said. "I need ten minutes where he's clear. Maybe fifteen if he gets stubborn."

"He gets stubborn every day ending in y." Mina checked the infusion line, then tucked a blanket edge under Salvador's elbow with a practiced sweep. "I can hold the eight-thirty dose for a little. Not long. He hurts when I do."

"I know." Marisol swallowed. "Thank you."

Mina gave a shrug that was almost embarrassed. "My brother kept saying tomorrow right up till there wasn't one. I hate tomorrow." She touched Salvador's chart once, then left, letting the door click soft behind her.

Marisol pulled a chair close enough to smell him. It was not decay, not exactly. Hospitals did their best to erase rot with detergent and bleach and dry air, but there was still a sweet-sour edge under it that made her think of pears left too long in a bowl. Salvador opened his eyes without turning his head.

"You walk like your mother when you're angry," he said. His voice was dry paper. "Heel first. Like you want the floor to confess."

"Good morning to you too."

"It isn't morning in here. They took that from me days ago." He blinked, chin tipping toward the folder. "That's the execution order."

"Release authorization. Interview audio. Notebook quotations. Derivative rights for publication and performance." She sat, set the folder on her knees, and tried to keep her hands still. "You asked me to finish the catalogue essay. This is how I can finish it without getting buried in court before page one."

He made a small sound that might have been a laugh. "Court was always waiting at the bottom of the stairs. I just had better shoes."

"Salvador."

He turned his head then, slow, eyes watery but focused. In this light he looked both enormous and already gone. "Read me the ugly parts. Not the polite parts."

She opened to the marked pages. "Clause six. Estate retains ownership of originals, but grants me nonexclusive license to quote recordings and notebooks in the manuscript currently titled *Ash Under Gold Leaf* and related readings. Clause seven. I indemnify the estate against claims arising from false statements that are not supported by documentary evidence."

"There it is," he said. "False statements. Their favorite paint thinner."

"I didn't write that one. They did."

"Then read clause eight."

She turned a page. "Clause eight says no excerpt can be used in a context that implies criminal conduct by identifiable living persons unless independently corroborated by public records or technical documentation."

"Identifiable."

"Yeah."

"Meaning your mother. Your sister. Nora. Half the board." He shut his eyes for a second, then opened them again. "They call it caution so they can call truth defamation."

"Then sign anyway." She heard her voice harden and did not pull it back. "Sign, and I can fight from inside the language they gave me. If you don't sign, I get nothing from you that I can print."

"You have your death certificate." His lip twitched. "Not mine. Tomas's."

"That's not enough for what this is."

"It is enough if you stop asking permission."

"I got suspended for stopping permission. My stipend got frozen. My badge doesn't open a bathroom now. I'm behind on rent and I'm one missed shift from losing the room I already can't afford. I don't need advice about heroic disobedience from a man who died famous. I need your signature."

The words landed in the room like glass. She heard the edge in them as soon as they left her mouth and hated it, but she did not take them back. Salvador stared at her, then closed his eyes again and let out a breath that rattled.

"Good," he said. "You finally sound like the person writing about me." He licked his lips. "Pen."

She grabbed one from her tote, a cheap black roller with COPY LAND stamped in blue on the barrel. His hand shook when he reached for it, so she held the folder against a hardcover book to make a flat surface and steadied the paper at the margin with two fingers. He signed the first line with patient violence, each letter dragged like he was carving into wet plaster. S-a-l-v-a-d-o-r came out legible enough. On the second page he made it through Rivas and stopped, breathing through his mouth.

"You can rest for a minute," she said.

"No." He tried to lift the pen again. It tapped the page twice, leaving two black dots that looked like fly shit. "Where's the witness line?"

"Last page."

"Always the last page."

His right shoulder jerked once. The pen fell from his fingers and rolled onto the blanket. For half a second Marisol thought he was joking, staging some little theater of fragility to control tempo again. Then his chest pulled in too hard and stayed there. His jaw worked, and a thin string of spit slid to the corner of his mouth. The monitor began to alarm in quick high beeps.

"Salvador?" She stood so fast the chair legs screamed against linoleum. "Hey. Hey, look at me."

His eyes were open but unfocused, fixed above her shoulder. His hand clawed at the blanket, knuckles white under jaundice. She hit the call button with the side of her fist and shouted for help. Mina was in the room before the second alarm cycle, then another nurse, then a resident with an exhausted ponytail and a stethoscope that kept slipping off her neck. Someone lowered the bed. Someone turned him. Someone said DNR status, comfort measures, call respiratory anyway.

Marisol got pushed toward the wall without anyone touching her. She watched the room turn into choreography she could not interrupt. Mina leaned close to Salvador's ear and said his name three times like she was trying to pull him back by sound alone. The resident drew up medication with a hand so steady it made Marisol furious. Salvador's breath came in rough wet pulls, each one farther apart than the one before. His face, already yellow, went waxen at the mouth. Marisol thought of pigment names because she could not think of death directly: Naples yellow, old ivory, a thin wash of gray over everything.

He stopped moving his hand first. Then the jaw unclenched. Then the next breath did not come.

The alarm changed tone, lower and continuous. Mina checked the monitor, got the resident's nod, then called the time from the clock above the sink. Her voice was formal when she said it. Formal and kind at once, which was a thing Marisol had not known was possible until then.

No one looked at Marisol for several seconds. The resident wrote something in the chart. The second nurse pulled the sheet up to Salvador's chest and smoothed it twice even though it was already smooth. Mina turned off the alarm and adjusted the cannula out of habit, then stopped with it in her hand and let it fall.

"I'm sorry," Mina said.

Marisol did not answer. She stepped to the bedside and put her fingers on Salvador's wrist, as if a pulse might still be hiding somewhere she had not checked. His skin was already cooling at the knuckles while his forearm stayed warm, heat leaving in layers she could not negotiate with. She bent until her forehead touched the blanket over his hip, wool scraping her skin, and breathed in detergent, sweat, morphine, and the sour-metal smell of the room.

When she straightened, her vision narrowed to a bright tunnel. She grabbed the sink rail, hard enough that her nails bent backward, and gagged once without vomiting. A wall sanitizer dispenser sat inches from her face. She hit it by reflex, and the ethanol stink bloomed sharp and sweet. Her mouth flooded with the memory of freezer-burned vodka, plastic cap digging her palm, throat already opening. For one humiliating second she almost brought her slick hand to her lips.

She wiped her palm on her jeans instead, fast, like she was erasing evidence. Paper whispered under the vent, and she turned to the folder still open on the bed tray. There were two complete signatures and one partial signature, while the witness line was empty, the notarization block was empty, and the final page was untouched.

"He was signing," she said. Her voice came out thin. "He was in the middle of it."

Mina followed her gaze. She pressed her lips together. "I can't witness something that wasn't finished before death. If legal needs chart timing, they'll get chart timing."

"Can you write that he intended to sign?"

"I can write what I observed." Mina met her eyes and did not flinch. "What I observed is that he started and then he died."

Marisol nodded once, because if she did not nod she would scream. Mina put a hand on her shoulder for a brief moment, squeezed through wool and bone, then left her with the body.

Marisol stood there until the room cooled around her. She picked up the pen from the blanket and wiped it clean with a tissue, not because it mattered but because she could not bear leaving his spit on the barrel. Then she gathered the papers, stacked them square, and slid them back into the folder with hands that were now shaking hard enough to rattle the clip.

Her phone buzzed as she stepped into the corridor. Unknown number. She answered.

"Ms. Vega, this is Malcolm Price for the Rivas estate." The voice was male, clipped, expensive, the kind that had practiced not needing volume. "I understand Mr. Rivas passed at approximately 8:47. I'm on-site. Could we speak in consult room B in ten minutes?"

She almost hung up. Instead she said, "You were waiting?"

"I was nearby anticipating execution of documents. Given events, we need immediate clarity."

"Yeah," she said. "Clarity."

Consult room B was too warm and smelled of stale coffee from a machine down the hall. Malcolm Price stood when she entered. He was in his early fifties, trim suit, dark tie with a tiny repeating pattern of rowing oars, and he had a permanent pale groove where a wedding ring used to be. He touched that groove while he introduced himself, thumb circling skin that was already smooth. On the table in front of him sat a leather portfolio and a paper cup of tea he had not touched.

Nora sat at the far end with her glasses off, pinching the bridge of her nose where eye strain gave her headaches. She had kicked off her heeled boots and switched into foldable black flats, the same emergency pair Marisol had seen peeking out of her tote at board meetings. She looked older than forty-five that morning, not softer, just worn thin.

"I'm not here for condolences," Marisol said, staying standing. "Let's skip that part."

Malcolm gave a small nod. "Understood."

Nora said, "Sit, Marisol. You're shaking." Her voice had the institutional polish stripped out of it. "Please."

Marisol sat because her knees had started to feel unreliable.

Malcolm opened his portfolio and slid a copy of the release form between them. A yellow tab marked the signature page. "I reviewed what nursing scanned to administration. Two pages are signed. The execution page is not. Witness and notary blocks are blank. Under New York estates law and this contract's integration clause, the instrument does not execute."

"He signed," Marisol said. "You can see his name."

"Partial signature does not vest rights."

"He died with the pen in his hand."

"I'm not disputing sequence. I'm stating legal effect."

Instrument. Execute. The words scraped the back of her teeth; he'd already translated Salvador's last breath into filing language.

Nora leaned forward. "Malcolm, give me a narrow lane. One excerpt window pending probate."

He kept his eyes on the table. "No interim lane on this fact pattern."

"Fact pattern?" Marisol repeated. "He was choking and still trying to write."

"I mean unverified allegations about living individuals and institutions." Malcolm folded his hands. "Litigation cost is coercive even when facts are arguable. The estate will not expose itself, the museum, or third parties to foreseeable claim."

Her palms slicked against the vinyl chair. Under his voice she could still hear the flatline tone, low and continuous.

"Third parties?" Marisol laughed once, raw and joyless. "My mother's name is in the underlayer. The death certificate has his witness signature. I'm not pitching rumors off Reddit."

"Mr. Rivas told many versions. Dying declarations are not magic."

"This isn't a murder trial. It's a book, and it's my life."

"Exactly." Malcolm tapped the table lightly with two fingers, as if keeping time to music only he could hear. "Books multiply publication risk by jurisdiction. You publish paternity without DNA, allege coordinated deceit for financial benefit, or imply institutional fraud, we seek injunction. If injunction fails, we pursue damages."

Nora's eyes stayed on the table. "Marisol, you still have a public-record spine. Municipal documents, direct observation, independently sourced facts."

Heat pooled under Marisol's coat, sweat threading down her spine. If she lost her temper here, she'd lose the room and the record with it.

"Without the interview tape?"

"Without estate-owned recordings or notebook excerpts," Malcolm said. "Yes."

"And the notebooks?"

"Restricted assets pending inventory and probate." He took a breath, then finally let a trace of weariness into his face. "Ms. Vega, nobody is forbidding writing. We are forbidding use of material you do not have rights to use."

"You're forbidding me from writing the part that matters."

"I'm preventing a defamation case that will consume you and everyone attached to you."

"You don't give a shit about me."

"I give a great deal of attention to foreseeable harm." He slid a one-page notice across the table. "This preserves all rights and requests immediate confirmation that no excerpts from recordings or notebooks will be disseminated."

Marisol did not touch the paper.

Nora said softly, "Take counsel before you respond."

"With what money?" Marisol snapped, then looked away. The room hummed with forced air and fluorescent buzz. She could feel sweat between her shoulder blades despite the cold outside. "My stipend is frozen because your board panicked about a poem. My rent arrears are two-four. My card is at six-eight. Don't tell me to hire counsel like you're recommending a brunch place."

Nora flinched but did not argue.

Malcolm gathered his portfolio with careful hands. "I'm sorry for the timing."

"No, you're not," Marisol said. "You're efficient. That's different."

He stood. "Efficiency is how I keep families from being eaten alive in public." He let the silence sit for a second longer, ring groove flashing pale as he reached for his coat. "I know that sounds obscene right now."

"It does," Marisol said.

After they left, Marisol stayed in the consult room with the stale coffee smell and the legal notice she still had not touched. She took her blue day-one chip out of her pocket, rolled it between her fingers, and watched her hand shake anyway. She wanted a drink with a level of specificity that frightened her: not whiskey, not wine, just cheap vodka from the corner store on Flatbush where the guy with the lazy eye did not ask for ID if you were buying only one bottle and looked tired enough. Her mouth flooded. The first swallow rose in memory so hard she could taste plastic cap and freezer burn.

She put the chip between her teeth until the metal edge hurt her gums. Then she took out her phone and opened Luis's number. She did not call. She typed: `He died before signing. Estate blocked tape. I want to drink.` She hit send before she could edit it into something noble.

By the time she walked out of the hospital, sleet had started. Not snow yet, just hard wet pellets that hit her cheeks like thrown sand. She zipped her coat to the chin and headed toward the bus stop, folder under her arm, legal notice folded into quarters in her back pocket like an unpaid bill.

Halfway to Flatbush, her phone buzzed with a message from an unknown ProtonMail address.

`Need to talk. Not phone. 5:30, branch library by Grand Army, basement stacks. - A`

She read it twice, then a third time, and felt her pulse change.

***

The basement stacks at the library smelled like dust, damp carpet, and radiator heat that never reached the floor. A security guard watched videos on his phone behind the desk and barely looked up when she came in. At a back table near the city-planning section, Ada sat with a canvas tote at her feet and a paper cup of barley tea gone cold. She had tied her hair up with a yellow pencil, and the skin on her hands was cracked at the knuckles despite thick ointment, pale lines across brown skin like unfinished seams. A roll of white finger tape stuck out of her coat pocket.

When Marisol sat, Ada did not hug her. She did not offer condolences. She slid a packet of gum across the table.

"You smell like hospital," Ada said. "Chew one."

Marisol took a piece, unwrapped it with fingers that still would not stop shaking, and put it in her mouth. Peppermint hit hard enough to make her eyes water.

"He died at eight forty-seven," she said.

Ada nodded once. "I heard at noon. Conservation got a lockdown email at twelve nineteen. Everything tied to Rivas moved to legal hold. Server permissions changed. Physical storage locked."

"They blocked the tape." Marisol leaned forward, voice low. "Estate counsel said truth is defamation risk if it has names in it."

The sanitizer reek from upstairs still sat in her throat, sweet and chemical.

"That's not wrong, legally." Ada's tone was flat, technical. "It's also not neutral."

Marisol pressed her tongue against her back molars until the ache steadied her. "Why this cloak-and-dagger email?"

Ada let out a breath. "Because my work account is monitored right now and my phone is on museum device management. Because if I say one dumb thing on a recorded line, they can call it intent and terminate for cause. Because I still need to pay rent next month." She rubbed the cracked knuckle of her right hand with her thumb, then stopped herself. "And because legal forced a decision at two-oh-seven."

Marisol went still.

"What decision?"

Ada pulled a printed email from her tote and flattened it between them before she touched the manila folder. The subject line read `LEGAL HOLD ACTION REQUIRED - RIVAS MATERIALS`. Midway down, one sentence was bolded: `Re-index all Rivas conservation files as undated study references and remove donor-family cross-links by 16:00.`

"General counsel sent that," Ada said. "Then they put us on a call and watched my cursor while they told me to click acknowledgment."

Marisol scanned the page. "Did you click it?"

"No." Ada held her gaze. "I exported the live index with timestamps, pulled checksums on every flagged file, wrote refusal in the audit log, and walked out. Then I built this." She set the printout aside and pulled a thin manila folder from her tote, keeping one hand on top of it. "Your signature route died upstairs. These technical files are museum custody, not estate custody. Different leverage."

"You told me in October you couldn't share raws."

"I couldn't then. I can now if I eat the consequences." Ada met her eyes. "I'm not giving you cute screenshots and pretending we slipped. I'm giving you a chain-of-custody packet that can stand up in a fight."

Marisol's heartbeat thudded against the table edge. "What packet?"

"Multispectral composites of *Witness at the River*. Full-resolution IR reflectography TIFFs from the October session. Cross-section notes from varnish removal." Ada tapped the folder. "And two pieces legal buried before I could show you. Underlayer inscription revealed by channel isolation: `G.V., Dec 1991, cinco meses.` Plus a 1992 intake note where Rivas asked for overpaint on the male figure and removal of a dedication line with your mother's first name."

Marisol stared at her.

"That doesn't prove paternity," she said.

"No. It corroborates timeline and relationship proximity before your birth. It kills deniability that they were strangers." Ada leaned back, jaw tight. "It's evidence, not closure."

"If you hand me that, they torch your badge."

"Probably." Ada's mouth twisted. "Or they suspend first so they can sound merciful in HR language."

"Why do it?"

Ada rubbed the tape seam on her knuckle before she answered. "Because I spend my days lifting nicotine varnish off other people's lies. I can tell myself I'm neutral while I do it. I can say process over story. That email made it plain what process meant this time." She gave a tiny humorless laugh. "I like my job. I like my mortgage autopay. I also like sleeping without feeling like I swallowed bleach."

Marisol swallowed hard. "I don't want to be the reason you lose your life."

"You're not that important," Ada said, and for a second there was the dry private wit Marisol had missed. Then Ada's face went serious again. "Listen to me. If I do this, you don't turn me into a saint in your pages. You don't write me as collateral damage that proves your purity. You use the files exactly, with context, and you carry the blowback with your name on it."

"I can do that."

"Can you?" Ada's voice dropped lower. "You were very good at making yourself the tragic center when things got bad. You were also drunk in that bus shelter after your mother's confession, and you still haven't told half the people you owe. I'm helping evidence, not your myth."

The words hit clean and left no room to hide. Marisol nodded slowly.

"You're right," she said. "I can still slip into performance. I know that." She placed her palm flat on the table, as if taking an oath in a cheap courtroom. "I won't make this about my redemption."

Ada studied her for a long beat, then pushed the folder halfway across.

"Good. Because this next part is deliberate."

Inside were printouts of file hashes, accession numbers, and a draft email already composed on museum letterhead. Subject line: `Evidence Preservation Copy - Witness at the River Conservation Records`. Recipients: Marisol's personal email, Ada's personal email, and a legal-discovery address that included estate counsel and museum general counsel. CC: Nora Feldman.

Marisol felt her stomach drop anyway. "You're copying them?"

"Yes." Ada's voice was precise now, almost classroom. "If I send this in secret, they can call it tampering. If I send it in the open with checksums and timestamps, they can still discipline me but they can't argue fabrication. This is traceable by design."

"They'll know it was you in three seconds."

"Correct." Ada zipped her tote closed. "We're not doing a thriller. We're doing a record."

Marisol barked a short laugh that broke in the middle and turned into something close to a sob. She covered her mouth with her fist and breathed until it passed.

"When?" she asked.

"Now. Public library Wi-Fi is trash but stable enough for a small file package. Full-res set uploads from my home connection tonight." Ada pulled a laptop from her bag, opened it, and typed in a long password without looking at the keys. Her fingers moved quick despite the taped knuckle.

She attached three compressed files first: IR composites, intake memo scans, chain-of-custody report. Then she pasted the hashes into the body and added one sentence above them: `Sending this preservation copy in the interest of evidentiary integrity after legal hold notification.`

"That's too clean," Marisol said quietly.

"It needs to be clean." Ada kept one palm flat on the table. "Messy gets eaten."

She hit send.

The progress bar crawled. Twelve percent. Twenty-eight. Forty-nine. Marisol watched as if the number itself were a pulse on a monitor.

At one hundred, the screen flashed SENT 5:58 PM.

For two seconds nothing happened.

Then Ada's phone lit with a calendar alert she had not made. Another ping, this one email. She opened it. Her face did not change, but she exhaled through her nose in a way Marisol recognized as impact.

"What?"

Ada rotated the phone across the table so she could read.

`Dr. Park, effective immediately you are placed on paid administrative leave pending investigation into unauthorized dissemination of restricted conservation records. Building and systems access suspended at 18:00. Please arrange return of badge and museum-issued equipment by 10:00 tomorrow. - HR Compliance`

Marisol read the message twice and felt her skin go cold from the inside.

"I'm sorry," she said. "Fuck. Ada, I'm sorry."

Ada locked the screen and put the phone face down. "Don't apologize like a tourist. We both knew the toll before the bridge." She reached into her tote, took out her museum badge on a retractable clip, and set it on the table between them. "I hate that this little plastic rectangle can decide whether I touch paintings tomorrow." Her voice thinned for the first time. "I really hate that."

Marisol ran her thumb once along the badge photo. Ada wore a blue lab coat in it, with a neutral expression, sharp eyes at the corners, and hair shorter than now. The photo did not show the cracked knuckles, the finger tape, or the way she lined up objects exactly parallel when she was trying not to panic.

"What do you need tonight?" Marisol asked.

"I need to back up my personal drive before legal asks for devices. I need to email my mortgage officer because leave pay can get delayed in payroll audits. I need to call my mother and tell her no, I didn't get fired for sleeping with an artist, and yes, she can stop saying `I told you so` in three languages." Ada put the badge back in her bag with deliberate care. "And I need you to open those files in front of me so I know they arrived intact."

Marisol pulled out her phone. The email was there, time-stamped, headers visible, attachments heavy with numbers. She opened the first scan. A ghost image bloomed in grayscale: underpainting figure, male torso turned three-quarters, the letters T.V. near the shoulder where the visible surface now carried only river dark. She opened the second file. The inscription emerged as pale strokes under later pigment: G.V., Dec 1991, cinco meses.

She felt the room tilt slightly.

"Jesus," she whispered.

"No," Ada said. "Chemistry."

Marisol opened the intake note scan. There it was in a conservator's old handwriting from 1992, slanted and impatient: `Artist requests overpaint over male figure + remove dedication line incl. G.V.` Two names had been blacked out in old marker, but the redaction had thinned with copying and age, so the first letters still bled through like bruises.

"It's enough to corroborate," Ada said. "Not enough to absolve anybody."

"It's enough to keep them from saying I made it up."

"Then don't make anything else up either." Ada packed her laptop and stood. "You have the files. Now you have the burden."

The sentence sat between them like something heavy set down on purpose.

When they reached the library doors, sleet hit sideways across Flatbush, tiny hard beads that bounced off the concrete before they melted. Marisol wanted to kiss Ada, to hold her wrist, to give her some visible thanks that was not language. Ada read her face and shook her head once.

"Not tonight," Ada said. "I don't want tenderness used as a receipt."

"Okay."

"Go home. Duplicate everything. Three encrypted copies, two print sets. Don't text details to anyone. And call Luis before the liquor store calls you."

Marisol nodded. "Are you going to be okay?"

"I'm going to be employed by a process for a while." Ada gave a small, tired smile. "That's not the same as okay, but it's survivable."

She pulled her hood up, checked traffic out of habit like somebody who had grown up crossing too many dangerous intersections, and stepped into the weather. In thirty seconds she was another dark coat in a city full of them.

Marisol rode the train to Sunset Park with her phone in her fist and her jaw clenched hard enough to ache. In the car across from her, two teenagers argued over a cracked screen protector, and an older man slept upright with a takeout bag against his chest like a child. Ordinary city evening. Nobody knew she had a dead man's unsigned release in her tote and a live woman's career in her inbox.

In the sublet, the radiator hissed and failed to heat the room past fifty-eight. She put water on for instant coffee, opened her laptop, and began duplicating files exactly as Ada had instructed. Folder by folder, checksum by checksum. External drive, encrypted cloud vault, offline archive. She printed two hard sets and labeled each page with date and source so nobody could claim she had shuffled order. Her fingers cramped twice. She kept going.

At 9:12 p.m. Luis replied to her earlier text.

`Good. You told the truth fast. Call now.`

She called.

"You drink?" he asked before hello.

"No."

"You planning to?"

"I planned it all day. I didn't do it."

"Keep it plain. What happened."

She told him in sequence: death at 8:47, incomplete execution, estate block, Ada's transmission at 5:58, HR leave at 6:03, craving sharp enough to taste freezer-burned vodka in her throat. She did not edit for dignity. She did not leave out the parts where she had wanted to disappear.

Luis listened through dispatch static and paper shuffling. When she finished, he cleared his throat.

"You're in consequence country now," he said. "No shortcuts. Tonight you secure records and stay sober. Tomorrow you make the next right call without drama."

"What's the next right call?"

"First, don't post online. Second, get legal advice that isn't from panic. Third, treat Ada's risk like debt, not romance."

The line burned because it was true.

"Copy," Marisol said.

"Eat something salty and with protein before bed. Your body can't tell grief from depletion half the time."

She made eggs in a scratched pan and ate them standing at the sink. Then she called Luis back as instructed, read him her plan for the morning, and set two alarms.

At 11:03 p.m., Nora wrote from her museum account.

`Marisol - You have received materials now under active dispute. I cannot advise you formally. Off record: preserve original files, preserve metadata, do not discuss contents by text. I am sorry this landed on Ada. - N.F.`

Two minutes later came Malcolm Price, subject line in all caps: NOTICE OF PRESERVATION DEMAND. Keep all materials. Do not alter. Do not disseminate. Direct all correspondence through counsel.

The instruction was to preserve and not use, to hold and not speak, and to carry evidence like contraband and call that ethics.

Marisol opened the IR composite again. Beneath the finished surface, the hidden figure leaned toward someone outside the frame. The inscription glowed pale under black paint and varnish: G.V., Dec 1991, cinco meses.

Proof had a temperature. It did not warm anything.

She thought of Salvador's hand clawing the blanket while the pen rolled out of reach. She thought of Ada setting her badge on the library table, the clip still rewinding by reflex after she let go. Twelve hours between those gestures. Two institutions, same lesson about who got to authorize a story.

At midnight she clipped the unfinished release pages to the chain-of-custody report and wrote a sticky note in block letters: `HE DIED BEFORE THE WITNESS LINE. SHE SENT THE FILES ANYWAY.`

Then she opened a clean notebook page and drafted the first paragraph she would have to live inside:

`At 8:47 a.m. on a sleet morning in December, Salvador Rivas died before completing a release for publication use of his recorded testimony. At 5:58 p.m. the same day, Dr. Ada Park transmitted conservation records with full metadata through traceable channels, accepting immediate professional risk to preserve evidentiary integrity. I possess those records because she chose consequence over cover.`

She put the pen down and rubbed the bridge of her nose until color burst behind her eyes.

If she used the files badly, Ada's leave became waste.

If she used them well, Ada still paid.

Marisol set the blue day-one chip on top of the paper stack, turned off the lamp, and sat in the dark until her breathing slowed enough to sleep.
By dawn, the calls about Saint Brigid's would begin.


# Chapter 16

By the time Marisol reached Saint Brigid's parish hall, the cuffs of her jeans were wet with black slush and salt. December had gone from rain to sleet to a hard, mean cold in three days, and everyone tracked it indoors: crushed grit under boots, damp wool steaming over radiators, umbrellas opened like broken birds against the wall. A volunteer in a Knicks beanie stood by the door with a brass thurible hanging from one hand and a roll of blue painter's tape in the other, trying to keep the smoke from setting off the detector. Every time he swung the chain, incense rose in a pale ribbon and got beaten flat by the fluorescent hum.

By dawn, the calls about Saint Brigid's had started, and by evening she had stopped checking names before she silenced the phone.

The hall itself looked like every church basement she'd ever sat in for grief, baptism, and bingo. Folding chairs in narrow rows, some beige, some gray, some the old heavy metal kind with cracked vinyl seats. A table in back held two dented coffee urns, a stack of Styrofoam cups, sugar packets in a glass bowl, and powdered creamer clumped into pebbles from humidity. Someone had brought supermarket sheet cake with white frosting and blue roses that bled dye. The stale coffee smell had settled into the cinder block walls years ago, and incense only sharpened it.

At the front, the casket sat open on a platform draped in burgundy cloth. Salvador lay in the suit Nora had once called "appropriately neutral" in a donor meeting, charcoal wool with a narrow black tie, his tremoring right hand now folded still over his chest, fingers tucked by someone else's careful arrangement. They had powdered the yellow from his skin but not enough. Under the hall lights he looked waxed, the way fruit looked when a grocery sprayed it to hide bruises. On either side of him stood two enlarged photos on foam board: one from the seventies with his hair to his shoulders and paint on his cheek, one recent from the museum brochure where he looked frail and beatific in exactly the ratio marketing liked.

The line for condolences bent around the first row of chairs and then broke into clusters, because no one in that room had one grief. Old neighbors from Flatbush hugged former museum interns. A pair of critics in black turtlenecks stood by the prayer candles whispering into their scarves, both of them holding the same tiny notebook, same black cover, same yellow elastic band. Two women from Gloria's church wore navy coats and practical shoes and kept pressing foil trays of arroz con pollo onto people who said they were not hungry. Hunger did not matter. Showing up did.

Carina moved through it all with a roll of raffle tickets looped around her wrist, tearing stubs for food donations and sliding dollar bills into an old cookie tin with a chipped Santa on the lid. She had a purple folder tucked under her arm with an elementary-school district logo, and every few minutes she pulled out a permission slip, checked a box with a pink pen, and shoved it back without missing a conversation. That was how she handled panic: list, sort, staple, act.

When she spotted Marisol, she pressed two fingers against Marisol's sleeve like a brake and gave her the kind of smile people used when they were trying not to start.

"You're late," Carina said.

"The B train stalled at Atlantic for twenty minutes."

"Everyone's late. He was late to his own wake." Carina glanced toward the casket and then away. "Mami's in the kitchen counting trays. If you're gonna go in there, don't start anything."

"I didn't come to start anything."

Carina's mouth did a fast sideways twitch, not quite a laugh. "You came with that tote bag and your jaw locked like a pit bull. Don't lie to me in church, Mari. It bounces."

Before Marisol could answer, Gloria appeared in the kitchen doorway with a dish towel over her shoulder and hair pinned so tight it made her eyes look sharper than usual. She moved with the careful weight transfer of someone hiding knee pain from an audience. A smear of frosting marked the heel of her hand; she had been cutting cake with a plastic knife that snapped every third slice.

"Ven acá," Gloria said softly.

Marisol followed her into the kitchen. Steam fogged the one window over the sink. On the counter sat aluminum pans of pernil, a mountain of paper plates, and three canisters labeled with masking tape: SUGAR, FORKS, MONEY FOR FLOWERS. Gloria opened the SUGAR canister and pulled out a folded envelope thick with condolence cash.

"This is what people trust us with," she said. "You understand? They bring ten dollars, twenty dollars, and they bring stories. Today is not for corrections."

"Mami."

"Listen first. You always listen when it's too late." Gloria tapped the envelope against the counter. "Father Efrain will speak. Nora will speak. Carina has a short thing from Leo because he wanted to draw a pigeon and call it Abuelo Bird, and that's what children do at wakes. You're on the program for one poem. The one you sent last night. The soft one."

Marisol felt the folded pages in her tote push against her thigh like heat.

"I sent two drafts," she said.

"You sent one draft and one knife. You won't use the knife in this room." Gloria's voice stayed polite, almost tender, which made the command harder. "I'm asking you as your mother."

"You asked me that before records, before dinner, before everything."

Gloria closed the canister, palm flat on the lid. "And I was right before, even when you hated me for it. We survived because we knew when to shut our mouths."

Marisol held her gaze. "Who survived?"

For a beat Gloria's face opened, then shut. "Not here," she said. "Not in front of these people."

When they came back into the hall, Nora Feldman had arrived in a black coat that still held rain along the shoulders, her tote bag bulging with printed programs and a packet of museum condolence cards on thick cream stock. She had changed from heels into spare matte-black flats, and she kept blinking hard, thumb and forefinger pressing at the bridge of her nose in a rhythm Marisol remembered from suspension week.

Nora touched Marisol's elbow with practiced discretion. "Can we step aside for sixty seconds?"

They moved near the radiator, where the heat knocked damp wool and perfume into a single sweet-metal smell.

"First, thank you for coming," Nora said. "Second, I need to be explicit. Today is high exposure. We have press in the room and at least two livestreams already running. Estate counsel is tracking public statements."

"Is that condolences in museum language?"

Nora did not flinch. "It's risk language because risk is currently what I can control. You're free to read what you choose. I'm urging you not to use unverified allegations or material that implicates private individuals who are not consenting."

"You mean my family."

"I mean people who are alive and vulnerable to harm. Including you." Nora took a breath and shifted register, less polished. "Marisol, there is no legal container left. If this detonates here, it detonates everywhere."

"It already did."

Nora's eyes tracked the casket, the critics, the phone screens, then back to Marisol. "Then don't hand it gasoline."

Father Efrain called for everyone to take seats, and chairs scraped the floor in a ragged chorus. He was short, broad through the middle, with a smoker's rasp and nicotine-yellow fingertips despite his careful nails. A silver inhaler sat half-hidden in his sleeve cuff; every time he raised his hand in blessing, metal flashed. He began the rosary in Spanish and English, voice catching on the first mystery then steadying as the room answered.

Marisol sat in the second row beside Carina, who kept one hand on the purple school folder like it might sprout wings. The incense thickened.
